</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that photon antibunching can be observed using classical thermal light by utilizing detectors as photon-number-resolving tools. The effect arises from the interplay between thermal photon statistics and photon-number projection measurements. This finding helps clarify the connection between classical and nonclassical correlations in optical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It challenges the traditional association of antibunching with nonclassical light and provides insight into how measurement projection can induce nonclassical-like signatures in classical statistical distributions, which is relevant for quantum imaging and multiphoton interference.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the boundary between classical and nonclassical phenomena by exploring whether photon antibunching, typically a nonclassical effect, can be observed using classical thermal light.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that antibunching can be observed with thermal light in a modified HBT interferometer through photon-number projection measurements, specifically via the $g^{(2)}_{10}$ correlation function.

**Method.** The study combines a theoretical framework using a joint probability generating function with an experimental setup using a modified Hanbury Brown-Twiss interferometer and single-photon detectors acting as photon-number-resolving detectors.

**Model / system.** A modified Hanbury Brown-Twiss (HBT) interferometer using a thermal light source (generated via a laser and rotating ground glass) and a 1:1 non-polarizing beam splitter, with single-photon detectors used for photon-number projection.

**Key observables.** Normalized second-order correlation functions $g^{(2)}_{mn}$, specifically $g^{(2)}_{11}$ and $g^{(2)}_{10}$, and the degree of first-order coherence $\mu$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Average photon number $\bar{n}$, degree of coherence $\mu$, and the transition regime from antibunching to bunching as $\bar{n}$ increases.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The detectors are treated as photon-number-resolving detectors via photon-number projection measurements.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematic of the modified HBT interferometer; Figures 8(a) and 8(b) illustrate the transition of the $g^{(2)}_{10}$ correlation from antibunching to bunching as the average photon number increases.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of classical vs nonclassical boundaries, describes the experimental setup and theoretical model, presents results for various correlation functions and photon numbers, and concludes with an analysis of the nature of the observed effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding the boundary between classical and nonclassical phenomena is important for both fundamental researches in quantum optics and applications in quantum information. One of the most interesting research directions in this field is exploring nonclassical effects with classical light. In this paper, we will show that it is possible to observe antibunching with thermal light in a Hanbury Brown-Twiss interferometer by treating single-photon detectors as photon-number-resolving detectors to perform photon-number projection measurements. Both temporal and spatial antibunching is observed via the correlation of two detectors detecting one and zero photon, respectively. By comparing the measured results of thermal and laser light, it is found that the observed antibunching arises from the combined effect of photon statistics of thermal light and photon-number projection measurement.The classical and nonclassical nature of the observed antibunching is analyzed. The results are helpful to understand the connection between classical and nonclassical correlation and may find applications in multiphoton interference and quantum imaging.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28117"></a>
### [Strong Mpemba Effect Through a Reentrant Phase Transition](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28117v1)

**Authors:** Kristian Blom, Doron Benyamin, Uwe Thiele, Oren Raz, Aljaz Godec  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28117v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28117_figures/2604.28117_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Emergence of reentrant phase behavior with increasing coordination number. (a)–(c) Equilibrium values [Eq. (11)] of the staggered magnetization s, total magnetization m, and nearest-neighbor correlation q as functions of temperature T at fixed magnetic field H/z|J|=0.5 for z=3&lt;z∗≈5.14. Solid lines denote stable equilibria, dashed lines unstable ones. (d) Corresponding phase diagram in the (H, T) plane. The critical line (green) is monotonic and no reentrant phase transition is observed. (e)–(g) Equilibrium values of (s, m, q) for z=7&gt;z∗and H/z|J|=1.02. In contrast to z=3, the staggered magnetization is nonzero only within an intermediate temperature window. (h) Corresponding phase...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.28117_figures/2604.28117_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Mpemba and inverse Mpemba effects across the reentrant regime and their spectral origin. (a) Displace- ment from equilibrium D(t) [Eq. (13)] for a cooling quench from Ti1 = 3 (paramagnetic phase) and Ti2 = 2 (antiferromagnetic phase) to Tf = 0.12 (paramagnetic phase). Although the quench from Ti2 starts closer to equilibrium, the relaxation curves cross at finite time, showing the Mpemba effect. Insets show the corresponding relaxation trajectories in the (m, s) plane, zoomed in around the trajectories; blue and red dots denote the initial states, and the black dot the final equilibrium. (b) Same protocol but with Tf and Ti2 interchanged, corresponding to a heating quench. The...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the strong Mpemba effect in an antiferromagnetic Ising model is driven by reentrant phase transitions. It shows that the effect occurs because quenches into the paramagnetic phase avoid exciting the slowest staggered relaxation mode. The work establishes a direct connection between equilibrium phase behavior and anomalous non-equilibrium relaxation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides deep insights into how equilibrium phase topology (reentrance) can fundamentally alter non-equilibrium relaxation dynamics and the selection of slow modes in many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the origin of the strong direct and inverse Mpemba effects during temperature quenches and establishing a link between these anomalous relaxation phenomena and reentrant phase transitions.

**Main result.** The strong Mpemba effect arises when temperature quenches terminate in the paramagnetic phase due to the selective excitation of the slowest relaxation mode, a phenomenon that disappears in the absence of reentrance.

**Method.** The authors use the Bethe-Guggenheim (pair) approximation to derive macroscopic dynamical equations and analyze the spectral decomposition of the Jacobian matrix to study relaxation modes.

**Model / system.** An antiferromagnetic Ising model in a uniform magnetic field on a Bethe lattice with varying coordination number z, governed by single spin-flip Glauber dynamics.

**Key observables.** Sublattice magnetizations (ma, mb), total magnetization (m), staggered magnetization (s), nearest-neighbor correlation (q), and excess free energy (D(t)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), magnetic field (H), coordination number (z), and exchange interaction (J).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results are based on the Bethe-Guggenheim pair approximation and assume the thermodynamic limit (N to infinity).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows equilibrium order parameters and phase diagrams for different coordination numbers, demonstrating monotonic vs. reentrant phase boundaries. Figure 2 illustrates the decay of excess free energy and the crossing of relaxation curves during quenches.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of anomalous relaxation, defines the Ising model and BG approximation, derives the dynamical equations and free energy, analyzes the reentrant phase transition mechanism, and concludes with the spectral analysis of relaxation modes and the emergence of the Mpemba effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate temperature quenches across the reentrant phase transition of the antiferromagnetic Ising model in a magnetic field and show that the strong direct and inverse Mpemba effects arise when quenches terminate in the paramagnetic phase. These anomalous relaxation phenomena originate from the selective excitation of the slowest relaxation mode, which in the paramagnetic phase is purely staggered. Consequently, quenches starting from the paramagnetic phase have zero overlap with the slow mode and exhibit a strong (inverse) Mpemba effect. Quenches from the antiferromagnetic phase excite the staggered mode and display a slow-relaxation tail. By varying the lattice coordination number we show that the strong Mpemba effect disappears in the absence of reentrance. Our results provide the first demonstration of the strong (inverse) Mpemba effect in the antiferromagnetic Ising model based on the pair-approximation, and establish a link between anomalous relaxation and the equilibrium phase behavior.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27749"></a>
### [Timescales for Deep and Full Thermalization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27749v1)

**Authors:** Tabea Herrmann, Felix Fritzsch, Arnd Bäcker  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27749v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates whether the decay of higher-order correlations (full thermalization) and the approach of projected ensembles to Haar-random states (deep thermalization) occur at the same rate. The authors find that while both are exponential, full thermalization is faster at higher orders. The study also identifies a sub-exponential decay for the observable-independent distance between ensembles.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides new insights into the hierarchy of thermalization timescales in many-body systems, which is crucial for understanding how information scrambles and how local subsystems reach equilibrium in quantum chaotic dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Comparing the relaxation timescales of two extensions of the Eigenstate Thermalization Hypothesis (ETH): Full Thermalization (higher-order correlation functions) and Deep Thermalization (approach of projected ensembles to state designs).

**Main result.** Both processes exhibit exponential relaxation, but full thermalization at higher orders is faster than deep thermalization. Additionally, the decay of the observable-independent norm distance is found to be sub-exponential.

**Method.** Large-scale numerical simulations of a kicked Ising chain combined with theoretical frameworks including Free Probability Theory, Random Matrix Theory, and the replica picture.

**Model / system.** A 1D kicked mixed-field Ising spin chain subject to periodic Floquet dynamics with random longitudinal fields, serving as a paradigmatic model for chaotic many-body quantum dynamics.

**Key observables.** Higher-order Out-of-Time-Order Correlators (OTOCs) $F_k(t)$, moments of the projected ensemble $\Delta_k(t)$, and the Frobenius norm distance $\delta_k(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Order of the correlation function/moment ($k$), subsystem size ($L_A$), total system size ($L$), and the chaotic/ergodic regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is in a chaotic/ergodic regime where the system acts as its own bath; the study focuses on the infinite temperature limit or pure states.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the time dependence of $F_k(t)$ and $\Delta_k(t)$ for different orders, the scaling of thermalization rates with system size, and the comparison between exponential decay of moments versus sub-exponential decay of the norm distance.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the mathematical distinction between full and deep thermalization, presents a case study using the kicked Ising chain to extract relaxation rates, analyzes the scaling of these rates with system size and order $k$, and uses Random Matrix Theory to explain late-time fluctuations and symmetry effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Isolated quantum systems typically approach thermal equilibrium as described by the Eigenstate Thermalization Hypothesis (ETH). Going beyond this involves either higher order correlators (full thermalization) or the formation of state designs, i.e., the approach of moments of state ensembles after a projective measurement towards thermal equilibrium (deep thermalization). We compare these two extensions of ETH using extensive numerical studies within a paradigmatic model for chaotic many-body quantum dynamics. For this we find exponential relaxation for both extensions: For deep thermalization all moments relax with the same rate, which approximately equals the relaxation rate of the autocorrelation function captured by ETH. In contrast, higher order correlation functions in full thermalization approach equilibrium faster. This means that at higher orders full thermalization is faster than deep thermalization.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27824"></a>
### [Compressed Sensing for Efficient Fidelity Estimation of GHZ States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27824v1)

**Authors:** Farrokh Labib, David Nicholaeff, Vincent Russo, William J. Zeng  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27824v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper introduces a compressed sensing protocol to efficiently estimate the fidelity of large GHZ states with significantly fewer measurements than traditional methods. By leveraging the sparsity of parity oscillations and implementing error detection via flag qubits, the authors demonstrate robust performance on both simulators and real trapped-ion hardware. This approach provides a scalable pathway for verifying multipartite entanglement in the presence of experimental noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in quantum information and open quantum systems as it provides a scalable method for verifying large-scale entanglement and addresses the practical challenges of noise and measurement bias in many-body quantum states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently estimating the fidelity of large-scale multipartite GHZ states while overcoming the prohibitive measurement overhead of standard tomography and the high sampling requirements of traditional parity oscillation methods.

**Main result.** The proposed compressed sensing protocol achieves a double exponential reduction in measurement overhead, scaling logarithmically with system size, and maintains high accuracy even in noisy experimental environments on trapped-ion hardware.

**Method.** The authors use compressed sensing (Lasso and OLS) to reconstruct sparse parity oscillation signals in the DCT basis, combined with a flag-qubit strategy for error detection and readout error mitigation (REM) for error correction.

**Key observables.** Fidelity (decomposed into population and coherence), parity oscillation signal, phase offset, and coverage ratio of flag qubits.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of random measurement angles (M approx 5 ln N), number of experimental shots (S), and gate error rates (single-qubit p1q and two-qubit p2q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The parity oscillation signal is assumed to be sparse in the Discrete Cosine Transform (DCT) basis.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the binary tree structure for flag placement; Figure 2 shows the bounded error of the CS estimator; Figure 3 shows the success probability of frequency recovery; Figure 4 and 5 demonstrate the trade-off between flag-induced noise and heralded fidelity; Figure 6 shows hardware results for N=50.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of GHZ verification, defines the compressed sensing framework for sparse signal reconstruction, details the error detection/mitigation strategy using flag qubits and REM, presents simulation results for scalability, and concludes with experimental validation on trapped-ion hardware.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Accurately characterizing multipartite entangled states is a critical challenge in quantum information processing. In this work, we focus on applying compressed sensing techniques to efficiently estimate the fidelity of Greenberger-Horne-Zeilinger (GHZ) states. By exploiting the inherent sparsity of these states, our compressed sensing protocol drastically reduces the measurement overhead traditionally required for state verification while maintaining high accuracy. To evaluate the practical performance of this approach, we test the protocol on GHZ states using both quantum simulators and Quantinuum's trapped-ion hardware. Furthermore, we implement error detection techniques during our hardware evaluations, demonstrating the robustness and viability of compressed sensing for fidelity estimation in noisy experimental environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27055"></a>
### [Nonlocal nonstabilizerness in free fermion models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27055v1)

**Authors:** Mario Collura, Benjamin Béri, Emanuele Tirrito  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27055v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper explores a measure of nonstabilizerness called nonlocal magic in the context of free fermion models. It provides an analytical bound for Gaussian states and shows how this quantity behaves during phase transitions and quantum quenches. The findings reveal that nonlocal magic can capture physics, such as the separation from entanglement, that standard entanglement measures might miss.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for many-body dynamics and quantum information because it identifies a new resource-like quantity (nonlocal magic) that behaves differently from standard entanglement during quantum evolution and phase transitions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates 'nonlocal magic,' which quantifies the irreducible nonstabilizerness of a bipartite quantum state after optimizing over local basis changes, specifically within the context of fermionic Gaussian states.

**Main result.** The authors derive a closed-form entanglement spectrum bound for nonlocal magic in pure fermionic Gaussian states and demonstrate that it peaks near critical points in the Kitaev chain. They also show that nonlocal magic grows diffusively in random circuits and exhibits a separation from entanglement in the XY chain quench.

**Method.** The study employs random matrix theory (circular unitary ensemble), simulated annealing over local Gaussian unitary transformations, and analytical derivations involving the singular values of the subsystem-restricted covariance matrix.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on free fermion models, including pure fermionic Gaussian states, the Gaussian Haar ensemble, the Kitaev chain, and the XY chain.

**Key observables.** Nonlocal magic, entanglement spectrum, covariance matrix singular values, and entanglement entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trivial and topological phases of the Kitaev chain, critical points, and the XX limit of the XY chain.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is primarily restricted to fermionic Gaussian states and Gaussian evolution.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of nonlocal magic, provides a closed-form bound for Gaussian states, benchmarks the result via simulated annealing, analyzes the thermodynamic limit using random matrix theory, and then applies the framework to study ground states (Kitaev chain) and dynamical evolution (random circuits and XY chain quenches).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonlocal magic quantifies the irreducible nonstabilizerness of a bipartite quantum state after optimizing over local basis changes. We study nonlocal magic for pure fermionic Gaussian states, and derive a simple closed-form entanglement spectrum bound in terms of the singular values of the subsystem-restricted covariance matrix. We benchmark our result against simulated annealing over local Gaussian unitary transformations, which supports optimality along the full local Gaussian orbit. For states drawn from the Gaussian Haar ensemble, we show that the average nonlocal magic is extensive and determine its thermodynamic limit using random matrix theory for the appropriate circular unitary ensemble. We also study Gaussian ground states, focusing on the Kitaev chain, and find that nonlocal magic is suppressed deep in both trivial and topological phases and peaks near the critical points. Finally, we investigate Gaussian evolution via random circuits and in quenches with the XY chain. For random circuits, we find that nonlocal magic grows diffusively, while in the XY chain the XX limit reveals a striking separation between nonlocal magic and entanglement.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28121"></a>
### [Quantum Lattice Boltzmann Solutions for Transport under 3D Spatially Varying Advection on Trapped Ion Hardware](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28121v1)

**Authors:** Sayonee Ray, Jezer Jojo, Jason Iaconis, Abeynaya Gnanasekaran, Apurva Tiwari, Martin Roetteler, Chris Hill, Jay Pathak  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28121v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper advances the Quantum Lattice Boltzmann Method by implementing 3D spatially varying advection on IonQ trapped-ion hardware. It identifies significant bottlenecks in density readout and proposes using MPS shadow tomography to overcome them. The work also introduces a new method for simulating wall boundaries in quantum fluid simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it demonstrates the practical application of quantum algorithms to simulate complex fluid-like transport and introduces new methods for efficient state readout.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Implementing the Quantum Lattice Boltzmann Method (QLBM) for transport under 3D spatially varying advection on real quantum hardware.

**Main result.** Demonstrated transport under non-uniform velocity fields on IonQ trapped-ion systems and identified density readout/reloading as a primary algorithmic bottleneck.

**Method.** Utilization of the Quantum Lattice Boltzmann Method (QLBM) implemented on IonQ Forte and Barium-based trapped-ion hardware, including the proposal of MPS shadow tomography for scalable readout.

**Model / system.** The study uses the advection-diffusion equation as a canonical model for transport phenomena, implemented via the mesoscopic particle distribution functions of the QLBM on IonQ trapped-ion systems.

**Key observables.** Fluid density distributions and transport behavior under non-uniform velocity fields.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 3D spatially varying advection fields and wall boundary conditions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the discrete Boltzmann transport equation for approximating macroscopic advection-diffusion behavior.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QLBM framework, presents the implementation of non-uniform velocity fields on trapped-ion hardware, analyzes readout bottlenecks, proposes MPS shadow tomography for scaling, and introduces a new method for wall boundaries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Quantum Lattice Boltzmann Method (QLBM) has emerged as one of the most promising quantum computing approaches for the numerical simulation of problems in computational fluid dynamics (CFD). The dynamics is formulated in terms of mesoscopic particle distribution functions governed by a discrete Boltzmann transport equation, comprising local streaming and collision operations. In this work, the resulting macroscopic behavior corresponds to the advection-diffusion equation, which we adopt as a canonical model problem for transport phenomena. Building upon recent progress in QLBM implementations, we advance towards more realistic problem settings that better reflect conventional CFD requirements. We address, for the first time, transport under the action of non uniform velocity fields on quantum hardware. We implement our demonstration using IonQ's trapped-ion systems including Forte generation systems and a 64-qubit Barium development system similar to the forthcoming IonQ Tempo line. We identify the density readout and subsequent reloading of the fluid density as a potential bottleneck of the current algorithm and discuss several approaches to mitigate this bottleneck. We identify the use of MPS shadow tomography as a promising method to efficiently scale the readout to large system with complex density distributions. Lastly, we introduce and simulate a novel method to implement wall boundaries for advection-diffusion in QLBM, and discuss the prospects of scaling to higher-complexity problems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27664"></a>
### [An Analytical Approach to Design Space Exploration for Cavity-Mediated Quantum State Transfer in Multi-core Architectures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27664v1)

**Authors:** Biel Pons Zaragoza, Junaid Khan, Rohit Sarma Sarkar, Sahar Ben Rached, Carmen G. Almudever, Eduard Alarcon, Sergi Abadal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27664v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27664_figures/2604.27664_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Schematic of a waveguide-mediated interconnect between two qubit chips in a modular quantum computing system.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27664_figures/2604.27664_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2604.27664_figures/2604.27664_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27664_figures/2604.27664_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Comparison between heatmaps using the analytical and simulation results for κ = γ = 0.1 MHz.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27664_figures/2604.27664_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Study of the types of N combinations. If a and b are both odd, fidelity will always be low. If N is even, fidelity will be high with low latency. a) Occupation probability for N = 3 for a lossless system. b) Occupation probability for N = 4 for a lossless system. c) Contour map of some relevant values of N for low fidelity regions due to the periodicity of the envelope and the internal waves, for κ = 0.1 MHz and γ = 0.1 MHz.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new analytical method for rapidly simulating quantum state transfer in multi-core quantum processors. By replacing expensive numerical simulations with exact closed-form expressions, the authors enable efficient design space exploration and identify specific parameter regimes that optimize the trade-off between fidelity and latency.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a highly efficient tool for analyzing open quantum system dynamics and offers physical insight into how dissipation and detuning affect state transfer, which is crucial for designing scalable quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing quantum state transfer in multi-core quantum architectures is hindered by the high computational cost of numerical simulations and the difficulty of exploring the design space to balance fidelity and latency.

**Main result.** The authors derived an analytical framework that is two orders of magnitude faster than standard numerical solvers, enabling large-scale parameter sweeps and revealing physical phenomena like destructive interference-induced low-fidelity regions.

**Method.** The study uses an analytical approach based on the Monte Carlo wave-function method and a non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonian to derive closed-form solutions for qubit occupation probabilities.

**Model / system.** A two-qubit system (chips A and B) coupled via a single-mode waveguide, modeled using a Jaynes-Cummings-type Hamiltonian and the Lindblad master equation to account for qubit decay and waveguide loss.

**Key observables.** Qubit occupation probabilities, state transfer fidelity, transfer latency, and an efficiency-based function for optimization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength (g), qubit-waveguide detuning (delta omega), qubit decay rate (gamma), waveguide decay rate (kappa), and a trade-off parameter (eta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the Rotating Wave Approximation (RWA), a single waveguide mode, a single-excitation subspace, and neglects quantum jumps due to the initial empty state of the waveguide.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a system schematic; Figure 2 compares analytical and numerical time evolution; Figure 3 shows fidelity heatmaps; Figure 4 compares analytical and numerical heatmaps; Figure 5 explores the impact of the ratio between oscillation and envelope frequencies; Figure 6 demonstrates latency and fidelity trade-offs.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the derivation of the Hamiltonian and Master equation, followed by the derivation of closed-form analytical solutions, validation against QuTiP numerical simulations, and finally, the application of the model to design space exploration and optimization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In multi-core quantum computing architectures, waveguide-mediated interconnects are essential for facilitating fast, high-fidelity quantum state transfer between qubits located in different chips. However, optimizing these systems typically relies on computationally expensive numerical simulations that offer limited physical insight. In this work, we derive exact analytical expressions for the state transfer dynamics of a two-qubit system coupled via a waveguide, modeled through a Jaynes-Cummings Hamiltonian and the Lindblad master equation. We apply the Monte Carlo wave-function method and obtain a closed-form solution for qubit occupation probabilities that accounts for both detuning and dissipative losses. Our analytical framework provides a significant computational speedup compared to standard numerical solvers, enabling large-scale parameter sweeps while maintaining high precision in both fidelity and latency predictions. Furthermore, the model reveals and explains systematic low-fidelity regions arising from destructive interference between internal oscillations and detuning-induced envelopes, which are phenomena that are difficult to characterize through numerical means alone. Finally, we propose a simplified latency model and an efficiency-based function to enable rapid identification of optimal operating points. This analytical approach provides a robust foundation for the design and optimization of interconnects in multi-core quantum processors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27327"></a>
### [High-key-rate Fully-Passive Quantum Access Network with Thermal Source](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27327v1)

**Authors:** H. W. Yin, B. D. Zhu, H. Peng, T. Wang, X. Q. Jiang, Y. K. Xu, G. H. Zeng  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27327v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5**

**Summary.** The paper presents an experimental demonstration of a fully passive quantum access network that achieves a record-breaking key rate of 19.48 Mbps. By using a passive state preparation scheme, the researchers show a scalable point-to-multi-point architecture that is compatible with existing classical optical fiber infrastructure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information as it demonstrates a scalable, high-rate architecture for quantum communication networks using passive components.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high-speed, stable, and low-resource quantum access networks (QAN) that can scale from point-to-point to point-to-multi-point without the instability of active protocols.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a downstream fully passive quantum access network protocol achieving a record-breaking key generation rate of 19.48 Mbps per quantum network unit.

**Method.** The researchers implemented a passive state preparation (PSP) scheme within a quantum passive optical network (QPON) architecture using hybrid free-space and single-mode fiber channels.

**Model / system.** A downstream quantum access network utilizing a passive state preparation (PSP) protocol for continuous-variable quantum key distribution (CVQKD) across a point-to-multi-point architecture.

**Key observables.** Final key generation rate (Mbps per quantum network unit).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key generation rate of 19.48 Mbps; hybrid free-space and single-mode fiber channels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The network is compatible with existing classical optical communication infrastructure.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for optimized QANs, proposes a passive state preparation (PSP) protocol, demonstrates experimental implementation using hybrid channels, and discusses compatibility with classical infrastructure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

To accommodate classical communication systems with progressively increasing transmission rates, quantum access networks (QAN) have undergone systematic and protocol-level optimizations in recent years, where quantum passive optical network (QPON) architectures are gaining significant attention due to their simple structure. It is challenging for the previous QAN based on active protocols or Stokes operator coding protocols to achieve high-speed linear modulation with high extinction ratio and stability under practical conditions. In this work, we propose and experimentally demonstrate a downstream fully passive quantum access network protocol using passive state preparation (PSP) with free-space and single-mode fiber hybrid channels, and the final key generation rate is up to a record-breaking 19.48 Mbps per quantum network unit. The proposed PSP-QPON scheme extends the scope of PSP-CVQKD from point-to-point to point-to-multi-point networks, which enables high-key-rate, high-stability, and low-resource-consumption implementation. Moreover, the network channel in this experiment is fully compatible with access networks in classical optical communications, which allows integration with existing optical infrastructure without the need for additional modifications, providing a promising solution for local area network quantum access network at home or a mobile terminal.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a high-fidelity iSWAP gate between fixed-frequency transmons using a parametric flux drive on a specialized coupler. By operating at the zero-flux sweet spot, the researchers significantly reduced decoherence and parasitic ZZ interactions. This approach provides a scalable path toward high-fidelity entangling gates in superconducting quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics due to its focus on controlling light-matter-like interactions (qubit-coupler hybridization) and managing decoherence through parametric modulation and frequency-domain engineering.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** High-fidelity two-qubit gates in superconducting circuits are often limited by pulse distortion, decoherence from qubit-coupler hybridization, and residual ZZ interactions caused by large flux-pulse amplitudes.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated a high-fidelity (99.92%) parametrically driven iSWAP gate at the zero-flux sweet spot, achieving reduced error by minimizing qubit-coupler hybridization and canceling ZZ interactions.

**Method.** The researchers used a capacitively shunted double-transmon coupler (CSDTC) with a parametric flux drive, employing randomized benchmarking and quantum process tomography for characterization and virtual-Z gates for phase error compensation.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two highly detuned, fixed-frequency transmon qubits coupled via a Capacitively Shunted Double-Transmon Coupler (CSDTC) that hybridizes into symmetric and antisymmetric modes.

**Key observables.** Average gate fidelity, iSWAP interaction rate, effective ZZ interaction strength, leakage error, and qubit-coupler hybridization fraction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate fidelity of 99.92(2)%, gate time of 112 ns, qubit detuning of 498 MHz, and a hybridization fraction of approximately 0.05.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calibration of the $|11angle$ state was omitted because it does not affect populations in the single-excitation subspace; the model assumes the validity of the three-level approximation for dynamical ZZ interaction.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the device micrograph, circuit diagram, and energy spectrum; Figure 2 displays chevron patterns and interaction rates; Figure 3 presents error components; Figure 6 shows the pulse sequence; Figure 14 shows Pauli Transfer Matrices; Figure 15 shows IQ readout clusters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the hardware architecture and the problem of flux-induced errors, describes the parametric driving mechanism, presents experimental verification of the iSWAP gate and its fidelity, analyzes the error budget and ZZ suppression, and concludes with a detailed calibration and characterization framework.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A double-transmon coupler (DTC) enables a fast, high-fidelity CZ gate between two highly detuned, fixed-frequency transmon qubits. Moreover, a recently proposed capacitively shunted DTC (CSDTC) realizes a small residual ZZ interaction over a wide flux-bias range around zero flux, eliminating the necessity of static flux biasing while maintaining high CZ-gate fidelity. However, CZ gates with the DTC and CSDTC require baseband flux pulses with large amplitudes, which are vulnerable to pulse distortion and decoherence due to large qubit-coupler hybridization. To address these issues, we experimentally demonstrate a parametrically driven iSWAP gate operated at zero flux bias between highly detuned, fixed-frequency transmon qubits coupled through a CSDTC. Using a simple flux-drive waveform without predistortion, we realize an average gate fidelity of 99.92(2)% at a total gate time of 112 ns. The observed high-fidelity performance is consistent with small qubit-coupler hybridization and small effective ZZ interaction during the gate. Our numerical simulations reproduce the experimentally observed iSWAP interaction rate and effective ZZ interaction, demonstrating the applicability of the theoretical model not only to spectral information but also to time-domain dynamics such as gate operations. These results boost further progress in the research of superconducting quantum computers.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the transition between weak and strong quantum measurements in the presence of thermal noise and environmental decoherence. It demonstrates that thermal effects can counterintuitively enhance or suppress anomalous weak values depending on the temperature regime and pointer squeezing. The findings offer a framework for understanding how dissipation reshapes quantum measurement statistics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into how environmental noise and thermal fluctuations can be leveraged or mitigated in quantum metrology and the control of quantum measurement-induced dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how thermal instabilities, environmental decoherence, and open quantum dynamics affect the continuous transition from weak to strong quantum measurements and the emergence of anomalous weak values.

**Main result.** The study reveals that thermal noise and dissipation can non-monotonically reshape measurement statistics, where specific temperature regimes and pointer squeezing can either suppress or enhance anomalous amplification and protect pointer coherence.

**Method.** The authors develop a general framework using the Generalized Amplitude Damping (GAD) channel, Kraus operator decomposition, and thermal Gaussian state formalism to derive the conditioned apparatus state and measurement statistics.

**Model / system.** A microscopic two-level system (qubit) interacting via an impulsive von Neumann coupling with a bosonic single-mode Gaussian pointer (modeled as a displaced, squeezed, thermal state) under the influence of a thermal reservoir.

**Key observables.** Measurement strength (s), post-selection success probability (P_succ), quantum overlap (O), spatial amplification factor (A_X), and momentum amplification factor (A_P).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement strength (s), dissipation strength (gamma), thermal population (p), pointer thermal occupation (n_bar), squeezing magnitude (r), and squeezing phase (chi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measurement interaction is assumed to be instantaneous (impulsive), and the apparatus is modeled as a Gaussian thermal squeezed state.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the measurement scheme including interaction and post-selection; Fig 4 illustrates the impact of pointer coherence and thermal effects on the transition; Fig 5 illustrates the effects of squeezing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the measurement scheme, develops the mathematical framework for the GAD channel and conditioned state, analyzes the transition from weak to strong regimes, and explores the specific roles of thermal noise, squeezing, and pointer momentum.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum measurement is physically realized through a finite dynamical interaction between a system and a measuring apparatus, giving rise to a continuous transition from weak to strong regimes. While this crossover is well understood under ideal conditions, the combined role of thermal instabilities and pre- and post-selection open dynamics has not been systematically addressed. Here, we develop a general framework to analyze the weak-to-strong measurement transition in the simultaneous presence of environmental decoherence and thermal noise. We model the probe as a thermal Gaussian state, explicitly incorporating temperature-dependent fluctuations in the measuring device, and include open-system evolution of the measured system prior to post-selection. By deriving the apparatus's final state, we show that the measurement statistics are modified in a nontrivial, highly sensitive manner by the temperature regime of the system's thermal instabilities, the probe's thermal properties, and the particular choice of pre- and post-selection. This approach allows us to characterize how thermal effects reshape the weak-value condition and influence the emergence of projective behavior across the full measurement crossover.

</details>

### [Light-Cone Structure of Propagation of Entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27170v1)

**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27170v1>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a rigorous 'light-cone' for entanglement, proving that entanglement cannot be transported or maintained across a distance faster than a specific velocity. It provides a fundamental speed limit for quantum information transfer in both lattice and continuous systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for the speed limits of entanglement, which is critical for designing reliable quantum networks and understanding information spread in many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Establishing a rigorous, effective light-cone structure for the propagation of entanglement in bipartite quantum systems, providing a hard lower bound on the time required to transport or maintain entanglement.

**Main result.** The paper proves that entanglement (quantified by the Schmidt number) propagates within a defined light cone, establishing a fundamental speed limit for entanglement transport in quantum networks.

**Method.** The author uses operator theory, trace norm estimates, and the Duhamel principle to compare full quantum evolution with free evolution, utilizing the Schmidt number to quantify entanglement.

**Model / system.** Bipartite systems (A and B) with localized couplings, including discrete lattice models (tight-binding) and continuous semi-relativistic N-particle Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Schmidt number (n_S), local entanglement, and trace norm distance to separable states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Propagation speed (c), distance between coupling and observation domains (d), and the degree of separability (kappa).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dispersion law must satisfy an analytic continuation condition (Condition AH), and the interaction must be localized with specific norm constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the physical setup of entanglement creation and measurement; Figure 2 depicts the spatial-temporal light cone boundary.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement propagation, defines the mathematical framework and Hamiltonian, presents theorems establishing the light-cone structure and Schmidt number bounds, and concludes with technical proofs and references.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For a wide class of bipartite systems with localized couplings, we establish existence of an effective light-cone for propagation of entanglement. This result yields a hard lower bound on the time it takes, under ideal conditions (no loss, no decoherence), to transport entanglement to a distant location (say, a node of a graph-structured quantum network), or to maintain it there.

### [Size-Limited Room Temperature Single-Photon Emission from Sidewall-Treated Fractional Dimension InGaN Quantum Dots: Determined by Density-of-States-Corrected Ultrafast Carrier Dynamics and Improved Signal-to-Noise Ratio](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27718v1)

**Authors:** Pratim K. Saha  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27718v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **3/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27718_figures/2604.27718_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. (a) diameter-dependent radiative and</sub>

**Summary.** This work demonstrates high-purity single-photon emission from InGaN quantum dots at room temperature by controlling their size and surface properties. By reducing the QD diameter below 31 nm, the researchers used Auger recombination to suppress multi-photon events. This provides a scalable pathway for developing semiconductor-based quantum light sources for quantum information applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a detailed study of how many-body effects (Auger recombination) and environmental coupling (surface states) can be engineered to control the purity of a quantum light source at room temperature.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Identifying the physical limits and optimal geometric parameters for achieving high-purity, room-temperature single-photon emission (SPE) from InGaN quantum dots.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that SPE is achievable at room temperature for QD diameters below 31 nm, where Auger recombination suppresses multi-photon events and surface treatments minimize non-radiative recombination.

**Method.** The study combines experimental micro-photoluminescence and Hanbury-Brown-Twiss autocorrelation measurements with a theoretical model using COMSOL and Silvaco to simulate carrier dynamics and strain-induced potential profiles.

**Model / system.** Site-controlled, fractional-dimension In0.14Ga0.86N quantum dots embedded in vertical GaN nanowires, fabricated via dry and wet-chemical/photoelectrochemical etching.

**Key observables.** Second-order autocorrelation g2(0), higher-order correlations g3(0) and g4(0), signal-to-noise ratio (r), and spectral linewidths of exciton and biexciton peaks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** QD diameters (ranging from 3 nm to 36 nm), exciton Bohr radius (3 nm), and the single-photon limit (g2(0) < 0.5).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglected Shockley-Read-Hall recombination due to low probability in small volumes; assumed g2(0) can be expressed via the ratio of radiative to total recombination rates.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the InGaN/GaN heterostructure, SEM images of the etched samples, and micro-photoluminescence spectra showing exciton and biexciton peaks.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of room-temperature SPE, describes the fabrication and characterization of InGaN nanowire QDs, presents a carrier dynamics model involving Auger and surface recombination, and concludes with a generalized framework for predicting SPE probability based on QD geometry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Room-temperature single-photon emission (SPE) resulting from a biexciton-exciton cascaded decay is demonstrated for the first time from chemically and photoelectrochemically etched site-controlled In0.14Ga0.86N quantum dots (QDs) embedded in vertical GaN nanowires. Diameter-dependent biexciton-exciton dynamics are analysed to determine the eligibility of QD as a single-photon emitter. The signal-to-noise ratio degrades with increasing QD diameter. Background noise photons pose a bottleneck to achieving SPE. This is also explained from a carrier dynamics perspective. Surface recombination contributes to inhomogeneous broadening at QD diameters larger than 35 nm. Below 35 nm, density-of-states-corrected Auger gradually becomes the principal biexciton-decay route with further reduction in QD diameter, thereby quenching the possibility of thermal broadening and setting a threshold for SPE. Below 9 nm, the Auger recombination rate becomes manyfold of other decay rates, causing multi-photon suppression via single Auger decay to form an exciton. Surface recombination probability of this exciton is minimized while biexciton state filling probability is maximized by reducing sidewall surface states through wet-treatment. These improve biexciton state preparation and enhance the single-photon purity of the exciton towards the exciton Bohr radius (3 nm) regime. Far away from this regime, higher-order autocorrelations to characterize quantum emission involving multi-photon events are discussed. This study establishes a generalized physical framework for predetermining SPE probability as a function of QD surface and geometry down to the exciton Bohr radius regime, with practical implementations. This work shows the pathway to design and develop next-generation semiconductor QDs for high-purity room-temperature SPE.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27339"></a>
### [Fixed-PVM Born Rule Uniqueness from Fisher Non-Expansion and Operational Calibration](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27339v1)

**Authors:** Aaron Lax  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27339v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27339_figures/2604.27339_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27339_figures/2604.27339_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27339_figures/2604.27339_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27339_figures/2604.27339_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27339_figures/2604.27339_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that the Born rule is the unique readout map for a fixed measurement if one assumes the map is regular, obeys a quantum Cramer-Rao bound, and is correctly calibrated. It achieves this by showing that such constraints force the readout map to be a rigid, identity-preserving map in a geometric space.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a new, purely geometric perspective on the foundations of quantum mechanics, showing how information-theoretic constraints (Cramer-Rao bounds) can recover the standard Born rule without needing Gleason-type basis-independence.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether a specific set of geometric and operational primitives is sufficient to uniquely force the Born rule for a fixed rank-1 projective measurement.

**Main result.** The paper proves that if a readout map satisfies square-root regularity, a universal readout Cramer-Rao bound (Fisher non-expansion), and operational calibration, then the map must be the Born rule.

**Method.** The authors use an information-geometric approach, employing a square-root chart to transform the readout map into a 1-Lipschitz self-map of a spherical orthant, then apply geometric rigidity theorems.

**Model / system.** A finite-dimensional complex Hilbert space (dimension d >= 2) and a fixed rank-1 projective valued measurement (PVM) on pure states.

**Key observables.** Classical Fisher Information (Fcl), Quantum Fisher Information (FQ), and the readout probabilities of the PVM.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimension d, Fubini-Study metric, and Fisher-Rao/Hellinger metric.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The result is restricted to pure states and a fixed measurement; it assumes the universal readout Cramer-Rao bound as an admissibility postulate.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Born rule uniqueness, defines the geometric framework using square-root charts, presents the Vertex Rigidity Lemma, proves the main readout theorem via Fisher non-expansion, and discusses corollaries like escort-class uniqueness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fix a finite dimension $d \geq 2$ and a fixed rank-1 PVM $M=\{|e_1\rangle\langle e_1|,\ldots,|e_d\rangle\langle e_d|\}$ on ${\bf C}^d$. Let $P_M:\mathbb{CP}^{d-1}\toΔ^{d-1}$ be a readout map on pure states. We prove that three primitives force the Born rule for this fixed measurement: (i) square-root regularity of $R_M=\sqrt{P_M}$ along Fubini-Study geodesics, (ii) the universal readout Cramer-Rao bound $F_{\rm cl}\leq F_Q$ on smooth pure-state curves, and (iii) operational calibration on basis preparations $P_M([e_i])=δ_i$. The geometric core is a rigidity theorem for Fisher-non-expanding self-maps of the probability simplex: after conjugation by the square-root chart, such maps become round-metric 1-Lipschitz self-maps of the positive spherical orthant, and vertex fixing forces the identity. The main readout theorem is dimensionwise, fixed-PVM, and pure-state only. Escort-class Born uniqueness and the Markov/coarse-graining routes appear as corollaries or alternative routes.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27565"></a>
### [Magnonic Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27565v1)

**Authors:** Zi-Xu Lu, Gang Liu, Matteo Fadel, Jie Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27565v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Operation sequence used to generate the GKP state |0⟩L. The transmon qubit is initially in the ground state |g⟩and the magnon mode is in the squeezed vacuum state |r⟩. (b) Operation sequence used to perform the log- ical Hadamard gate (phase gate) onto |0⟩L to generate the GKP state |+⟩L (|ϕ+⟩L). </details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new method to create GKP error-correcting states using magnons in a hybrid superconducting circuit. By leveraging the natural squeezing of an ellipsoidal magnetic crystal, the authors show how to prepare and manipulate logical qubits for fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It introduces a novel hybrid platform (magnonics) for implementing continuous-variable quantum error correction, bridging magnonics with bosonic quantum computing and quantum sensing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of preparing non-Gaussian Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) states for bosonic quantum error correction and the lack of a demonstrated protocol within magnonic systems.

**Main result.** The authors provide the first protocol for preparing magnonic GKP-like states using a hybrid magnon-qubit system, demonstrating the ability to generate multi-component superposition states and implement logical Pauli gates.

**Method.** The protocol uses sequential rounds of conditional-displacement (CD) interactions and qubit projective measurements, utilizing the intrinsic squeezing from geometric anisotropy and cavity-mediated qubit control.

**Model / system.** A hybrid system consisting of an ellipsoidal magnetic crystal (magnon mode) coupled to a transmon superconducting qubit via a microwave cavity, modeled using a quantized Hamiltonian with a conditional-displacement interaction.

**Key observables.** Wigner functions, stabilizer expectation values, effective squeezing, logical fidelity, and marginal distributions P(q) and P(p).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Effective coupling chi/2pi = 7.55 MHz, squeezing parameter r approx 1, temperature T = 10 mK, and interaction time t approx 143.8 ns.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Large-detuning limit for cavity elimination, Rotating Wave Approximation (RWA), and neglecting small squeezing from counter-rotating terms.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the phase-space representation of stabilizers and logical operators alongside a schematic of the experimental setup; Figure 3 (not fully described) relates to the results of the protocol.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the physical platform and Hamiltonian, derives the effective conditional-displacement interaction via unitary transformations, details the state preparation protocol and logical gate implementation, and concludes with a discussion on dissipation and fidelity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Bosonic quantum error correction encodes a logical qubit in an oscillator, avoiding the hardware overhead of large qubit arrays. Among such encodings, Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) states are paticularly powerful because their phase-space grid structure protects against small displacement errors simultaneously in both conjugate quadratures. Here we provide the first protocol for preparing magnonic GKP states, which involves an ellipsoidal magnetic crystal effectively coupled to a superconducting qubit via a microwave cavity. The geometric anisotropy intrinsically squeezes the magnon mode, while the cavity-mediated qubit control realizes an effective conditional-displacement interaction. We show that two rounds of a conditional-displacement interaction and a qubit projective measurement yield three- and four-component magnonic GKP-like states. We also show how to realize single logical qubit gate operations, such as Pauli, Hadamard and phase gates, completing the logical Pauli basis of the approximate GKP code. Our results establish hybrid magnon-qubit systems as a promising platform for preparing bosonic code states, with applications in magnonic fault-tolerant quantum computation and quantum sensing.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper overcomes the computational bottleneck of evaluating non-local magic in fermionic systems by introducing a polynomial-time formula based on Majorana covariance matrix eigenvalues. It demonstrates that this magic follows specific scaling laws, such as a quadratic onset in random states and logarithmic growth at critical points. The result enables the experimental estimation of non-local magic in large-scale quantum processors using shadow tomography.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable, analytical framework for quantifying a complex resource (non-local magic) in many-body systems, bridging quantum information theory with the dynamics of integrable and random quantum circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Evaluating non-local magic (nonstabilizerness tied to entanglement) is generally computationally intractable due to the need for minimization over the full Hilbert space.

**Main result.** The authors derive a closed-form, polynomial-time computable expression for fermionic non-local magic based on the eigenvalues of the reduced Majorana covariance matrix, providing a scalable way to characterize magic in large systems.

**Method.** The derivation utilizes the BCS canonical form of fermionic Gaussian states and applies random matrix theory, block-Toeplitz matrix theory, and the quasiparticle picture to study various physical regimes.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on pure fermionic Gaussian states (free fermions) on a lattice, including specific models like the 1D XY chain, the Ising model, and the SYK2 model.

**Key observables.** Fermionic non-local magic (FNL), stabilizer entropy (M_alpha), stabilizer purity (zeta_alpha), and entanglement entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bipartition ratio (r), subsystem size (l), transverse field (mu), anisotropy (eta), and the Renyi index (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The minimization of non-local magic is restricted to the subgroup of local Gaussian unitaries rather than the full unitary group.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Page-like curve for magic in Haar-random and SYK2 states; Figure 2 validates the analytical formula and shows scaling in the XY model; Figure 3 illustrates the ballistic and diffusive spreading of magic during quantum quenches and in random circuits; Figure 4 compares the proposed formula against numerical optimization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-local magic, derives a closed-form expression for fermionic Gaussian states, applies this to random states and the SYK2 model, extends the analysis to equilibrium properties of the XY model, investigates non-equilibrium dynamics via quantum quenches and random circuits, and concludes with stability and error analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement and magic are fundamental resources that capture the complexity of quantum many-body systems. Non-local magic isolates the irreducible nonstabilizerness intrinsically tied to entanglement. However, evaluating this quantity generally requires a prohibitive minimization over the full Hilbert space, making it computationally inaccessible beyond a few qubits. Here, we overcome this bottleneck by suggesting a closed-form expression for the non-local stabilizer entropies of fermionic Gaussian states over local Gaussian unitaries, which can be evaluated in polynomial time directly from the eigenvalues of the reduced Majorana covariance matrix. We apply this framework to characterize fermionic non-local magic across diverse physical regimes: we derive an exact Page-like curve for typical random states, reveal logarithmic scaling at the quantum critical point of the XY model, and establish a quasiparticle picture for magic generation during out-of-equilibrium quantum quenches. Crucially, because our result relies solely on two-point correlation functions, it provides a scalable route for the experimental estimation of fermionic non-local magic in large-scale quantum processors via fermionic shadow tomography.

</details>

### [CrossBench: Generalized Crosstalk Benchmark Generation for Quantum Computers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27183v1)

**Authors:** Jaden Hawley, Chi-Ren Shyu

<sub>Figure 2 shows box-and-whisker plots of the results from all seven runs for IBM Fez. We observe a noticeable difference in error on ID and CZ spectator gates. Using a t-test, we find ID spectators experience more error with SX drivers than ID drivers (p &lt; 0.01). We also find CZ spectators experience more error with CZ drivers than SX drivers (p = 0.021). Figure 3 shows the results for IBM Kingston. For this system, ID spectators experience higher error with X drivers than ID drivers (p &lt; 0.01) and experience more error with CZ drivers than ID drivers (p = 0.046). Additionally, SX spectators experience more error with X drivers than with ID drivers (p &lt; 0.01). For the Nighthawk system,...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces CrossBench, a scalable system for generating crosstalk benchmarks on large-scale NISQ devices. By using a graph-based approach to assign driver and spectator qubits, it provides an efficient way to identify which gates contribute most to error rates. This is crucial for improving error mitigation, circuit transpilation, and quantum hardware security.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Traditional crosstalk benchmarking methods like Randomized Benchmarking become too resource-intensive and costly to scale as quantum computers increase in size and topological complexity.

**Main result.** The CrossBench system successfully identifies gates that introduce significant crosstalk across multiple IBM quantum processors with strong statistical significance (p < 0.05).

**Method.** A customizable, scalable benchmark generator using a graph-labeling algorithm to assign 'driver' and 'spectator' qubits to detect gate-dependent error variations.

**Model / system.** NISQ-era superconducting quantum processors, specifically IBM Heron-class (Fez, Kingston) and Nighthawk-class (Miami) architectures.

**Key observables.** The change in error rate experienced by a spectator qubit when neighboring driver qubits execute specific gates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Native gate sets (X, SX, CZ, ID), driver depth (d_D), error threshold (delta), and device topology.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes that variations in error rates between different driver gates are primarily due to crosstalk and that identity (ID) gates introduce negligible additional error.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the circuit construction of driver and spectator qubits; Figures 2-4 are box-and-whisker plots of error rate distributions; Figures 5-7 are bar charts showing averaged CrossBench results across different IBM processors.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability problem of crosstalk, proposes the CrossBench algorithm and graph-labeling method, details the experimental implementation on IBM hardware, presents statistical results across multiple devices, and discusses limitations and future security applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

As quantum computers continue to increase in size and topological complexity, benchmarking crosstalk becomes more complex and resource-intensive. This limits the ability to obtain relevant crosstalk metrics for applications such as error mitigation, quantum computer security, and circuit transpilation. There applications benefit from accessible metrics on how each gate contributes to crosstalk. However, crosstalk metrics are rarely provided by quantum computer providers and can be expensive to obtain on modern large NISQ devices. In this work, we propose CrossBench, a customizable system that generates crosstalk benchmarks for finitely large NISQ devices with arbitrary topologies. CrossBench uses a custom graph labeling algorithm to generate crosstalk benchmarks for a given quantum topology and gate set. These benchmarks can be used to estimate the average contribution of each gate's crosstalk to qubit error rates. We evaluate the effectiveness of CrossBench by generating and running benchmarks on multiple IBM quantum computers with different topologies. Our results show that CrossBench can identify gates that introduce significant crosstalk across all tested devices, with strong statistical significance (p < 0.05). These promising results show that CrossBench can give simple and accessible crosstalk benchmarks for modern NISQ systems.

</details>

### [Formulating Subgroup Discovery as a Quantum Optimization Problem for Network Security](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27153v1)

**Authors:** Samuel Spell, Chi-Ren Shyu  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27153v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27153_figures/2604.27153_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Subgroup quality vs. subgroup size at k = 3 for the 20-qubit Probe configuration on ibm_pittsburgh. Green stars mark the exhaustive optimum (best WRAcc at any k), red triangles the beam- search results, and orange diamonds the QAOA-sampled subgroups. QAOA recovers subgroups in the same Pareto region as the classical baselines, confirming hardware competitiveness at this scale (𝑟, =</sub>

</details>

<img src="2604.27153_figures/2604.27153_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: QUBO fit quality for the R2L-filtered 10-qubit configuration. Each point is one QAOA-sampled bitstring plotted against its quadratic QUBO objective value; the OLS fit (R² = 0.989, Spearman |ρ| = 0.899) confirms the QUBO accurately ranks the WRAcc landscape.</sub>

<sub>Figure 5: Depth-matched comparison at k = 5 for the R2L-filtered configuration. </details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a quantum-enhanced pipeline for discovering interpretable rules in network intrusion detection by formulating feature selection as a quantum optimization problem. It shows that QAOA can find complex multi-feature attack patterns that classical greedy algorithms miss, though it also identifies a clear performance degradation limit due to hardware noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of finding interpretable, multi-feature interaction patterns (subgroups) in network security datasets, which is an NP-hard combinatorial optimization problem that classical greedy heuristics often fail to solve optimally.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that QAOA can identify high-precision attack patterns missed by classical beam search, while also establishing an empirical NISQ scaling boundary where hardware noise causes performance to collapse around 30 qubits.

**Method.** The researchers formulate Subgroup Discovery as a Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) problem using a least-squares regression of the WRAcc landscape and solve it using the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA) on IBM hardware.

**Model / system.** The problem is mapped to an Ising Hamiltonian with ZZ couplings on the IBM 'ibm_pittsburgh' superconducting processor, using a transverse-field mixer Hamiltonian for the QAOA circuit.

**Key observables.** WRAcc (Weighted Relative Accuracy), approximation ratios r5 (Hamiltonian quality) and r6 (WRAcc recovery), and test precision.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit count (10-30), circuit depth (p=1, 2), target cardinality (K), and the penalty weight (lambda).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The WRAcc landscape can be accurately approximated by a quadratic surrogate model; the study is limited by the noise levels of current NISQ-era hardware.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the end-to-end QAOA pipeline; Figure 2 compares subgroup quality between exhaustive, beam search, and QAOA methods; Figure 3 validates the QUBO surrogate fit (R2=0.989); Figure 4 shows a Pareto front of subgroup quality.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the security problem and data preprocessing to formulating the QUBO/Ising Hamiltonian, executing the QAOA on hardware, benchmarking against classical heuristics, and characterizing hardware scaling limits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While current network intrusion detection systems achieve satisfactory accuracy, they often lack explainability. Subgroup Discovery (SD) addresses this by building interpretable rules that characterize feature interactions associated with attack traffic. With large datasets, classical heuristic beam search methods struggle with exponentially scaling search spaces and can prune critical multi-feature interactions. This paper introduces a quantum-enhanced pipeline for SD applied to network intrusion detection using NSL-KDD, formulating SD as quantum optimization for the first time. By encoding feature selection as a Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) and solving it via the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA) on IBM Quantum hardware (ibm_pittsburgh), the pipeline identifies subgroups of network features that discriminate normal from attack traffic. A least-squares regression QUBO formulation fits the Weighted Relative Accuracy (WRAcc) landscape over feature subsets, with surrogate sampling for larger QUBOs. Results are benchmarked against exhaustive enumeration and Beam Search using ratios for Hamiltonian quality and WRAcc. Hardware scaling experiments on ibm_pittsburgh (10-30 qubits) reveal that QAOA at depth p = 1 shows WRAcc ratios of 0.983 at 10 qubits, 0.971 at 15 qubits, 0.855 at 20 qubits, and 0.624 at 25 qubits, degrading to 0.039 at 30 qubits as circuit noise dominates, establishing an empirical NISQ scaling boundary. Results demonstrate that QAOA discovers subgroups competitive with classical heuristics and finds multi-feature interaction patterns that greedy Beam Search prunes, with QAOA-unique subgroups achieving up to 99.6% test precision. This work establishes a framework for quantum combinatorial optimization in cybersecurity and characterizes hardware scaling for NISQ devices.

</details>
