<a id="paper-2604.27080"></a>
### [High-fidelity iSWAP gate with Double Transmon Coupler](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27080v1)

**Authors:** Tarush Tiwari, Sudhir K. Sahu, Guilhem Ribeill, Michael Senatore, Matthew D. LaHaye, Raymond W. Simmonds, Daniel L. Campbell, Archana Kamal, Leonardo Ranzani  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27080v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5**

**Summary.** The researchers demonstrated a high-fidelity 99.827% iSWAP gate between two transmon qubits using a double transmon coupler. This method allows for fast, 40ns entangling operations while effectively suppressing unwanted static interactions and crosstalk. The approach provides a scalable pathway for high-fidelity quantum computing using superconducting circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of parametric gates to achieve frequency-selective activation and the suppression of crosstalk is highly relevant to the control of open quantum systems and the development of scalable quantum architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high-fidelity entangling gates between superconducting qubits while suppressing parasitic crosstalk and static interactions.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a parametric iSWAP gate with 99.827% fidelity in 40ns using a double transmon coupler (DTC).

**Method.** Implementation of a parametric gate using a DTC architecture and the use of robust phase estimation to calibrate non-commuting error terms.

**Model / system.** A superconducting circuit architecture consisting of two transmon qubits coupled via a recently developed double transmon coupler (DTC).

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity and gate duration.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate fidelity of 99.827% and a gate time of 40ns.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DTC architecture, describes the implementation of the parametric iSWAP gate, details the calibration via robust phase estimation, and presents the achieved fidelity results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entangling operations are at the heart of all approaches to quantum information processing. Parametric gates, in particular, offer a versatile solution to strongly couple off-resonant superconducting qubits with suppressed parasitic crosstalk to spectator qubits due to frequency-selective activation. In this work, we demonstrate a parametric iSWAP gate between two transmon qubits using the recently developed double transmon coupler (DTC). The DTC supports robust internally-defined cancellation point (``off'' state) for static interactions, while simultaneously mediating a fast parametric coupling between data qubits that can be deployed for high-fidelity two-qubit operations. We use robust phase estimation to calibrate non-commuting error terms in the parametric iSWAP gate, and achieve a 99.827% gate fidelity in 40ns without any numerical optimization. The circuit architecture and calibration techniques developed here are extensible to other gate implementations and qubit modalities, paving the way towards resource-efficient quantum information processing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27739"></a>
### [OAM-mode sorting with a wavefront twister](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27739v1)

**Authors:** Suman Karan, Swati Chaudhary, Harshwardhan Wanare, Anand Kumar Jha  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27739v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `interference shaping light` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27739_figures/2604.27739_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Conceptual illustration of the proposed wavefront twister and the OAM sorting scheme. (a) A conventional wavefront rotator such as a Dove prism, oriented at angle θ0 rotates an input wavefront by angle 2θ0. (b) The proposed wavefront twister rotates an incoming wavefront by an angle θ(ρ) = aρ that varies linearly with radial location ρ. Con- sequently, the wavefront is not simply rotated but twisted. (c) The wavefront twister maps the point (ρ, ϕ) in the inci- dent wavefront to (ρ, ϕ + aρ). (d) The proposed OAM sorter scheme consisting of the wavefront twister followed by a lens of focal length f maps the OAM modes l1 and l2 to annuli of distinct radii in the focal plane, leading to...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27739_figures/2604.27739_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

(b) Numerically computer one-dimensional intensity profiles |E(r, θ = 0, f = 200 mm)|2 as a function of r for OAM mode index l ranging from 1 to 20 at a/w0 = 1π and w0 = 0.1 cm.</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new optical element, a 'wavefront twister,' that rotates a wavefront by an amount varying with radial position. This device allows for the efficient sorting of OAM modes into distinct rings, providing a scalable way to reduce crosstalk in high-dimensional optical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic quantum information, as it proposes a scalable method for managing high-dimensional OAM states, which are essential for high-capacity quantum communication and multiplexing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of efficiently and unambiguously mapping different Orbital Angular Momentum (OAM) modes to distinct spatial locations for parallel detection and multiplexing while minimizing inter-modal crosstalk.

**Main result.** The proposed wavefront twister maps OAM modes to distinct annuli at the focal plane, where increasing the twisting strength significantly reduces inter-modal crosstalk and enables scalable mode sorting.

**Method.** The study uses mathematical modeling of the wavefront transformation and the Fresnel-Kirchhoff diffraction integral, employing numerical evaluation via Bessel function identities and confluent hypergeometric functions.

**Model / system.** The system consists of Laguerre-Gaussian (LG) beams passing through a novel optical element called a wavefront twister, which applies a radial-dependent rotation to the wavefront, followed by a convex lens.

**Key observables.** OAM mode index (l), mean radial position (mu), radial intensity spread (sigma), and inter-modal crosstalk percentage.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twisting strength (a/w0), radial position (rho), and beam waist (w0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scheme cannot distinguish between positive and negative OAM modes (+l and -l) as they produce identical intensity distributions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the concept of the wavefront twister and the sorting scheme; Figure 2 shows 2D and 1D intensity profiles; Figure 3 demonstrates reduced overlap at higher twisting strength; Figure 4 plots mean radial position and radial spread against the OAM index; Figure 5 shows crosstalk as a function of twisting strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the wavefront twister concept, compares it to existing sorters, provides the mathematical framework for the transformation and diffraction, presents numerical results regarding mode separation and crosstalk, and discusses scalability and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose an OAM sorter based on a novel optical element that we refer to as a wavefront twister. It is a generalization of the conventional wavefront rotators such as the Dove prism. However, unlike a Dove prism, which simply rotates a wavefront, the rotation generated by a wavefront twister varies linearly with radial position, resulting in the twisting of the wavefront. We demonstrate that the wavefront twister, followed by a lens, maps each OAM mode to an annulus of distinct radius at the back focal plane of the lens with negligible inter-modal overlap and preserves the circular symmetry. Thus, the proposed wavefront twister offers a scalable scheme for high-dimensional OAM mode sorting, with important consequences for the practical realization of OAM-based applications.

</details>

### [Resolving spurious topological entanglement entropy in stabilizer codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27053v1)

**Authors:** Peilun Han, Zijian Liang, Yifei Wang, Bowen Yang, Yingfei Gu, Yu-An Chen

**Summary.** This paper resolves the problem of artificial entropy contributions in the calculation of topological entanglement entropy for stabilizer codes. By replacing standard rectangular partitions with a specifically designed concave geometry, the authors prove that the genuine topological order can be accurately recovered. The work also demonstrates how entanglement entropy can reveal topological frustration in quantum LDPC codes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the issue of spurious topological entanglement entropy (sTEE) in stabilizer codes, where standard rectangular partitions produce artificial constant contributions that overestimate the total quantum dimension.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a concave partition geometry that eliminates spurious contributions and provide a rigorous proof that this prescription recovers the genuine topological entanglement entropy for translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits.

**Method.** The study uses algebraic techniques including Gröbner basis reduction, Dubé's theorem for degree bounds, and the Smith Normal Form to analyze stabilizer group sizes and the decomposition of boundary gauge operators.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on 2D translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits (Zp), specifically examining models like the toric code, shifted toric code, and bivariate bicycle codes on a cylinder.

**Key observables.** Topological entanglement entropy (TEE), bipartite entanglement entropy, and total quantum dimension.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stabilizer range (r), qudit dimension (q), system size (L), and cylinder circumference (l).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results are specifically derived for translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits; the derived analytic lower bound for the partition size is noted to be conservative.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares rectangular and concave partitions; Figure 2 and 3 illustrate stabilizer decomposition and the removal of residual support; Figure 4 shows bipartite entanglement entropy on a cylinder; Figure 5 shows the shifted toric code stabilizers; Figure 15 shows a schematic of stabilizer decomposition.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the origin of sTEE, introduces the concave partition, provides a rigorous mathematical proof for the absence of spurious terms using algebraic geometry, and applies the method to study topological frustration in bivariate bicycle codes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological entanglement entropy (TEE) is a key diagnostic of long-range entanglement in two-dimensional gapped phases of matter, but it can suffer from spurious contributions that overestimate the total quantum dimension of the underlying topological order. In this work, we identify the microscopic origin of spurious TEE and introduce a concave partition for computing the Levin-Wen TEE of translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits. We rigorously prove that this prescription is free of spurious contributions. As a complementary probe, we study bivariate bicycle codes on a bipartite cylinder and show that the entanglement entropy depends sensitively on the cylinder circumference, revealing topological frustration of the underlying anyons.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28042"></a>
### [Deep Strong light-matter Coupling in 3D Kane Fermions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28042v1)

**Authors:** Dmitriy Yavorskiy, David Hagenmuller, Noureddine Charrouj, Yurii Ivonyak, Alexander Kazakov, Yanko Todorov, Wojciech Knap, Marcin Bialek  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28042v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Dicke superradiance` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5**

**Summary.** The paper reports the achievement of deep-strong light-matter coupling in 3D Kane fermions using a mercury cadmium telluride layer in a Fabry-Perot cavity. It proves that a diamagnetic A^2 term emerges to prevent the predicted superradiant phase transition. This work settles a major controversy in cavity QED and provides a platform for extreme light-matter interaction devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** It resolves a fundamental debate in cavity QED regarding the possibility of superradiant phase transitions in systems with relativistic-like dispersion and demonstrates record-high light-matter coupling in a semiconductor platform.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses whether a superradiant quantum phase transition is possible in relativistic-like electronic systems (Dirac and Kane fermions) where the absence of an A^2 term was previously hypothesized.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that a diamagnetic A^2 term naturally emerges in Kane fermions, which precludes the superradiant phase transition, thereby resolving a long-standing controversy.

**Method.** The study combines experimental realization of Landau polaritons in a bulk semiconductor with a rigorous, gauge-invariant microscopic theory to analyze the coupling regimes.

**Model / system.** A bulk mercury cadmium telluride (HgCdTe) layer containing 3D Kane fermions coupled to a Fabry-Perot resonator.

**Key observables.** Polariton spectra and the normalized coupling ratio.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Deep-strong coupling regime (normalized coupling ratio exceeding 1.6) and thermally tunable carrier density.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the deep-strong coupling regime, describes the experimental setup using HgCdTe and a Fabry-Perot resonator, presents the measured polariton spectra, compares them to gauge-invariant theory, and concludes by discussing the emergence of the A^2 term and its implications for phase transitions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Deep strong light-matter coupling represents an extreme non-perturbative regime of quantum electrodynamics, in which the interaction strength exceeds the bare frequencies of the uncoupled systems. The ground state features strong quantum correlations between photons and matter excitations, and new cavity-driven phase transitions are expected to occur. Whether a superradiant quantum phase transition, marked by spontaneous dipole ordering and photon condensation, is possible has remained a long-standing and controversial question. Such phenomena have been proposed to arise in exotic electronic systems hosting Dirac and Kane fermions, owing to the formal absence of an $A^2$ term in their low-energy Hamiltonian. Here we exploit the ultralow effective mass of Kane fermions to realise Landau polaritons in a bulk mercury cadmium telluride layer coupled to a Fabry-Perot resonator. Using thermally tunable carrier density, we continuously tune the coupling from the weak to the deep-strong regime, achieving a record normalised coupling ratio exceeding 1.6 above room temperature. The measured polariton spectra are in excellent agreement with a rigorous, gauge-invariant microscopic theory. Despite the nonlinear Landau level structure of relativistic Kane fermions, we show that a diamagnetic $A^2$ term naturally emerges and precludes a superradiant phase transition. These results resolve the long-standing controversy surrounding cavity quantum electrodynamics of relativistic-like matter systems, extend deep-strong-coupling physics to Kane fermions, and open new opportunities for polaritonic semiconductor devices operating in extreme light-matter coupling regimes.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper identifies 'synergistic interactions' as a new way to generate extended critical regimes and slow, Griffiths-like dynamics. By coupling different spreading mechanisms, the system develops a broad spectrum of relaxation rates. This effect is shown to be a general dynamical principle that works across various network structures and interaction types.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is relevant for many-body dynamics and open quantum systems as it identifies a dynamical mechanism for generating broad relaxation spectra and extended critical regimes, which could be applied to understanding non-equilibrium phase transitions in driven-dissipative systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether synergistic interactions (cooperative mechanisms) can serve as a distinct route to non-conventional critical phenomena, specifically 'stretched criticality' characterized by extended regimes of slow, algebraic-like decay.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that synergistic interactions create a broad distribution of relaxation rates, leading to Griffiths-like slow dynamics and an extended critical region that is robust across different network topologies and interaction types.

**Method.** The study uses spectral analysis of the diffusion density operator (including von Neumann entropy and spectral specific heat) and numerical simulations of SIS-type processes on various networks.

**Model / system.** The primary models are a simplicial Susceptible-Infected-Susceptible (SIS) process on a 2D triangular lattice and a pairwise quadratic contact process with threshold activation, applied to Erdős–Rényi, scientific collaboration, and US airport networks.

**Key observables.** Infection density rho(t), relaxation rates, von Neumann entropy S(tau), spectral specific heat C(tau), and integrated spectral excess Phi.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cooperative fraction p (density of active 2-simplices), synergistic strength (ratio of beta_delta to beta), and infection/recovery rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mechanism is assumed to be independent of specific geometry, though finite-size effects in heterogeneous networks like the US airport network may limit perfectly clean power-law behavior.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows the temporal evolution of infection density highlighting the extended slow relaxation regime; Figure 3 demonstrates the robustness of this effect across different network topologies; Supplemental figures show how varying p and beta_delta/beta affects the presence of stretched criticality.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of stretched criticality, presents the simplicial and pairwise models, details the spectral analysis method, demonstrates the emergence of slow dynamics through synergy, tests robustness across networks, and concludes with the conditions (balance of strength and density) required for the phenomenon.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Griffiths phases are typically associated with quenched disorder, while frustration gives rise to multistability and spin-glass behavior. Whether extended criticality can arise in other contexts remains an open question. Here, we show that synergistic interactions provide a distinct route to non-conventional critical phenomena. By combining spreading mechanisms that reinforce activity through complementary pathways, we uncover a broad distribution of relaxation rates, leading to Griffiths-like slow dynamics and extended criticality. We demonstrate that this mechanism is robust across networks and emerges both in systems with explicit higher-order interactions and in purely pairwise systems with nonlinear dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27858"></a>
### [Geometric complexity in thermodynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27858v1)

**Authors:** Tan Van Vu, Keiji Saito  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27858v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27858_figures/2604.27858_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Geometric framework for reset maps. (a) A state-agnostic reset map T clearing probability from a target subspace. This unified description encompasses diverse ther- modynamic operations, including cooling, information era- sure, and state copying. (b) The geometric complexity of T is defined as the minimal geodesic length between the iden- tity map 1 and T on the space of physical maps, as induced by a suitable Riemannian metric.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a geometric measure of complexity to establish a universal principle for the unattainability of perfect state resets. It proves that as the error in operations like cooling or erasure approaches zero, the required physical complexity diverges. This result provides a unified thermodynamic limit that applies equally to classical stochastic processes and quantum channels.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper provides a deep geometric unification of the third law of thermodynamics and quantum information theory, offering a new way to quantify the physical resources (time, energy, bandwidth) required for quantum control and state preparation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The authors seek a universal, dynamics-independent measure to quantify the fundamental difficulty of implementing state-reset operations, such as cooling or erasure, which currently lack a unified description across classical and quantum regimes.

**Main result.** They derive a fundamental complexity-error trade-off relation, e^C * epsilon >= 1, proving that achieving zero error in a reset operation requires infinite geometric complexity, effectively reframing the third law of thermodynamics as a geometric limit.

**Method.** The study employs a Riemannian manifold approach, defining complexity as the minimal geodesic length between the identity map and a target map using a specifically constructed log-barrier Hessian metric.

**Model / system.** The framework encompasses both classical stochastic maps (transition matrices) and quantum channels (CPTP maps), including models like Markov jump processes, Lindbladian dynamics, and unitary swaps with an environment.

**Key observables.** Geometric complexity (C), execution error (epsilon), geodesic distance (l), and Shannon/von Neumann entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimension (d), transition rate bounds (gamma), and the metric parameter (alpha=2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes smooth paths of maps exist, transition rates are bounded, and focuses on the regime where probability distributions remain strictly positive.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1(a) illustrates a state-agnostic reset map clearing probability from a target subspace, while Figure 1(b) defines geometric complexity as the minimal geodesic length on a manifold of maps.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the need for a universal complexity measure, moves to defining the Riemannian geometric framework, proves the complexity-error trade-off for both classical and quantum systems, provides analytical bounds and entropic relations, and concludes with specific applications to reset channels and protocol scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The third law of thermodynamics forbids cooling a physical system to absolute zero in a finite number of operational steps. Although this unattainability principle has been quantified for specific state-to-state transitions, a universal, dynamics-independent bound for implementing a state-agnostic reset map remains elusive. In this work, we unveil the fundamental limits of physical map implementation by deriving a trade-off relation based on geometric complexity. By analyzing continuous paths of maps on a geometric manifold, we prove that the geometric complexity of any classical stochastic map or quantum channel is bounded from below by its execution error. As a consequence, we show that achieving zero error in a state-reset operation requires a divergent geometric complexity -- a unified measure that naturally incorporates disparate physical resources, including infinite time, energetic cost, or control bandwidth. This unattainability principle holds universally across both classical and quantum regimes, establishing a strict geometric limit on the physical realization of reset operations in thermodynamic control and quantum computation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27838"></a>
### [Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning without short-time control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27838v1)

**Authors:** Myeongjin Shin, Junseo Lee, Changhun Oh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27838v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27838_figures/2604.27838_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Comparison between previous short-time control approaches and our framework. Previous Heisenberg- limited algorithms rely on stroboscopic control and Trotterization, requiring many ultra-short evolution segments with small 𝑡min. Our framework replaces these short-time queries by long-time evolutions interleaved with learned auxiliary operations, ensuring that every query to the unknown Hamiltonian has duration at least 𝑇. Thus, the short-time bottleneck is removed while Heisenberg-limited scaling in total evolution time is retained.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27838_figures/2604.27838_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Schematic overview of our iterative learning protocol. At iteration 𝑗, the current estimate 𝐻𝑗 defines a residual Hamiltonian Δ𝐻𝑗= 𝐻−𝐻𝑗. We first learn an auxiliary Hamiltonian 𝑊𝑗using only long-time evolutions under 𝐻, and then use 𝑊𝑗to emulate the residual-control primitive 𝑒−𝑖Δ𝐻𝑗𝑡. This emulated evolution reduces coefficient extraction to sparse pure-state tomography. After estimating Δ𝐻𝑗, we update and truncate the estimate to obtain 𝐻𝑗+1, preserving 𝑚-sparsity and bounded norm while halving the error.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that optimal, Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning is possible without needing ultra-short control pulses. By using a method to emulate short-time dynamics through longer evolution periods, the authors overcome experimental limitations related to finite control bandwidth.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for experimentalists in quantum computing and many-body dynamics because it proves that high-bandwidth, ultra-short pulses are not a fundamental requirement for optimal Hamiltonian characterization.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to achieve Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning when experimental constraints prevent the use of arbitrarily short-time evolution pulses.

**Main result.** The authors prove that Heisenberg-limited scaling (1/epsilon) can be achieved even with a minimum evolution time T > 0, resolving a major open problem. They establish a quantitative tradeoff between the minimum evolution time and total evolution time for many-body systems.

**Method.** The framework emulates continuous quantum control by replacing short-time Trotter steps with long-time evolutions interleaved with learned correction unitaries, reducing the task to sparse pure-state tomography.

**Model / system.** An n-qubit, m-sparse, traceless Hamiltonian composed of a sum of Pauli operators with unknown coefficients.

**Key observables.** Pauli coefficients of the Hamiltonian, total evolution time, and error in the operator norm.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sparsity (logarithmic vs. polynomial), minimum evolution time (T), target accuracy (epsilon), and total evolution time (t_tot).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The learner has oracle access to the time-evolution operator for durations at least T and can simulate the current Hamiltonian estimate.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 contrasts previous short-time control approaches (relying on vanishingly small time steps) with the new framework using long-time evolutions and auxiliary operations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the short-time bottleneck problem, defines the long-time evolution framework, presents the emulation method for continuous control, analyzes the logarithmic sparsity regime, extends the results to polynomial sparsity via quasi-sparsity and BCH expansion, and concludes with complexity bounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Characterizing quantum systems by learning their underlying Hamiltonians is a central task in quantum information science. While recent algorithmic advances have achieved near-optimal efficiency in this task, they critically rely on accessing arbitrarily short-time dynamics. This reliance poses severe experimental challenges due to finite control bandwidth and transient pulse errors. In this work, we demonstrate that Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning can be achieved without short-time control. We introduce a framework in which every query to the unknown dynamics has duration at least a prescribed minimum time $T$, and show that this restriction does not preclude Heisenberg-limited scaling. The key ingredient is a method for emulating the continuous quantum control required by iterative learning algorithms using only such lower-bounded evolution times. This reduces the learning task to sparse pure-state tomography. Notably, for logarithmically sparse Hamiltonians, our algorithm achieves the information-theoretically optimal $1/\varepsilon$ scaling in total evolution time for any arbitrary constant minimum evolution time $T$. For many-body (polynomially sparse) systems, we uncover a rigorous quantitative tradeoff, showing that the minimum required evolution time can be significantly relaxed from the standard limit at a polynomial cost in total evolution time. Our results affirmatively resolve a prominent open problem in the field and reveal that high-bandwidth, ultra-short pulses are not fundamentally necessary for optimal quantum learning.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28009"></a>
### [Learning quantum disentanglement scheduling from reduced states via modular hybrid policies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28009v1)

**Authors:** Y. -X. Xiao, J. -Z. Han, Z. Zheng, Z. -H. Zhang, M. Xue, J. Li, X. Lv  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28009v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes a hybrid quantum-classical machine learning approach to control the disentanglement of multiple qubits using only partial information from reduced density matrices. It demonstrates that while quantum circuits provide useful compact representations, the success of the control policy depends heavily on classical preprocessing. The findings offer design principles for optimizing hybrid controllers in resource-constrained quantum environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it addresses the practical challenge of controlling complex quantum states using only local, partial measurements, which is a realistic scenario for near-term hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient quantum control strategy for multiqubit disentanglement when only partial information (two-qubit reduced density matrices) is available.

**Main result.** The study identifies that classical preprocessing is the primary driver of performance, while the quantum module acts as a compact latent representation; it also finds that increasing circuit width is more effective than increasing depth for policy efficiency.

**Method.** A modular hybrid quantum-classical policy framework comprising classical preprocessing, a parameterized quantum circuit (PQC) acting as a nonlinear latent block, and classical postprocessing for pair-selection probabilities.

**Model / system.** A multiqubit system (benchmarked on 4, 5, and 6-qubit tasks) undergoing a disentanglement scheduling process based on observations of two-qubit reduced density matrices.

**Key observables.** Two-qubit reduced density matrices and pair-selection probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit width, circuit depth, and model budget.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The controller has restricted access to the state, receiving only partial information via reduced density matrices rather than the full wave-function.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of partial-observation control, describes the modular hybrid policy architecture, presents benchmarking results across different qubit numbers, and analyzes the performance-efficiency trade-offs and architectural design principles.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum control with restricted state access is central to near-term quantum devices, where full wave-function information is unavailable. We study this problem through multiqubit disentanglement scheduling from partial observations, where a controller receives only two-qubit reduced density matrices and selects which qubit pair to disentangle at each step. We introduce a modular hybrid quantum--classical policy framework consisting of classical preprocessing, a parameterized quantum circuit as a compact nonlinear latent block, and classical postprocessing for pair-selection probabilities. Benchmarking 4-, 5-, and 6-qubit tasks, we find that preprocessing is the dominant factor governing performance under reduced-state observations, while the quantum module provides a conditional compact representation whose utility depends on the input features and model budget. We further identify a performance--efficiency trade-off across policy families and find that increasing circuit width is generally more useful than increasing depth. These results provide practical design principles for hybrid policies in reduced-information quantum control.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27220"></a>
### [Multirate characterization of relaxation mechanisms for two nonequivalent nuclear spins 1/2 in a liquid using maximally entangled pseudo-pure quantum states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27220v1)

**Authors:** Georgiy Baroncha, Alexander Perepukhov, Boris V. Fine  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27220v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper introduces a method to characterize eight different relaxation rates in a two-spin NMR system using entangled Bell pseudo-pure states. It identifies an unconventional relaxation mechanism from distant spins and discovers a universal ratio for diagonal relaxation rates. This approach provides a powerful tool for fingerprinting complex molecular dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems as it demonstrates the ability to selectively initialize and characterize specific eigenmodes of relaxation in a multi-spin density matrix using entanglement.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize multiple relaxation mechanisms in a two-spin system by accessing various relaxation rates of the density matrix.

**Main result.** The authors successfully accessed 8 relaxation rates, demonstrated that off-diagonal relaxation eigenmodes can be selectively initialized using Bell pseudo-pure states, and identified a universal dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates.

**Method.** The researchers used a multirate NMR approach, employing both conventional inversion recovery/NOE measurements and a new method based on a detuned Hartmann-Hahn double resonance condition to create maximally entangled Bell pseudo-pure states (Bell PPSs).

**Model / system.** A system of two non-equivalent nuclear spins 1/2 ($^1$H and $^{13}$C) belonging to a molecule in a liquid, which is stable with respect to chemical exchange.

**Key observables.** 8 relaxation rates describing the density matrix, including diagonal and off-diagonal relaxation rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates determined by intra-pair magnetic dipolar interaction.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nuclear spin pair is assumed to be stable with respect to chemical exchange.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an experimental and theoretical investigation involving the creation of Bell PPSs, the measurement of various relaxation rates, the presentation of a microscopic theory, and the validation of the theory through parameter-free tests.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multirate characterization of spin responses in nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) is a promising approach to fingerprinting complex molecules in the presence of multiple relaxation mechanisms. Here we present experimental and theoretical investigations simultaneously accessing 8 relaxation rates describing the density matrix of two adjacent non-equivalent nuclear spins 1/2 ($^1$H and $^{\ 13}$C) belonging to a molecule in a liquid. The selected nuclear pair is stable with respect to chemical exchange. Some of the rates are obtained from conventional measurements of inversion recovery and nuclear Overhauser effect, while other, less conventional ones, are extracted from the relaxation initialized by the maximally entangled pseudo-pure Bell states (Bell PPSs) of the spin pair. The Bell PPSs are created using a hereby introduced method based on a detuned Hartmann-Hahn double resonance condition. Microscopic theory behind the measured relaxation rates is presented, and its consistency is demonstrated by several parameter-free tests. In particular, it is shown both theoretically and experimentally, that the eigenmodes of the off-diagonal relaxation of the two-spin density matrix can be selectively initialized using Bell PPSs. Our multirate analysis suggests that the measured off-diagonal relaxation is partly due to an unconventional mechanism arising from very weak $J$-couplings of the spin pair with fluctuating distant nuclear spins. Furthermore, we identify a dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates, which is determined exclusively by intra-pair magnetic dipolar interaction and hence possesses a universal value for a broad class of nuclear spin pairs. This value is consistent with both our experiments and other experiments reported in the literature.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27809"></a>
### [Bosonic Josephson junction dynamics: interplay between quantum and thermal fluctuations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27809v1)

**Authors:** Andrea Bardin, Francesco Lorenzi, Luca Salasnich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27809v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper extends the mean-field description of Bosonic Josephson junctions by incorporating both quantum and thermal fluctuations. It demonstrates that these two types of fluctuations act in opposition on key dynamical properties. The authors show that in current experimental setups, quantum fluctuations are the dominant correction to the mean-field dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a precise way to predict how temperature and quantum noise compete in many-body dynamics, which is crucial for controlling coherence in ultracold atom experiments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how thermal and quantum fluctuations affect the superfluid dynamics of a Bosonic Josephson junction beyond the mean-field approximation.

**Main result.** The study finds that quantum and thermal fluctuations play opposite roles: thermal fluctuations decrease the Josephson frequency, while both increase the thresholds for macroscopic quantum self-trapping and spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Method.** A semianalytical approach using a formalism that accounts for fluctuations in a homogeneous gas to derive a corrected equation of motion within a two-site approximation.

**Model / system.** A Bosonic Josephson junction modeled within a two-site approximation, considering the dynamics of a superfluid gas.

**Key observables.** Josephson frequency, strength of macroscopic quantum self-trapping, and the threshold for spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Regimes where quantum fluctuations dominate over thermal fluctuations, assessed via parameters from recent experimental realizations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transport of the non-condensed component is assumed to be negligible.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of fluctuations in Josephson junctions, presents a corrected equation of motion, analyzes the effects of quantum vs. thermal fluctuations on dynamical quantities, and concludes by comparing results to experimental regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the superfluid dynamics of a Josephson junction beyond the mean-field description, incorporating the role of thermal fluctuations as well as quantum fluctuations. Using a formalism that accounts for the fluctuations in a homogeneous gas, and under the assumption that the transport of the non-condensed component is negligible, we derive a corrected equation of motion within the two-site approximation. The resulting corrections for the typical dynamical quantities, like the Josephson frequency, the strength of macroscopic quantum self-trapping, and the threshold for spontaneous symmetry breaking, allow us to predict the effects of both types of fluctuations and assess their relative importance in different regimes in a semianalytical fashion. For all the dynamical quantities, the quantum fluctuations are shown to play an opposite role with respect to the thermal fluctuations. Josephson frequency is decreased by thermal fluctuations and both the critical strenghts of macroscopic quantum self trapping and spontaneous symmetry breaking are increased. We assess the experimentally accessible regimes by calculating the relevant parameters of recent experimental realizations of Bosonic Josephson junction and show that the expected regime is dominated by quantum fluctuations.

</details>

### [Cylindrical Matter: A beyond-quantum many-body system for efficient classical simulation of quantum pure-Ising like systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27248v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces 'cylindrical matter,' a beyond-quantum many-body model that allows for the efficient classical simulation of specific quantum Ising-like systems. By using a state space that extends beyond the Bloch sphere, the authors find a way to sample measurement outcomes of previously hard-to-simulate quantum states. The work establishes precise conditions on interaction decay and dimensionality for this classical simulation to remain efficient.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to understand the limits of classical simulation by using non-quantum models to mimic complex quantum entanglement, which is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and the study of computational complexity in quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the boundaries of classical simulability for quantum many-body systems by exploring whether 'beyond-quantum' descriptions can provide efficient simulation advantages.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a hypothetical 'cylindrical matter' model can efficiently classically simulate certain previously unknown quantum pure-Ising-like systems with power-law decaying interactions.

**Method.** The study uses a framework of 'cylindrical bits' and 'generalized separability' to analyze the growth of state space radii under diagonal unitaries, employing analytical proofs and numerical linear programming.

**Model / system.** A continuous-time many-body system of 'cylindrical bits' on a lattice, where each particle's state space is a cylinder in the Bloch vector space, subject to diagonal two-body interactions and adaptive measurements.

**Key observables.** Measurement outcomes in the Z eigenbasis and the XY plane (aX + bY).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cylindrical radius (r), interaction decay exponent (alpha), spatial dimension (D), and the growth factor (lambda) required to maintain separability.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model is 'non-free' due to required spatial constraints; measurements are primarily destructive or quasi-destructive; and the simulation assumes a finite gate set to ensure bounded fan-in.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 sketches the cylindrical state space protruding from the Bloch sphere; Figure 2 plots the growth factor as a function of gate phase; Figure 3 shows the tradeoff between the initial state angle and the power-law decay rate.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the cylindrical bit model, defines the mathematical framework for generalized separability, analyzes the radial growth required for diagonal gates, establishes simulation thresholds for power-law interactions, and compares the optimality of cylindrical state spaces against alternatives.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Even simplified models of quantum many-body systems can be difficult to analyse. However, taking inspiration from the foundations of physics, one may wonder whether there are practical advantages to constructing alternative beyond-quantum descriptions of many-body systems. We explore this question in the context of quantum interactions that are diagonal in the computational basis. We construct a hypothetical model of a continuous time dynamical many-body system that is based upon lattices of interacting particles called "cylindrical bits", a concept first introduced in [6]. In the language of [5] our toy model is {\it non-free}, as we need spatial constraints on how the particles interact to ensure valid probabilities. We investigate these constraints and explore the resulting `entangled' states that can exist. Certain pure {\it quantum} entangled systems can be faithfully mimicked by our cylindrical worlds. This allows us to simulate efficiently classically, in the sense of sampling measurement outcomes, a variety of previously unknown quantum systems. Examples include some states created by pure Ising interactions algebraically decaying faster than $\sim 1/r^{3D/2}$, with spatial dimension $D$, under measurements in the $Z$ eigenbasis or eigenbases of $aX+bY$ for $a,b \in \mathbb{R}$. We also explore whether another choice of non-quantum `particle' could expand the applicability of the classical simulation by defining and partially optimising a figure-of-merit that attempts to capture how useful various possibilities may be.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28187"></a>
### [Enhancement of superconducting stiffness in hybrid superconducting-metallic bilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28187v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that doping a superconducting-metallic bilayer away from half-filling enhances superconducting correlations by breaking the competition with density-density correlations. It identifies distinct regimes of superconductivity controlled by pairing amplitude and phase stiffness. The findings also provide a theoretical link to understanding magnetic ordering in heavy-fermion Kondo lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides deep insights into how engineered coupling between different quantum subsystems can manipulate many-body correlations and phase stiffness, which is highly relevant to the control of correlations in ultracold atomic gases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how a metallic reservoir can enhance superconducting correlations in a paired subsystem, specifically addressing whether doping the system away from half-filling can overcome the suppression of long-range order caused by a charge gap.

**Main result.** Doping away from half-filling breaks the degeneracy between superconducting and density-density correlations in favor of superconductivity, allowing for near-long-range order and revealing a transition between pairing-limited and stiffness-limited regimes.

**Method.** The authors employ modern many-body numerical techniques, specifically Density Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) and Auxiliary-Field Quantum Monte Carlo (AFQMC), alongside analytical Conformal Field Theory (CFT) approaches.

**Model / system.** A 1D hybrid bilayer model consisting of a pairing subsystem (P) with negative-U sites coupled via tunneling to a metallic subsystem (M). The system can be mapped to a 1D Anderson or Kondo lattice via particle-hole transformation.

**Key observables.** S-wave pair-pair correlations, density-density correlations, single-particle correlations, Luttinger liquid parameters (Kp-1 and Km-1), and superconducting susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U), inter-subsystem tunneling (t-perp), metallic kinetic energy (tm), and electron densities (np, nm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on 1D systems; extensions to 2D and 3D rely on theoretical scaling arguments and known physics of heavy-fermion materials.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the two-chain setup of the P-subsystem and M-subsystem and the mapping to the Anderson/Kondo lattice.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Kivelson bilayer proposal, defines the 1D Hamiltonian and its mapping to magnetic models, presents numerical results for various doping and coupling regimes, and discusses the implications for heavy-fermion materials.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Boosting superconductivity by metallic reservoirs is the essence of Kivelson's bilayer proposal.   One layer provides pairing to the electrons, while the weakly coupled metal provides additional phase coherence to those pairs by mediating extended-range pair-pair coupling.   Demonstrating significant and unambiguous performance gains with strong-coupling methods for such set-ups had been difficult.   In the present work, we study these systems doped away from half-filling, corresponding to a partially spin-polarized 1D Anderson- or Kondo-lattice.   We show that this breaks the coexistence of dominant superconducting and density-density correlations decisively in favour or the former.   Consequently, we provide evidence that in this doped regime, superconducting near-long-range order is not precluded by a small charge-gap in the thermodynamic limit, as we have recently shown to be the case at half-filling [JE Ebot $et$ $al.$, arXiv:2602.11153 [cond-mat.supr-con]].   We study the complex manner in which the enhancement of superconductivity in the pairing layer depends on the parameters of the metal, and especially that both pairing-limited and stiffness-limited regimes may appear in these systems.   In addition to superconducting bilayers, our results are relevant, via a particle-hole transformation, for heavy-fermion Kondo-lattice materials in magnetic fields, as we provide previously lacking insight on the competition between antiferromagnetic and easy-plane magnetism, as well as a route for comprehensive indirect tests of Kivelson's bilayer proposal well beyond previous capabilities.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27130"></a>
### [Optimised Inference of Quantum Phenomena in High-Energy Collider Experiments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27130v1)

**Authors:** Hai-Chau Nguyen, Gilberto Tetlalmatzi-Xolocotzi, Carmen Diez Pardos, Otfried Gühne, Matthias Kleinmann  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27130v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new framework using classical shadow tomography to detect spin entanglement in high-energy particle collisions. By bypassing the need for full state reconstruction, the method enables the efficient analysis of spin correlations in relativistic regimes like the LHC. It also provides a way to verify the accuracy of the experimental measurement models used in particle physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper applies advanced quantum information techniques, such as shadow tomography and entanglement witnesses, to a completely different physical regime (high-energy physics), demonstrating the universality of these information-theoretic tools.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Characterizing spin-spin entanglement and correlations in high-energy collider experiments, where relativistic effects and uncontrolled particle momenta make direct spin state reconstruction difficult.

**Main result.** The development of a generalized shadow tomography framework that allows for the direct estimation of momentum-averaged spin observables and entanglement witnesses without full state reconstruction.

**Method.** The authors adapt classical shadow tomography to high-energy physics, converting observed decay product directions into single-event spin operators to estimate expectation values and detect entanglement.

**Model / system.** The system consists of relativistic particle pairs, specifically top-antitop (t-tbar) production at the LHC, modeled using a momentum-dependent spin density operator and a POVM based on decay product directions.

**Key observables.** Spin-spin correlations, entanglement witnesses (W1, W2), spin-correlation matrices, and momentum-averaged expectation values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relativistic velocity (beta), scattering angle (theta), top quark mass, and the production ratio of gluon fusion to quark-antiquark channels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measurement model is initially assumed to be known, and the analysis relies on the rotational invariance of the underlying physical evolution operator.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows entanglement detection across different velocity bins; Figure 2 displays a spin correlation matrix; Figure 3 maps kinematic regions where entanglement witnesses are negative; Figure 4 shows a histogram of top quark momentum.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of relativistic spin analysis, develops a generalized shadow tomography framework for POVMs, demonstrates its application to top quark pairs at the LHC, and provides a method for testing the validity of the measurement model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement, a fundamental phenomenon of quantum theory, has recently been observed in processes in high-energy physics. This opens new avenues for probing quantum effects in relativistic regimes, but also poses conceptual and technical challenges. We develop a general framework based on shadow tomography techniques for characterising spin-spin correlations in collider experiments. This improves the analysis of spin-spin entanglement, where relativistic motion couples spin and momentum and the momenta of the investigated particles are not under experimental control. As a proof of concept we illustrate the application of our formalism to top quark pair production at the Large Hadron Collider at CERN. The framework, however, is general and flexible and can be readily applied to more complex final states and systems with more particles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27173"></a>
### [Quantum Coordination without Conditioning under Restricted Information](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27173v1)

**Authors:** Faisal Shah Khan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27173v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27173_figures/2604.27173_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Tree representation of the binary illustrative example. The dashed line indicates that the two second-step nodes are indistinguishable under the available information, so the local rule generating X2 cannot depend on the realized value of X1.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that quantum correlations can enable coordination in multi-agent systems even when agents cannot access past information. It shows that both classical-like shared variables and quantum discord allow for the implementation of distributions that are impossible for classical agents under the same information constraints.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for researchers in quantum information and open systems as it explores how quantum resources like discord can compensate for the lack of classical memory or information in distributed protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether quantum correlations can implement joint distributions that are impossible for classical local models when agents are subject to restricted information (imperfect recall) and cannot condition actions on past history.

**Main result.** Quantum systems can achieve coordination where classical models fail, using either shared latent variables (diagonal states) or quantum discord (noncommuting local structures), effectively acting as a partial substitute for perfect recall.

**Method.** The study uses a probabilistic framework comparing classical local conditional rules against quantum measurement outcomes via the Born rule, utilizing Kuhn's Theorem to analyze the breakdown of classical equivalence.

**Model / system.** A multi-stage decision process involving multiple agents sharing multipartite quantum states, specifically including two-qubit separable states and states with non-zero quantum discord.

**Key observables.** Joint probability distributions of measurement outcomes and quantum discord.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Information variables (Y_k) representing available history, and the presence or absence of noncommuting local structures.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum model assumes no signaling or adaptive quantum memory across stages, and the information constraint is strictly maintained.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a tree representation of a binary decision process, using dashed lines to indicate nodes that are indistinguishable due to information constraints.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the problem of restricted information, formalizes the classical limitation, proves Theorem 1 regarding diagonal/latent-variable implementations, proves Theorem 2 regarding discord-based implementations, demonstrates scalability to multi-stage processes, and discusses fundamental limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study coordination under restricted information, where classical local models fail to implement certain correlated distributions because agents cannot condition on past history. We show that quantum systems overcome this limitation even when using only separable states. Both classically diagonal encodings (shared latent variables) and separable states with noncommuting local structure (quantum discord) enable the implementation of joint distributions that are unattainable by any classical local rules under the same information constraints. The quantum advantage arises from enabling latent-variable coordination without requiring agents to condition on the latent variable itself -- a construction that succeeds where no classical local model can. Separable states with nonzero quantum discord provide an alternative mechanism for realizing such coordination. At the same time, quantum models remain strictly limited by the information structure: unlike perfect recall, they cannot reproduce fully adaptive dependence on realized past outcomes that are observationally indistinguishable. Thus, quantum correlations serve as a partial substitute for perfect recall.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27125"></a>
### [Derivation of the Born Rule and Operational Quantum Formalism in the Accessibility Framework through Boundary Reduction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27125v1)

**Authors:** Everett Fall, Hironori Kondo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27125v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27125_figures/2604.27125_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27125_figures/2604.27125_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27125_figures/2604.27125_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27125_figures/2604.27125_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27125_figures/2604.27125_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new framework called Accessibility Theory to derive the fundamental laws of quantum mechanics and the Standard Model from a single principle of complexity balance. It shows that quantum features like the Born rule and Bell violations are the inevitable result of an observer's limited access to a deeper, deterministic algebraic reality. This provides a unified explanation for both particle physics and the operational structure of quantum mechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to researchers of open quantum systems as it provides a structural, algebraic derivation of non-Markovian dynamics and the emergence of non-classicality from a fundamentally deterministic, unitary process via coarse-graining.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to derive the operational quantum formalism (Born rule, state updating, interference, and non-Markovianity) and the Standard Model parameters from a fundamental algebraic selection principle rather than postulating them as empirical inputs.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the Born rule, Lüders rule, and Bell inequality violation at the Tsirelson bound emerge from an 'Aperture'—a codimension-one information bottleneck created by boundary reduction in a framework called Accessibility Theory. Furthermore, a 'Balance Principle' uniquely selects the Standard Model gauge group, 4D spacetime, and three fermion generations.

**Method.** The study employs Noncommutative Geometry (NCG), specifically real graded spectral triples, combined with Diophantine analysis and Gleason's theorem to derive quantum probabilities and algebraic structures.

**Model / system.** The model is based on Accessibility Theory (AT), which utilizes a 'Continuum' represented by a product spectral triple and an 'Aperture' interface that acts as a commutative bottleneck for an embedded observer.

**Key observables.** Aperture record process (sector labels), transition probabilities, and Bell inequality violation (CHSH expectation value).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Foundational accessibility (A_disc = 144), spacetime dimension (n=4), number of generations (N_gen=3), and the trace reduction ratio (xi = 1/4).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the Principle of Universal Accessibility Balance (minimization of complexity), noncontextuality of measurements, and the existence of a deterministic, unitary underlying ontology.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the Accessibility Theory framework and the Balance Principle to select the Standard Model algebra; it then applies boundary reduction to define the Aperture; subsequently, it derives the Born rule and quantum dynamics (interference, non-Markovianity) via the Aperture's information bottleneck; finally, it addresses the resolution of the measurement problem and the emergence of classicality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the operational quantum formalism -- the Born rule, Lüders state updating, quantum interference, non-Markovian effective dynamics, and Bell inequality violation at the Tsirelson bound \(2\sqrt{2}\) -- arises within Accessibility Theory (AT) from the Aperture construction together with explicit coherence and locality assumptions stated in the paper. AT is a framework built on real graded spectral triples and a single algebraic selection principle. The Principle of Universal Accessibility Balance requires three independent measures of the complexity of a spectral triple -- its algebraic, gauge-theoretic, and geometric content -- to be exactly equal and minimized, uniquely selecting the algebra \(\C \oplus \Hbb \oplus M_3(\C)\) and with it the Standard Model gauge group, particle content, four-dimensional Lorentzian spacetime, three generations, and gravitational dynamics. Restriction to a codimension-one geometric boundary reduces this algebra to its commutative center \(\C \oplus \C \oplus \C\) -- the Aperture -- which defines a permanent information bottleneck for any embedded observer. Coherence conditions on inference through this bottleneck, together with Gleason's theorem on the 48-dimensional internal Hilbert space, uniquely determine the Born rule; the remaining operational features follow from the same observer-level framework under the stated assumptions. At the ontological level the theory is deterministic and state-realist, while the operational quantum formalism appears at the observer level as a consequence of structurally limited access to the underlying algebraic reality.

</details>

### [Effective Noise Mitigation via Quantum Circuit Learning in Quantum Simulation of Integrable Spin Chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27648v1)

**Authors:** Wenlong Zhao, Yimeng Zhang, Yan Guo, Yufan Cui, Zhuohang Wang, Rui-Dong Zhu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27648v1>

**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

**Summary.** The authors propose using Quantum Circuit Learning to compress deep, noisy time-evolution circuits into shallower, more noise-resilient ones. By training the circuit to respect the conserved charges of integrable spin chains, the method effectively mitigates the impact of common hardware noise. This approach provides a way to perform more accurate quantum simulations without the high sampling overhead of traditional error mitigation techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work presents a physics-informed error mitigation strategy that uses the intrinsic symmetries of many-body systems to improve the fidelity of quantum simulations, which is highly relevant for studying many-body dynamics on near-term hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Mitigating hardware noise (decoherence and gate infidelities) in the quantum simulation of integrable spin chains on NISQ devices.

**Main result.** Quantum Circuit Learning (QCL) can produce shallower, more robust circuits that effectively suppress noise-induced decay of conserved quantities and dynamical observables.

**Method.** A machine learning framework that trains a shallow variational circuit to approximate a deeper, noisy time-evolution operator by leveraging conserved charges as a training signal.

**Model / system.** Integrable quantum spin chains, specifically the XXX Heisenberg model, studied for small system sizes (L=2 and L=3).

**Key observables.** Conserved charges (total magnetization Z_tot, X_tot, Y_tot, and Hamiltonian H) and dynamical local observables (e.g., <Z1>, <X1>, <Y1>).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depths (evolution depth d and learning depth D), noise probabilities (p), and the initial state parameter (x).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The strategy is optimized for integrable systems where conserved charges are available; the learning circuit depth D must be balanced to avoid accumulating too much noise.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare predictions of the learned circuit against true data, demonstrate the preservation of conserved charges under various noise models (bit-flip, depolarizing, amplitude, and phase damping), and show the scalability and reusability of the learned circuits.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the noise mitigation problem, describes the QCL method and the use of conserved charges for training, presents numerical results for L=2 and L=3 systems under various noise channels, and discusses scalability and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a noise-mitigation quantum simulation strategy for near-term quantum devices based on Quantum Circuit Learning (QCL), which is in particular effective for integrable quantum spin chains. The method trains a shallow variational circuit to approximate a deeper time-evolution circuit by learning the conserved charges and only a small amount of dynamical information in the system. Under realistic noise models, the learned circuit maintains both conserved quantities and dynamical observables significantly closer to their true values than the noisy simulation of the original circuit. This demonstrates QCL as an effective, physics-informed error mitigation strategy, producing shorter, more robust circuits without exponential sampling overhead.

</details>

### [Nodal algebraic curves and entropy diagnostics in degenerate two-dimensional harmonic-oscillator shells](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28127v1)

**Authors:** C A Escobar Ruiz, H Olivares-Pilon, A M Escobar-Ruiz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28127v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28127_figures/2604.28127_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Mutual information I(x; y) versus the mixing param- eter t in the N = 1 shell (a = √</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.28127_figures/2604.28127_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.28127_figures/2604.28127_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Nodal-domain entropy Sdom at α = 1 as a function of the mixing parameter t.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.28127_figures/2604.28127_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Mutual information I(x; y) versus t at α = 1 along the symmetric path a = c = p</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that the topological restructuring of nodal patterns in 2D harmonic oscillator shells is governed by algebraic properties of the wavefunction's polynomial component. It shows that while global entropies remain smooth, nodal-domain entropy can precisely detect topology-changing transitions. This provides a way to characterize complex quantum states in platforms like trapped ions and paraxial optics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum optics and AMO physics as it provides a framework for identifying topological transitions in structured light (Hermite-Gaussian modes) and the motional states of trapped ions using entropic signatures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the nodal geometry (zero-sets) of wavefunctions undergoes topological restructuring within degenerate 2D harmonic oscillator energy shells and how this is reflected in information-theoretic diagnostics.

**Main result.** The study finds that nodal geometry is organized by algebraic stratification (singularities and projective degeneracies) rather than spectral ordering, and that nodal-domain entropy is a highly sensitive diagnostic for these topology-changing events.

**Method.** The authors use algebraic geometry (singularity theory and discriminant analysis) combined with information theory (Shannon entropy and mutual information) to analyze the properties of real Hermite-constrained algebraic curves.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-dimensional isotropic quantum harmonic oscillator, specifically focusing on real-phase eigenstates within fixed energy shells N.

**Key observables.** Nodal-domain entropy (S_dom), Cartesian mutual information I(x;y), configuration-space Shannon entropy (S_r), momentum-space Shannon entropy (S_p), and the entropic uncertainty sum (S_r + S_p).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shell index N, mixing parameter t (interpolating between states), and the oscillator scale alpha.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to real-phase wavefunctions (no phase singularities/vortices) and fixed energy shells.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of nodal geometries (lines, conics, and cubics) and the corresponding peaks or changes in mutual information and nodal-domain entropy as the mixing parameter varies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of nodal restructuring, establishes a mathematical framework using algebraic geometry, analyzes specific low-order shells (N=1, 2, 3) to demonstrate hierarchy and sensitivity, and concludes with experimental implications for structured light and trapped ions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Degenerate quantum eigenspaces can support substantial changes in nodal geometry at fixed energy. We show that, for the two-dimensional isotropic harmonic oscillator, this restructuring is organized by the Hermite-constrained algebraic curve $P_N(x,y)=0$ appearing in every real shell state, $ψ_N=e^{-αr^2/2}P_N(x,y)$. Finite singularities, $P_N=\nabla P_N=0$, and projective degeneracies of the leading homogeneous part identify the strata where topology-changing events can occur. We combine these criteria with entropy diagnostics: the nodal-domain entropy $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$, Cartesian mutual information $I(x;y)$, and the entropic uncertainty sum $S_r+S_p$. The first three shells reveal a hierarchy: $N=1$ only rotates a nodal line; $N=2$ has a conic transition at $b^2=2ac$, sharply detected by $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$ but not by global entropies; and $N=3$ supports cubic close-branch regimes organized by the projective discriminant, with enhanced responses in $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$ and $I(x;y)$. Thus algebraic stratification, rather than spectral ordering, organizes nodal geometry inside a degenerate eigenspace, while entropy diagnostics quantify probability redistribution and correlation. The framework suggests experimentally reconstructible signatures for real-phase Hermite--Gaussian structured light and approximately isotropic trapped motional systems.

</details>
