### [Tuning of quantum nanoscaled friction within the Prandtl-Tomlinson model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27216v1)

**Authors:** Dai-Nam Le, Lilia M. Woods  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27216v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper explores how to control nanoscale friction by adjusting system parameters like corrugation and length ratios. It extends the understanding of friction beyond the stick-slip regime by incorporating quantum effects like Landau-Zener tunneling. These insights are crucial for controlling friction in future nanotechnological applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the mechanisms for controlling frictional force at both quantum and classical levels in nanoscale systems.

**Main result.** The study identifies that frictional dynamics can be tuned via corrugation and characteristic length ratio parameters, and uncovers motion types beyond the standard stick-slip regime, including the role of Landau-Zener tunneling.

**Method.** Theoretical investigation using the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to simulate and analyze frictional dynamics.

**Model / system.** A nanoparticle-chain system modeled within the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework, incorporating both classical and quantum mechanical effects.

**Key observables.** Frictional force, dynamical force behavior, and types of motion (e.g., stick-slip).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Corrugation, characteristic length ratio, and Landau-Zener tunneling probability.

**Paper structure.** The paper utilizes the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to systematically investigate control means of friction, explores various motion regimes, and analyzes the impact of Landau-Zener tunneling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nanoscaled friction is a fundamental tribological phenomenon with complex behavior of its dynamical force. Here, we utilize the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to investigate systematically the different means of control of the frictional force at the quantum and classical levels. It is found that the frictional dynamics can be controlled by the corrugation and characteristic length ratio parameters dependent upon properties of the nanoparticle-chain system. In addition to the stick-slip regime, other types of motion are uncovered, highlighting the richness of the frictional dynamics. The importance of Landau-Zener tunneling for the quantum motion is also analyzed. These findings provide valuable insights for interpreting experimental observations and controlling quantum frictional behavior in nanoscale systems.

</details>

### [Entanglement of multi-qubit quantum graph states and studies structural properties of tripartite graphs with quantum programming](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27829v1)

**Authors:** Kh. P. Gnatenko  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27829v1>

<img src="2604.27829_figures/2604.27829_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>

The results obtained using the AerSimulator which includes a readout error of the order 10−2, a Pauli-X error of 10−4, and a CNOT error of 10−2 are indicated by cross markers, while the continuous surface represents the corresponding analytical calculations.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a framework to map the structural properties of tripartite graphs onto the entanglement and correlation properties of multi-qubit states. It demonstrates that quantum programming can be used to study classical graph theory features like cycles and neighbor overlaps. The theoretical predictions are successfully validated using noisy quantum simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to establish a quantitative relationship between the structural properties of weighted tripartite graphs and the quantum properties (entanglement and correlators) of multi-qubit quantum graph states.

**Main result.** The authors derive analytical expressions linking quantum correlators to graph features like 4-cycles and common neighbors, and demonstrate that qubit entanglement depends on vertex degrees and edge weights.

**Method.** The study uses analytical derivations for entanglement distance and Pauli expectation values, complemented by quantum simulations on IBM AerSimulator with noise models to validate the theory.

**Model / system.** The system consists of multi-qubit quantum graph states representing weighted and directed tripartite graphs, constructed by applying rotation gates (Rxy, Rxz, Ryz) to an initial separable state.

**Key observables.** Entanglement distance (EED), quantum correlators (Pauli expectation values), vertex degrees, and graph metrics like 4-cycles and common neighbors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate rotation angles (phi, theta, alpha), edge weights, and vertex set partitions (U, V, W).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The graph is assumed to be tripartite (no edges within the same set), and specific cases assume unweighted graphs with uniform initial parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the quantum circuit protocol; Figures 2, 4, and 6 compare analytical entanglement distance surfaces with noisy simulation data; Figures 3, 5, and 7 show the absolute error between analytical and simulated results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the construction of tripartite quantum graph states, provides analytical derivations for entanglement and correlators, establishes the link to graph topology, and concludes with numerical validation via noisy quantum simulation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a method for constructing multi-qubit entangled quantum states representing weighted tripartite graphs. An expression for the entanglement distance for multi-qubit states corresponding to arbitrary tripartite graph structures is obtained. The entanglement of a qubit with the rest of the system in a quantum graph state is determined by the weights of the edges in the closed neighborhood of the corresponding vertex and by its degree with respect to other sets. We also calculate quantum correlators in the general case of tripartite quantum graph states. We establish a relationship between these quantum properties and the structural properties of the corresponding tripartite graphs, including the number of non-overlapping neighbors, the number of common neighbors of the corresponding vertices, and the number of 4-cycles. As an illustrative example, we consider a tripartite graph forming a triangle and compute the entanglement distance using quantum simulations on the AerSimulator with noise models. The numerical results are consistent with the theoretical predictions. The obtained results demonstrate that quantum graph states provide an effective framework for studying structural properties of tripartite graphs. They open up the possibility of investigating such properties using quantum programming. It is worth highlighting that tripartite graphs have applications in solving practical problems such as resource allocation, scheduling, and database and hypergraph modeling.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27440"></a>
### [Evidence for interior-gap pair-density-wave state in Kondo-Heisenberg chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27440v1)

**Authors:** Yuto Hirose, Shunsuke C. Furuya, Yasuhiro Tada  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27440v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that an interior-gap pair-density-wave state can emerge dynamically in 1D Kondo-Heisenberg chains through strong Kondo coupling. Unlike traditional models requiring Fermi-surface mismatch, here the low-energy single-particle structure develops alongside the superconducting correlations. This provides a new mechanism for realizing exotic interior-gap superconductivity in strongly correlated systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The realization of interior-gap superconductivity in canonical strongly correlated models has been elusive, as it is traditionally associated with pre-existing Fermi-surface mismatch.

**Main result.** The authors find that the superconducting phase of 1D Kondo-Heisenberg chains realizes an interior-gap pair-density-wave (PDW) state where the gap structure emerges dynamically from strong correlations.

**Method.** The study utilizes infinite density-matrix-renormalization-group (iDMRG) and finite DMRG calculations.

**Model / system.** One-dimensional Kondo-Heisenberg models with spin $S=1/2$ and $S=3/2$ chains.

**Key observables.** Pair-density-wave (PDW) correlation, momentum distribution function $n(k)$, and real-space correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin magnitude ($S=1/2, 3/2$), Kondo coupling, and the spin-gapped regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the 1D Kondo-Heisenberg model as a representative for studying the emergence of interior-gap physics.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents the motivation regarding interior-gap superconductivity, describes the DMRG methodology, details the results for $S=1/2$ and $S=3/2$ chains regarding PDW correlations and $n(k)$ reconstruction, and discusses the impact of boundary effects in finite systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Interior-gap superconductivity has long been discussed as an exotic paired state in the presence of Fermi-surface mismatch, but its realization in canonical strongly correlated models has remained elusive. Here we present evidence that the superconducting phase of one-dimensional Kondo-Heisenberg models realizes an interior-gap pair-density-wave (PDW) state generated by strong correlations. Combining infinite density-matrix-renormalization-group (iDMRG) and finite DMRG calculations for $S=1/2$ and $S=3/2$ chains, we show that the PDW correlation is the dominant bulk superconducting correlation in the spin-gapped regime and that the momentum distribution function $n(k)$ exhibits a reconstructed structure characteristic of interior-gap physics. In particular, while the feature in $n(k)$ for the $S=1/2$ chain is only hump-like, the corresponding structure in the $S=3/2$ chain develops into a clear dip, strongly supporting the interpretation in terms of an interior-gap-like dip structure. Unlike conventional interior-gap scenarios based on a mismatch between preexisting Fermi surfaces, the present system starts from a single bare conduction-electron Fermi surface, and the additional low-energy single-particle structure emerges dynamically together with the dominant PDW correlation through the Kondo coupling. Finite DMRG data further demonstrate that boundary effects can substantially modify real-space correlations in this gapless one-dimensional system, making a direct thermodynamic-limit calculation essential for identifying the intrinsic bulk momentum structure and the dominant correlation channel.

</details>

### [Momentum-Space Entanglement Signatures and Spinon Breakdown in the $J_1$-$J_2$ Zig-Zag Heisenberg Chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27048v1)

**Summary.** This paper uses momentum-space entanglement analysis to show that spinon quasiparticles in the zig-zag Heisenberg chain are much more resilient to inter-chain coupling than predicted by standard field theories. It identifies a specific mechanism for spinon breakdown driven by a shift in ground-state momentum. The findings provide a new tool for tracing quasiparticle stability in frustrated quantum magnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the stability and breakdown of spinon quasiparticles in the J1-J2 zig-zag Heisenberg chain, specifically addressing the discrepancy between field theory predictions and numerical evidence regarding the robustness of the decoupled-chain phase.

**Main result.** The study finds that a 'double-spinon' description remains robust over an extensive parameter regime, with an asymmetry in stability where ferromagnetic coupling sustains fractionalization into the spiral phase while antiferromagnetic coupling drives earlier confinement.

**Method.** The authors use Exact Diagonalization (ED) with the Lanczos algorithm and momentum-space entanglement spectroscopy (ES) to analyze the entanglement gap and spinon weight.

**Model / system.** A spin-1/2 J1-J2 zig-zag Heisenberg chain with nearest-neighbor (J1) and next-nearest-neighbor (J2) interactions, modeled as two coupled Heisenberg subchains in the highly frustrated regime.

**Key observables.** Entanglement Gap (EG), Spinon Weight (W), Total Crystal Momentum (Kc), and Haldane-Shastry ground-state fidelity (F).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Frustration ratio (alpha = J1/J2), ferromagnetic vs. antiferromagnetic coupling (J1 < 0 vs. J1 > 0), and the Majumdar-Ghosh point.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is limited by the numerical resolution of Exact Diagonalization and system sizes up to L=22.

**Figures summary.** Figure 6 shows the simultaneous maximization of various spinon deconfinement measures (W, Delta, F) in the spin-liquid regime; Figure 7 illustrates the spectral flow and collapse of the entanglement gap during the transition between different momentum characters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spinon stability, describes the J1-J2 model and the double-spinon framework, presents numerical results using entanglement spectroscopy to identify the breakdown of fractionalization, and concludes with a comparison to CFT/RG predictions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the resilience of spinon quasiparticles in the $J_1$-$J_2$ zig-zag spin chain ($J_2>0$) from the viewpoint of momentum-space entanglement. For small $J_2$, we show that deconfined spinons survive well past the liquid-dimer transition before eventually collapsing towards the Majumdar-Ghosh point. In the highly frustrated zig-zag regime ($J_2 \gg |J_1|$), we model the system as two coupled Heisenberg chains and by Fourier transforming each subchain individually, a framework we dub the double-spinon description. While continuum field theories predict that this decoupled phase is strictly unstable to any finite inter-chain coupling, our analysis reveals that the double-spinon description remains robust over an extensive parameter regime. Notably, we find a stark asymmetry in spinon stability reflecting the underlying renormalization group flow: ferromagnetic coupling ($J_{1} < 0$) is marginally irrelevant and sustains fractionalization deep into the spiral phase, whereas antiferromagnetic coupling ($J_{1} > 0$) is marginally relevant and drives confinement much earlier. The ultimate breakdown of this fractionalized description is driven by a continuum of inter-chain excitations which manifests itself as a sharp ground-state momentum shift distinct from macroscopic thermodynamic phase boundaries. Our results establish momentum cut entanglement analysis as a tool to trace the quasiparticle resilience of spinons, as we show that treating the zig-zag Heisenberg chain as two coupled SU(2)$_1$ Wess-Zumino-Witten models provides a theoretical framework for strongly frustrated quantum magnets applicable beyond the decoupled limit.

</details>

### [Nonadiabatic Renormalization Group for Strongly Coupled Multiscale Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27381v1)

**Authors:** Bing Gu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27381v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<sub>FIG. 3. Low-lying eigenstates of an strongly interacting bo- son model with 20 modes. The lowerst 16 eigenvalues are shown, with a rapid convergence with respect to the number of retained adiabatic states D.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27381_figures/2604.27381_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. NARG for ab initio quantum chemistry. Electronic correlation is gradually incorporated into the NARG by in- creasing the retained adiabatic states. More than 80% of the correlation energy in the hydrogen chain H8 is captured by D = 200.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new renormalization group method (NARG) for quantum systems with hierarchical energy scales. By iteratively suppressing high-energy degrees of freedom instead of tracing them out, it creates a new type of tensor network (LETTA) that captures entanglement through shared physical legs. The method proves highly efficient for both interacting boson models and electronic correlation in quantum chemistry.

**Why it may be interesting.** It introduces a novel tensor network architecture (LETTA) specifically designed for multiscale entanglement, offering a potential alternative to MPS for systems where energy scales are strongly coupled, such as molecular dynamics or open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently modeling complex, multiscale quantum systems where multiple energy or time scales are strongly coupled, specifically overcoming the exponential scaling of computational resources and the failure of standard tracing-out or product-basis approaches.

**Main result.** The development of the Nonadiabatic Renormalization Group (NARG) and the Leg-Tied Tensor Ansatz (LETTA), which successfully capture significant electron correlation in hydrogen chains and achieve rapid convergence in interacting boson models.

**Method.** An iterative approach called Nonadiabatic Renormalization Group (NARG) that suppresses rather than traces out high-energy degrees of freedom, resulting in a Leg-Tied Tensor Ansatz (LETTA) where physical legs are shared among sites to encode entanglement.

**Model / system.** The method is applied to an interacting boson model with multiple modes and anharmonicity, as well as ab initio quantum chemistry models such as the hydrogen chain (H8) involving interacting electrons.

**Key observables.** Low-lying many-body eigenstates, correlation energy, and quantum geometric properties like Berry curvature and the quantum geometric tensor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Energy/time scales (fast vs. slow), coupling regimes (strong vs. weak), and the number of retained adiabatic states (D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes exponential convergence of truncated states for practical utility and requires managing expansion coefficients and global overlap matrices across different configurations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares MPS to the new LETTA architecture; Figure 2 illustrates NARG as a nested fiber bundle; Figure 3 shows eigenvalue convergence in a boson model; Figure 4 shows ground state energy capture in the H8 chain.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the failure of standard methods in strong coupling, proposes the NARG framework and the resulting LETTA tensor network, demonstrates the mathematical structure of nested fiber bundles, and validates the method using boson and quantum chemistry models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Complex quantum systems are often multiscale in nature with strong interactions between different scales. We present a novel idea: iteratively suppressing, rather than tracing out, the fast, high-energy degrees of freedom in strongly correlated quantum systems with multiple energy scales in a non-perturbative way, termed nonadiabatic renormalization group. This leads to a quantum geometric structure of a nested fiber bundle, in which each fiber of a layer is itself a fiber bundle of the next layer. The nonadiabatic renormalization group brings a new type of tensor network states that shares physical legs among ''sites'' and encodes quantum entanglement beyond conventional matrix product states. We demonstrate how to apply the nonadiabatic renormalization group to different types of problems, including an interacting boson model and ab initio quantum chemistry with interacting electrons.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27459"></a>
### [Galilean boost invariance does not survive the trace: symmetry breaking in open quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27459v1)

**Authors:** Leonardo F. Calderón, Esteban Marulanda, Santiago Morales, Leonardo A. Pachón  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27459v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27459_figures/2604.27459_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Dimensionless boost-violation parameter ℏγ/kBT across representative open-quantum-system platforms in the high-temperature Ohmic regime. Markers indicate representa- tive values quoted in the text; horizontal bars span the experi- mental ranges of γ and T [17, 31–35]. The shaded band delin- eates the crossover between the decoherence-dominated regime (left, covariant maps serve as an excellent approximation) and the dissipation-dominated regime (right, where Eq. (8) attains quantitative significance). Cold atoms in dissipative optical lat- tices span the crossover and enter the violated regime; ultra- cold molecules reach its lower edge. Bars cap at ℏγ/kBT = 1, beyond which the...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that tracing out an environment breaks Galilean boost invariance in the reduced dynamics of a quantum system. This violation is tied to the dissipative term and the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, creating a fundamental conflict between these physical principles. The effect is most prominent in regimes where dissipation dominates, such as in certain cold atom experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** It identifies a fundamental kinematic obstruction in open quantum systems that affects the interpretation of dynamics in platforms like cold atoms and trapped ions, and provides a method for calibrating dissipation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether Galilean boost invariance is preserved in the reduced dynamics of an open quantum system after tracing out environmental degrees of freedom.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate a 'no-go' result: for any non-trivial bath spectral density, the dissipative term in the master equation breaks boost covariance, meaning one cannot simultaneously satisfy Galilean invariance, the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and reduced boost covariance.

**Method.** The study employs an operator-level analysis of the exact Hu-Paz-Zhang master equation, algebraic testing of transformations under infinitesimal boosts, and stochastic decomposition of the influence functional.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on the Caldeira-Leggett model, featuring a system particle coupled bilinearly to a bath of harmonic oscillators via relative coordinates.

**Key observables.** The dissipative anticommutator term, momentum diffusion, and the boost-violation parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless ratio h-bar*gamma/k_B*T, the damping coefficient Gamma(t), and the squeezing rate mu.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a factorized initial condition and focuses on quadratic/bilinear coupling; the extension to arbitrary anharmonic potentials is presented as a conjecture.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 plots the dimensionless boost-violation parameter across various experimental platforms, delineating the crossover between the covariant regime and the regime of substantial violation.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes global Galilean invariance, applies the partial trace to identify the symmetry-breaking term in the master equation, links this breaking to the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and discusses experimental implications and potential restoration via parametric driving.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tracing out a Galilean-invariant Caldeira-Leggett environment breaks Galilean boost covariance of the reduced dynamics, while spatial translations and rotations survive intact. An operator-level analysis of the exact Hu-Paz-Zhang master equation localizes the violation entirely in the dissipative anticommutator term, scaling with the damping coefficient $Γ(t)f(t)$. The fluctuation-dissipation theorem ties this coefficient to the absorptive bath response that drives equilibrium momentum diffusion, so for any non-trivial bath spectral density bilinear-coupled Galilean invariance, the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and reduced boost covariance cannot hold simultaneously. The stochastic decomposition of the influence functional extends the mechanism beyond the quadratic regime. The dimensionless ratio $\hbarγ/k_\mathrm{B} T$ delineates the crossover: cold atoms in dissipative optical lattices and ultracold molecules sit at its edge. Parametric driving offers a one-directional escape: the squeezing rate that protects nonequilibrium entanglement above the standard quantum limit also suppresses boost-breaking over a driving cycle.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27489"></a>
### [Anomalous tunneling as a low-energy theorem for Nambu-Goldstone modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27489v1)

**Authors:** Keisuke Fujii, Daichi Kagamihara, Masaru Hongo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27489v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper establishes that the perfect transmission of Nambu-Goldstone modes through potential barriers at zero energy is a universal consequence of symmetry and scaling. It demonstrates that while symmetry-preserving potentials do not impede transmission in the long-wavelength limit, symmetry-breaking perturbations suppress it. This provides a general theoretical foundation for anomalous tunneling in various broken-symmetry phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental, symmetry-based principle for understanding transport properties of collective excitations in many-body systems, which is highly relevant to the dynamics of ultracold gases and superfluids.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to establish the general conditions under which anomalous tunneling (perfect transmission at zero energy) occurs in systems with spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Main result.** The authors prove that anomalous tunneling of Nambu-Goldstone modes is a universal low-energy theorem determined by symmetry and scaling, where symmetry-preserving potentials are irrelevant in the long-wavelength limit.

**Method.** The study utilizes a low-energy effective field theory (EFT) framework to formulate the scattering of Nambu-Goldstone modes by external potentials.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to Nambu-Goldstone modes in systems with spontaneous symmetry breaking, with specific illustrative examples provided for superfluid phonons and magnons.

**Key observables.** Transmission coefficient as a function of incident particle/quasiparticle energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low-energy/long-wavelength limit, symmetry-preserving vs. symmetry-breaking perturbations, and spatially dependent EFT coefficients.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the validity of the low-energy effective field theory and focuses on the long-wavelength limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of anomalous tunneling, establishes a universal theorem using EFT, distinguishes between relevant and irrelevant perturbations based on symmetry, and provides specific examples (phonons and magnons).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Anomalous tunneling refers to the phenomenon in which the transmission coefficient through a potential barrier approaches unity as the energy of an incident particle or quasiparticle tends to zero. This counterintuitive effect has been reported in systems exhibiting spontaneous symmetry breaking (SSB), such as superfluids, yet the general conditions for its occurrence remain unclear. In this Letter, we establish that anomalous tunneling of Nambu-Goldstone (NG) modes is a universal low-energy theorem dictated solely by symmetry and scaling, using a low-energy effective field theory (EFT) framework. We formulate the scattering of NG modes by external potentials in terms of spatially dependent EFT coefficients and demonstrate that symmetry-preserving localized potentials are irrelevant in the long-wavelength limit, leading to perfect transmission. In contrast, symmetry-breaking perturbations are relevant and suppress transmission, resulting in the absence of anomalous tunneling. We illustrate this universal behavior with explicit examples of superfluid phonons and magnons.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27965"></a>
### [Propelling catalytic structures using active phase separation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27965v1)

**Authors:** Benjamin Sorkin, Ned S. Wingreen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27965v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper proposes a motor-free mechanism for biological transport where active droplets propel colloids through a chemical cycle of protein passivation and activation. This process creates a localized condensate that exerts a repulsive force, enabling efficient movement even in the presence of noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How can active droplets sustain the indefinite self-propulsion of a colloid in a homogeneous environment without traditional molecular motors?

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a passivation-activation cycle of phase-separating proteins can induce symmetry breaking and create a repulsive force that propels colloids at speeds up to 100 microns per second.

**Method.** Numerical simulations of an active phase-separating system involving enzymatic reactions.

**Model / system.** A system consisting of phase-separating proteins, enzymes that passivate them, and complementary enzymes anchored to the surface of a spherical colloid.

**Key observables.** Colloid propulsion speed, stability of the condensate, and resistance to Brownian fluctuations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Micron-sized scale, speeds up to 100 microns per second, and the presence of Brownian fluctuations and external forces.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The environment is homogeneous, isotropic, and autonomous.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of biological motility, proposes a minimal mechanism based on enzymatic phase separation, and provides numerical evidence for the resulting propulsion and its robustness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Living systems routinely consume energy to achieve motility, often using intricate biomolecular machinery. In this work, we show that active droplets can sustain indefinite self-propulsion of a spherical colloid in an otherwise homogeneous, isotropic, and autonomous environment. Our proposed minimal mechanism consists of phase-separating proteins, enzymes passivating them, and complementary enzymes anchored to the colloid surface that reactivate the proteins. This passivation-activation cycle gives rise to a symmetry breaking - nucleation and stabilization of a condensate near the colloid surface, which in turn exerts a repulsive force on the colloid. We numerically demonstrate that this mechanism can propel micron-sized colloids at speeds of up to a hundred microns per second. This propulsion mode is strongly resistant to Brownian fluctuations and external forces, suggesting that propulsion mechanisms based on biomolecular condensates may offer a complementary, motor-free route to biological transport.

</details>

<sub>Figure 5. Spatially averaged two-time height autocorrelation function, rescaled by the squared roughness, Ct(t, t0)/w2(t0), as a function of the time difference t −t0 for the RSOS model, shown for different waiting times t0 = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7}, which appear bottom to top in the inset. The system size is N = 1000 and m = 10. The solid black line corresponds to the theoretical prediction given by Eq. (17) with τRSOS = m = 10. Inset: Same data shown without rescaling by the squared roughness w2(t0).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper examines how the absence of spatial structure in a complete graph affects the universality of interface growth models. It finds that while some models like RSOS retain features of their continuum counterparts, others like Ballistic Deposition deviate significantly, exhibiting entirely new growth regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether discrete lattice growth models (RSOS and BD) belonging to the EW and KPZ universality classes reproduce the behavior of their corresponding continuum equations when defined on a complete graph (infinite-dimensional limit).

**Main result.** The RSOS model shows similarities to continuum equations with decreasing saturation roughness, while the BD model exhibits non-standard behavior, including an ultrafast growth regime and saturation roughness that increases with system size. Both models show height fluctuations with a pronounced left tail due to lagging nodes.

**Method.** Extensive numerical simulations of discrete models on a complete graph, supplemented by analytical derivations using master equations and approximations of growth events.

**Model / system.** Discrete interface growth models, specifically the Restricted Solid-on-Solid (RSOS) and Ballistic Deposition (BD) models, implemented on a complete graph (all-to-all connectivity).

**Key observables.** Global interfacial roughness (w), height fluctuations, power spectra (S(omega)), and two-time height autocorrelation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (N), height constraint (m), and time (t).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a complete graph topology where spatial separation is undefined, and uses simplified approximations for the BD model's growth dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Plots of squared roughness evolution (showing RD-like growth and rebound), histograms of height fluctuations showing exponential tails, power spectra showing omega^-2 scaling, and autocorrelation functions showing exponential decay.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to interface growth and complete graph topology; analysis of trivial models (RDSR, Etching, PNG); detailed study of the RSOS model (dynamics, scaling, and height distributions); detailed study of the BD model (ultrafast regime and saturation); comparison with continuum EW/KPZ equations; conclusion.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the behavior of discrete interface growth models belonging to the Edwards--Wilkinson (EW) and Kardar--Parisi--Zhang (KPZ) universality classes, when defined on a complete graph, a topology commonly used to probe the infinite-dimensional limit of statistical mechanical systems. Our aim is to assess to what extent discrete lattice models reproduce the behavior of their corresponding continuum equations in this highly connected setting. After assessing the trivial behavior shown by some well known cases (like random deposition with surface relaxation or the etching model), we focus on two paradigmatic models associated with the KPZ universality class, the Restricted Solid-on-Solid (RSOS) and Ballistic Deposition (BD) models, and assess non-trivial behavior through several observables including the roughness, height fluctuations, power spectra, and two-time autocorrelation functions. Still, despite similarities with continuum equations, important differences arise in the fluctuations and long-time dynamics. In both discrete models the rescaled height fluctuations display a pronounced left tail, indicating the presence of lagging nodes. While the RSOS model exhibits a saturation roughness that decreases with system size, similarly to the EW and KPZ equations, the BD model exhibits a saturation roughness that increases with system size and an additional ultrafast growth regime, placing it outside the KPZ universality class on a complete graph.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27834"></a>
### [Hypergeometric Functions of Nilpotent Operators: Functional Collapse and Structural Depth at Exceptional Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27834v1)

**Authors:** Ramon Moya  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27834v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proves that applying analytic functions to nilpotent operators causes hypergeometric series to collapse into finite polynomials. It provides a quantitative rule for how much the Jordan structure of an exceptional point is reduced based on the function's contact order at the eigenvalue.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in non-Hermitian physics and open quantum systems, as it provides a rigorous mathematical tool to predict how functional transformations (like time evolution or filtering) can simplify or destroy the complex algebraic structures of exceptional points.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the 'functional collapse' of hypergeometric functions and other analytic functions when applied to nilpotent operators, specifically how these transformations affect the Jordan structure at exceptional points.

**Main result.** The author establishes a 'nilpotent depth criterion' showing that if a function has a contact order r at an eigenvalue, the nilpotency index of the transformed operator is reduced to at most ceil((m+1)/r).

**Method.** The study uses algebraic functional calculus, Taylor expansions, and matrix theory within the framework of complex associative algebras to analyze the properties of hypergeometric series and Jordan blocks.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a non-Hermitian Hamiltonian H = lambda I + N at an exceptional point, where N is a nilpotent operator of index m+1, representing systems like PT-symmetric gain-loss models.

**Key observables.** Jordan depth (nilpotency index), contact order of the function, and the order of the pole of the modified resolvent.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nilpotency index (m+1), contact order (r), and the eigenvalue (lambda).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The operators are assumed to be finite-dimensional and nilpotent, and the functions are assumed to be analytic at the eigenvalue.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes explicit 3x3 Jordan block computations for various functions (Kummer, Gauss hypergeometric, and time evolution) to demonstrate the sharpness of the theoretical bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the functional collapse of hypergeometric functions, introduces the nilpotent depth criterion (Theorem 2), applies this to Hamiltonians at exceptional points (Theorem 3), and concludes with explicit 3x3 matrix examples and consequences for the resolvent and time evolution.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study hypergeometric functions of nilpotent operators in finite-dimensional settings, motivated by the algebraic structure of exceptional points in non-Hermitian quantum mechanics. Our starting point is the following exact result: if N is a nilpotent operator of index m+1 in an associative algebra over C, then every generalized hypergeometric function pFq evaluated at N reduces to a finite polynomial in N of degree at most m, without any analytic convergence requirement. This "functional collapse" is distinct from the classical parameter-termination mechanism and arises purely from the nilpotent structure of the argument. The main result is a "nilpotent depth criterion" (Theorem 2): if the first non-constant coefficient of a formal series F appears in degree r >= 1, then the nilpotent part F(N) - F(0)I has nilpotency index bounded above by ceil((m+1)/r). We apply this criterion to Hamiltonians at exceptional points, where H = lambda I + N with N^{m+1} = 0. Theorem 3 establishes that a function F analytic at lambda reduces the Jordan depth of the exceptional point from m+1 to at most ceil((m+1)/r), where r is the contact order of F at lambda. As consequences: the time evolution operator e^{tH} preserves the full Jordan depth for all t != 0; a function with a zero of order m+1 at lambda annihilates the entire Jordan structure; and the order of the pole of the modified resolvent is reduced from m+1 to at most m+1-r. Results are illustrated with explicit 3x3 Jordan block computations for 1F1, 2F1, and the time evolution operator, confirming sharpness of the bounds.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28089"></a>
### [Source-independent quantum key distribution without pre-sending entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28089v1)

**Authors:** Rong-Zheng Liu, Hua-Lei Yin  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28089v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. Protocols comparison. (a) Single-photon based and (b) WCS-based BB84 protocols: both the source and measurement devices must be trusted. (c) SI-QKD protocol: The non-classical source generation and distribution are mod- eled as an untrusted black box, while the local measurements at Alice and Bob are trusted. PM: phase modulator, IM: in- tensity modulator, BS: beam splitter.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. The polarization scheme of SI-QKD protocol. Charlie uses two independent non-classical sources that each emits a sequence of optical pulses. Each photon is prepared in the |+⟩state by a polarizer (Pol) and half-wave plate (HWP). Pulses from two sources interfere at a polarizing beam splitter (PBS) and distributed to receivers. Alice and Bob use a local beam splitter (BS) to randomly select the measurement basis, followed by polarization analysis in either the rectilinear (Z) basis or the diagonal (X ) basis. Measurement outcomes are recorded by four single-photon detectors (SPDs) corresponding to polarization bases.</sub>

The parameters are set as ed = 0.01, pd = 10−7 and N = 1012. (b) Finite SKR for various mis- alignment error ed with fixed parameters g(2)(0) = 0.01. The transmission distance denotes the fiber length between Alice and Bob.</sub>

</details>

<sub>FIG. 5. The experimental setup of optical switch. Sin- gle photons enter the Sagnac interferometer via the circulator (Cir) and beam splitter (BS). An electro-optic phase modu- lator (PM) applies periodic phase shifts at specific time slots. Depending on the relative phase (π or 0), photons from odd and even time slots are routed to separate output ports (c and d).</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new source-independent QKD protocol that is immune to all known and unknown source-side attacks without needing to pre-send entanglement. By using non-classical light sources, the protocol can double the transmission distance compared to standard single-photon BB84. The study demonstrates that this approach is robust against source imperfections and provides a practical path for long-distance quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a significant advancement in quantum optics and quantum communication by leveraging non-classical states (like cat states) to achieve higher security and longer transmission distances without the overhead of entanglement distribution.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The vulnerability of QKD protocols to source-side attacks (e.g., photon-number splitting, light-injection) and the high experimental complexity of existing source-independent protocols that require pre-sending entanglement.

**Main result.** The proposed SI-QKD protocol eliminates all known and unknown source-side attacks without pre-sending entanglement, while simultaneously doubling the transmission distance and outperforming conventional WCS-based decoy-state protocols.

**Method.** The authors propose a protocol using non-classical light sources (single-photon or odd cat states) where an untrusted third party interferes pulses at a PBS, followed by a security analysis using the entropy uncertainty relation and finite-size key rate calculations.

**Model / system.** A source-independent QKD setup where an untrusted third party (Charlie) uses independent non-classical sources or a single source with an active optical switch to inject pulses into a polarizing beam splitter (PBS) for distribution to Alice and Bob.

**Key observables.** Secure key rate (SKR), quantum bit error rate (QBER), phase error rate (phi_z), and the second-order correlation function g(2)(0).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Average photon number (n), second-order correlation function g(2)(0), channel attenuation (0.16 dB/km), detector efficiency (0.8), and dark count rate (10^-7 to 10^-5).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum source is treated as an untrusted black box; local measurements at Alice and Bob are assumed to be trusted and well-characterized; the channel is assumed to be symmetric.

**Figures summary.** Fig 3(a) compares finite SKR of the proposed protocol against BB84 variants; Fig 3(b) investigates performance; Fig 4 shows g(2)(0) and photon number as functions of intensity; Fig 5 shows the optical switch schematic; Fig 6 shows SKR vs. distance for different dark count rates and data sizes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of source-side vulnerabilities, proposes a new SI-QKD protocol using non-classical light, provides a theoretical security framework based on uncertainty relations, presents numerical simulations comparing performance and distance limits, and discusses experimental implementation via active optical switching.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum key distribution (QKD) theoretically offers information-theoretic security. The prevailing approach is the prepare-and-measure BB84 protocol, which implements QKD using conventional laser rather than single-photon source via the decoy-state method. However, side-channel attacks targeting sources severely threaten system security. Despite extensive efforts, including fully passive scheme, this vulnerability persists even with perfect single-photon source. Here, we propose a source-independent (SI) QKD protocol that resolves all known and unknown source-side attacks without pre-sending entanglement source. Aligning with advances in quantum light sources, our protocol simultaneously doubles the transmission distance while remaining robustness against imperfection of source. Theoretical analysis shows that non-classical light source provides practical security advantages unattainable with conventional laser.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27171"></a>
### [Structure-Aware Transformers for Learning Near-Optimal Trotter Orderings with System-Size Generalization in 1D Heisenberg Hamiltonians](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27171v1)

**Authors:** Shamminuj Aktar, Reuben Tate, Stephan Eidenbenz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27171v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Figure 1: Fidelity of random and structured Trotter orderings across system sizes L = 5, 10, 16 for the N´eel initial state, first-order product formula, and r = 5 Trotter steps. At L = 5, random and structured orderings are often comparable. By L = 10, a clear gap emerges, and at L = 16 the structured candidates consistently occupy the high-fidelity region while random orderings are concentrated lower. This trend shows that structured orderings become increasingly...</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Overview of the coloring-aware Trotter ordering pipeline, divided into two stages. Dataset generation (left): For a given 1D XXZ Hamiltonian (L, ∆, g), we construct the commutation graph C(H) from Pauli terms and apply four coloring methods (xyz groups, greedy, gurobi, handcrafted), producing C = 24 candidate orderings (4×3! = 24). For each candidate ordering, we compute the fidelity F(σ) = |⟨ψexact|ψTrotter(σ)⟩|2 between the exact and Trotter-evolved states. These fidelities are used during training only: the oracle fidelity, defined as the best fidelity among the 24 structured candidates, provides the supervision label. Model learning (right): Per-term local features and global...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Selected-ordering fidelity versus oracle fidelity for the learned model and the strongest non-learned baseline, MajRegime, across held-out test instances (L ∈{16, 17, 18, 19, 20}). Points are colored by method and shaped by Trotter regime (p, r), and the dashed line marks oracle-level performance y = x. The learned selector remains more tightly concentrated near the oracle line overall, while MajRegime shows substantially larger deviations, especially in the lower-fidelity regime.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Error landscape: fidelity gap f∗−f model versus transverse field g for each Trotter regime, averaged over five seeds on test sizes L ∈{16, 17, 18, 19, 20}. Color indicates anisotropy ∆, and the dashed line marks the poor-prediction threshold (gap = 0.01). Under first-order Trotter (top row), the poor-prediction rate remains low at 0.7% for r = 3 and 1.8% for r = 5. Under second-order Trotter (bottom row), failures are more frequent, with poor-prediction rates of 6.0% for r = 3 and 6.0% for r = 5. The largest gaps concentrate at g ≳2.0, where oracle fidelity is already low and differences between candidate orderings become more pronounced.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Generalization sweep. Mean fidelity gap versus system size L for each validation size Lmax ∈{5, . . . , 15}, where training uses L ∈{3, . . . , Lmax −1} and evaluation is performed on larger sizes up to L = 20 (one line per Lmax, mean over three seeds). The shaded region marks the final held-out test range L ∈{16, . . . , 20}, and the dashed horizontal line marks the MajRegime reference. Lines for Lmax ≤8 remain near 10−2 on larger systems, indicating limited generalization. Generalization emerges at Lmax = 9, and lines for Lmax ≥13 compress toward 10−3 with little growth across system size, confirming clean extrapolation to larger systems.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents a machine learning approach using Transformers to solve the Trotter ordering problem in quantum simulations. By training on small-scale 1D Heisenberg models, the model learns to predict high-fidelity term sequences that generalize to much larger system sizes. This allows for efficient, high-accuracy quantum time-evolution without the need for expensive classical fidelity evaluations during inference.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides an AI-driven method to reduce approximation errors in quantum simulations, potentially enabling more accurate time-evolution studies of complex spin systems on near-term quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the ordering of non-commuting Hamiltonian terms in Trotterization to maximize simulation fidelity without the exponential computational cost of evaluating all possible permutations.

**Main result.** A structure-aware Transformer encoder can predict near-optimal Trotter orderings for large systems by training on small systems, achieving a mean fidelity gap of only 0.00115 on unseen larger systems.

**Method.** The authors use a Transformer encoder trained on size-invariant features of 1D XXZ Hamiltonians to select the best ordering from a structured set of 24 candidates derived from commutation-graph colorings.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on 1D XXZ Heisenberg Hamiltonians with a transverse field and open boundaries, decomposed into local Pauli terms.

**Key observables.** Simulation fidelity (squared overlap between exact and Trotter-evolved states), fidelity gap, and normalized gain.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (L from 3 to 20 qubits), Trotter order (1st and 2nd order), step count (r = 3, 5), anisotropy (Delta), and transverse field strength (g).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The search space is restricted to a structured set of 24 candidates rather than the full k! permutations, and the study is limited to 1D XXZ Hamiltonians.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares random vs. structured orderings; Figure 2 shows the two-stage pipeline of candidate generation and model learning; Figure 3 shows the model tracking the oracle fidelity; Figure 5 shows the emergence of generalization with increasing training size; Figure 6 shows the model's sample efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Trotter ordering problem, describes the Hamiltonian and candidate generation via graph coloring, details the Transformer architecture and size-invariant feature encoding, presents results on fidelity gaps and system-size generalization, and concludes with error analysis and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Trotterization is a standard approach for simulating quantum time evolution on quantum computers, where the Hamiltonian is split into local terms and each term is applied in sequence. The order of these terms affects the fidelity of the simulation when they do not commute, so the choice of ordering directly impacts the accuracy of the simulation. We study this problem for one-dimensional XXZ Heisenberg Hamiltonians using a structured set of 24 candidate orderings derived from colorings of the Hamiltonian's commutation graph and their group permutations. Finding the best candidate for large systems becomes prohibitive because fidelity evaluation is computationally expensive. In this work, we train a transformer encoder on smaller systems to predict the best candidate ordering for larger systems directly from Hamiltonian and Trotter-configuration features, without computing candidate fidelities at inference time. The model is trained on in-range systems of 3 to 14 qubits with 15-qubit systems held out for validation. Experimental results show that the model reaches a mean test fidelity gap of 0.00115 relative to the best of the 24 candidates on out-of-range systems of 16 to 20 qubits. A training-size sweep further shows that generalization emerges once training includes systems up to L=8 qubits, with validation at L=9, and the gap continues to decrease as the training range grows. To our knowledge, this is the first application of a learned model to Trotter ordering, and it motivates future work on AI-guided Trotter ordering with generalization across Hamiltonian families and system types.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27873"></a>
### [Theory for the mixed alkali effect in glasses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27873v1)

**Authors:** Justus Leiber, Quinn Emilia Fischer, Sven Lohmann, Philipp Maass  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27873v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. Occupation of sites with energies ϵA and ϵB in the zero-temperature limit. <sub>FIG. 6. Impact of energy landscape features on the variation of activation energies EAHL A (x) and EAHL B (x) of partial conductiv- ities: data for (a) two mismatch energies ϵmis [Eq. Lines are guides to the eye.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive theory for the mixed alkali effect in glasses by linking microscopic energy landscapes to macroscopic transport properties. It explains how energy mismatches and spatial correlations drive nonlinear changes in conductivity. The theoretical predictions show excellent agreement with experimental measurements in alkali phosphate glasses.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a theoretical framework to explain the mixed alkali effect (MAE) in glasses, specifically the nonlinear variations in ionic transport properties when different types of mobile ions are mixed.

**Main result.** The theory demonstrates that the MAE arises from mismatch energy and site energy correlations, and can occur even with identical site energy distributions. It successfully predicts the 'vulnerability effect' and matches experimental activation energies in phosphate glasses.

**Method.** A generalized Ambegaokar-Halperin-Langer (AHL) theory combined with kinetic Monte Carlo simulations and the use of the grand canonical ensemble to derive transport coefficients.

**Model / system.** A fermionic hopping model on a disordered lattice representing ion-conducting glasses, featuring site-exclusion interactions and a joint probability density function for site energies of different ion species.

**Key observables.** Ionic conductivity, mobility, activation energies, partial conductivities, and Onsager coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mixing ratio (x), mismatch energy (epsilon_mis), spatial correlation strength (kappa), and site energy fluctuations (Delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of long-range Coulomb interactions, use of a simple cubic lattice, and the assumption of a constant lower barrier for saddle point energies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows jump rates and energy barriers; Figure 2 shows site occupation in the zero-temperature limit; Figure 3 displays joint PDFs of site energies; Figure 4 illustrates the energy landscape; Figure 5 compares KMC and AHL theory; Figure 6 shows the impact of correlations on activation energies; Figure 7 shows fits to experimental data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MAE problem, defines the Hamiltonian and statistical mechanics of the site-exclusion model, develops the generalized AHL transport theory, introduces spatial and energy correlations, validates the theory via KMC simulations, and concludes with an application to experimental phosphate glass data.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The mixed alkali or mixed mobile ion effect in glasses manifests itself by strong nonlinear variations of ionic transport properties upon mixing of different types of mobile ions. We develop a theory for this effect based on thermally activated hopping transport in disordered site energy landscapes that consistently incorporates the statistical-mechanical and kinetic aspects of a mobile ion mixture. This includes a consideration of the joint probability density of site energy states, generalized Fermi distributions for mean site occupations, and cross-terms in the current response described by nondiagonal Onsager coefficients. The theory shows that a mixed alkali effect can arise even when the two ion species share identical site energy distributions. It suffices that sites have distinct energies when occupied by ions of different type. Taking into account that a mismatch energy is needed for ions of one type to occupy sites adapted to the other type, the mixed alkali effect becomes stronger. Spatial correlations between site energies are needed for the mobility of the majority ion to decrease stronger than exponential upon replacement by the minority ion. The theory agrees well with kinetic Monte Carlo simulations. Application to mixed alkali phosphate glasses yields good agreement with measured conductivity activation energies.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27851"></a>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new wavelet-based method to accurately measure the fractal dimensions of quantum interference patterns (quantum carpets) without needing prior assumptions about scaling laws. The method is shown to be highly robust and recovers established theoretical predictions for space, time, and space-time fractals. It offers a unified, computationally efficient tool for analyzing multiscale dynamics in confined quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a new, robust diagnostic tool for analyzing complex interference patterns and self-similar structures in any system exhibiting wave-like interference and confinement, such as ultracold atoms in optical lattices or wave-packet dynamics in cavities.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The quantitative characterization of quantum fractals (quantum carpets) is difficult because traditional methods like box-counting or Fourier analysis are sensitive to numerical cutoffs and require prior assumptions about scaling ranges.

**Main result.** The authors developed a wavelet-based multiresolution analysis (MRA) framework that provides a robust, hypothesis-free, and scale-adaptive way to extract fractal dimensions, successfully recovering Berry's predicted values.

**Method.** A wavelet-based multiresolution framework using discrete wavelet transforms (Haar and Daubechies families) to analyze the scale-dependent distribution of wavelet energies and Bohmian trajectories as adaptive probes.

**Model / system.** A quantum particle confined in a one-dimensional infinite potential well, starting from initial states with spatial discontinuities (e.g., square wave functions).

**Key observables.** Fractal dimensions (space, time, and space-time), wavelet energies, probability density, and quantum probability flux trajectories.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dyadic scales (j), spectral truncation (N), recurrence time (T), and Hurst exponent (H).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is inherently 'pre-fractal' due to finite spectral truncation; the use of Bohmian trajectories is for methodological probing rather than ontological interpretation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows quantum carpets with superimposed Bohmian trajectories; Figure 2 shows spatial density profiles and convergence of fractal dimensions vs. truncation; Figure 3 shows temporal profiles and convergence; Figure 4 shows convergence for flux-based trajectories.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of fractal characterization, presents the wavelet-based MRA framework, applies it to spatial and temporal profiles of quantum carpets, validates the method using Bohmian trajectories, and demonstrates robustness across different wavelet families and parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fractal structures naturally emerge in quantum systems whose initial states exhibit spatial discontinuities, a phenomenon first identified by Berry in the paradigmatic case of a particle confined in an infinite potential well. While previous analyses of quantum fractals have mainly relied on spectral decompositions and geometric scaling arguments, their quantitative characterization often depends on scale choices and truncation effects. Here we present a wavelet-based multiresolution framework that enables a direct and assumption-free quantification of quantum fractality. Fractal dimensions are extracted from the scale-dependent distribution of wavelet energies, without invoking prior power-law hypotheses. The method is applied to space and time quantum fractals arising in confined dynamics, as well as to dynamical curves generated by the associated quantum probability flux. These flux-driven trajectories provide a natural space--time parametrization of the underlying fractal structure and yield scaling properties fully consistent with Berry's predictions for space--time fractals. The resulting fractal dimensions are shown to be robust with respect to the choice of wavelet family, numerical cutoffs, and system parameters. Beyond validating earlier conjectures, the present framework offers a unified and computationally efficient tool for the multiscale analysis of quantum fractality in confined and interference-driven quantum dynamics. That is, it provides an operational, scale-adaptive criterion that unifies the characterization of space, time, and space--time quantum fractals within a single, hypothesis-free approach.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28050"></a>
### [A No-Cloning Trade-off Between Black Hole No-Hair and Horizon Smoothness](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28050v1)

**Authors:** Sudhanva Joshi, Sunil Kumar Mishra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28050v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28050_figures/2604.28050_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Penrose diagram of a black hole formed by gravitational collapse. The spacetime is divided into an interior region I (above the dashed horizon line H +) and an exterior region E (below). An infalling quantum system F in state |ψ⟩crosses the horizon, with its state embedded in I via the isometric embedding V . The reduced states ρI(ψ) and ρE(ψ) encode the information accessible to interior and exterior observers, respectively. The trade-off inequality Eq. (16) constrains the relationship between horizon smoothness (ε) and exterior distinguishability (Dmax).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a mathematical trade-off showing that an observer cannot simultaneously detect information about infalling matter from the exterior and maintain a smooth event horizon. It proves that any 'quantum hair' or microstate distinguishability requires a quantifiable violation of the equivalence principle. This provides a universal constraint on various quantum gravity models like fuzzballs and firewalls.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the fundamental compatibility between the existence of observable quantum hair (exterior distinguishability of microstates) and the smoothness of the black hole event horizon.

**Main result.** The authors derive a quantitative trade-off inequality, epsilon >= D_max^2/8, proving that any model predicting non-zero exterior quantum hair must necessarily violate the equivalence principle at the horizon.

**Method.** The study uses a quantum information-theoretic framework involving the Stinespring dilation of quantum channels, the diamond norm, and the Kretschmann-Schlingemann-Werner (KSW) continuity theorem.

**Model / system.** A semi-classical black hole model where an infalling quantum system enters the horizon, with the total Hilbert space decomposed into interior and exterior sectors.

**Key observables.** epsilon (horizon smoothness violation), D_max (maximum exterior distinguishability), and F_I (interior fidelity).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Bekenstein-Hawking entropy (S_BH), the regime of perfect isometry (epsilon = 0), and the firewall/fuzzball regimes (epsilon ~ O(1)).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Global unitarity, horizon causality (no signaling between interior and exterior), and diamond-norm interior accessibility.

**Figures summary.** A Penrose diagram of a black hole illustrating the interior and exterior regions and the encoding of infalling states.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing an 'Exact no-hair lemma' for perfect isometries, then uses the KSW theorem to derive a quantitative bound on distinguishability, and concludes by discussing implications for fuzzballs, soft hair, and entanglement.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The black hole no-hair theorem is traditionally derived from the uniqueness theorems of general relativity. We show that a quantitative form follows from unitarity together with the standard semiclassical assumptions of horizon causality and interior accessibility. For a semiclassical black hole, we prove that the trace distance between exterior states corresponding to two same-charge infalling states is bounded by $2\sqrt{2\varepsilon}$, where $\varepsilon$ quantifies the diamond norm departure of the interior channel from a perfect isometry which is a quantitative measure of horizon-smoothness violation that upper-bounds $1 - F_I$, where $F_I$ is the interior fidelity capturing how faithfully the infalling state is retained. Inverting this relation yields a trade-off inequality, $\varepsilon \geq D_{\max}^2/8$, between the maximum exterior distinguishability $D_{\max}$ and the degree of horizon smoothness. This establishes that observable exterior quantum hair is quantitatively incompatible with exact horizon smoothness under unitary evolution: any model predicting nonzero exterior hair must violate the equivalence principle at the horizon by a quantifiable amount. Pre-existing entanglement with the infalling system is the only channel for quantum hair compatible with both unitarity and horizon smoothness.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27363"></a>
### [Constructing Bulk Topological Orders via Layered Gauging](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27363v1)

**Authors:** Shang Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27363v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new, intuitive method called 'layered gauging' to construct complex higher-dimensional topological phases, including fracton orders, from simpler lower-dimensional systems. This provides a versatile toolkit for generating new models with various symmetry types, such as non-abelian or non-invertible symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of applying sophisticated mathematical formalisms to construct higher-dimensional bulk topological orders from lower-dimensional symmetries.

**Main result.** The proposal of a 'layered gauging construction' that systematically generates (k+1)-dimensional liquid or fracton topological orders from k-dimensional generalized symmetries.

**Method.** Stacking layers of k-dimensional quantum systems and sequentially gauging a diagonal symmetry acting on nearest-neighbor pairs of layers.

**Model / system.** The method is applied to various systems including the 2d plaquette Ising model and 1d systems with anomalous Z2 symmetry, resulting in models like the X-cube model and double semion topological order.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the existence of well-defined symmetries (conventional, higher-form, subsystem, etc.) in the constituent k-dimensional layers.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of topological holography, proposes the layered gauging construction, demonstrates its versatility across different symmetry types, and provides specific examples including fracton orders and anomalous symmetry constructions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding quantum phases and phase transitions in the presence of symmetries is a central objective of quantum many-body physics. A powerful modern paradigm for investigating this problem is topological holography, which relates symmetries in $k$ dimensions to "bulk" topological orders in $(k+1)$ dimensions. While conceptually profound, most existing bulk construction methods rely on sophisticated mathematical formalisms and can be difficult to apply to certain symmetry types. In this work, we propose a physically intuitive and versatile method, termed the layered gauging construction, to systematically generate $(k+1)$-dimensional (liquid or fracton) topological orders from $k$-dimensional generalized symmetries. Roughly speaking, the prescription is to stack many layers of $k$-dimensional quantum systems with certain symmetries into a $(k+1)$-dimensional pile, and then sequentially gauge a diagonal symmetry acting on each nearest-neighbor pair of layers. The detailed procedure depends on the specific symmetry types. We have successfully implemented the method in a number of examples in different spatial dimensions, with symmetries that are conventional, higher-form, subsystem, anomalous, nonabelian, or noninvertible. We hence conjecture the method to be very general. For example, from the subsystem symmetry of the $2d$ plaquette Ising model, we derive the X-cube model and also an anisotropic fracton topological order. Additionally, starting from an anomalous $\mathbb Z_2$ symmetry in $1d$, we construct a new square lattice model realizing the double semion topological order.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Hyperfine-resolved laser excitation and detection of nuclear isomer in trapped $^{229}$Th$^{3+}$ ions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27614v1)

**Authors:** Wu Wang, Ke Zhang, Ke-Mi Xu, Shan-Gui Zhou  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27614v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides a theoretical framework for the excitation and detection of the Th-229 nuclear isomer in trapped ions. It proposes specific fluorescence-based detection schemes and demonstrates that the nuclear transition can be identified within a month using existing laser technology. These findings are crucial for the practical implementation of next-generation nuclear clocks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and AMO physics as it provides a roadmap for the development of nuclear clocks and the control of nuclear states using atomic-scale laser techniques.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the hyperfine-resolved laser excitation and detection of the low-energy nuclear isomeric state of Thorium-229 in trapped ions.

**Main result.** The study identifies two effective detection schemes using fluorescence channels at 690, 984, and 1088 nm and demonstrates that the nuclear transition can be located within one month using current VUV technology.

**Method.** A quantum master equation approach is used to analyze the dependence of isomeric population on laser parameters and to quantify photon detection rates.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of Th-229+ ions trapped in an ion trap, focusing on the hyperfine-resolved electronic and nuclear transitions.

**Key observables.** Isomeric population, photon detection rates (per ion), and laser linewidth/detuning/irradiation time dependencies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser linewidth, detuning, irradiation time, and specific fluorescence wavelengths (690, 984, and 1088 nm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the use of currently available vacuum-ultraviolet (VUV) laser technology and specific fluorescence channels.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a theoretical investigation of excitation dynamics, proposes two detection schemes based on fluorescence, and evaluates the practical feasibility of locating the nuclear transition.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a comprehensive theoretical investigation of hyperfine-resolved excitation and detection of the low-energy isomeric state of $^{229}$Th in trapped $^{229}\mathrm{Th}^{3+}$ ions. Using a quantum master equation approach, we analyze the dependence of the isomeric population on laser linewidth, detuning, and irradiation time, showing that their proper matching is essential for efficient excitation. We further propose two nuclear-state detection schemes based on three hyperfine-resolved electronic fluorescence channels at 690, 984, and 1088 nm. Our analysis shows that the 690-nm and 984-nm scheme yields detectable photon rates on the order of $10^4~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ per ion for each wavelength, whereas the 1088-nm scheme achieves a higher rate on the order of $10^5~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ per ion. By quantifying the trade-off between irradiation time and scan-step size, we show that the nuclear transition can be located within one month for a 100-MHz uncertainty using currently available vacuum-ultraviolet laser technology. These results provide practical guidance for trapped-ion $^{229}\mathrm{Th}$ spectroscopy and the development of nuclear clocks.

</details>
