### [Magnetic excitation spectrum and hierarchy of magnetic interactions in ErFeO3](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27857v1)

**Authors:** Dnyaneshwar R. Bhosale, Piotr Fabrykiewicz, Devashibhai Adroja, Martin Meven, Astrid Schneidewind, Michal Stekiel  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27857v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides a detailed map of the magnetic excitations in ErFeO3 using neutron spectroscopy. It distinguishes between iron-based spin waves and erbium-based crystal field excitations, establishing a clear hierarchy of magnetic interactions in this orthoferrite.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to map the complete magnetic excitation spectrum and identify the hierarchy of magnetic exchange interactions in the orthoferrite ErFeO3.

**Main result.** The researchers identified two distinct excitation components: dispersive spin waves from the Fe3+ sublattice and crystal electric field (CEF) excitations from Er3+ ions, revealing how exchange coupling and sublattice interactions drive dispersion and level splitting.

**Method.** Time-of-flight neutron spectroscopy was used to measure the excitation spectrum, with data analyzed using linear spin wave theory.

**Model / system.** The system is the ErFeO3 orthoferrite, consisting of interacting Fe3+ and Er3+ sublattices characterized by Fe-Fe exchange, Er-Er exchange, and Er-Fe interactions.

**Key observables.** Spin wave dispersions, spectral weight, and crystal electric field (CEF) excitation energies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fe-Fe exchange parameters, energy scales ranging from below 36 meV to 65 meV, and the influence of dipolar and antisymmetric exchange interactions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The spin wave dispersions and spectral weight are assumed to be well-captured within the framework of linear spin wave theory.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an investigation of the excitation spectrum, details the identification of spin wave and CEF components, applies linear spin wave theory to extract exchange parameters, and discusses the role of various exchange interactions on the magnetic ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report a comprehensive investigation of the excitation spectrum of ErFeO$_3$ orthoferrite by means of time-of-flight neutron spectroscopy. The spectrum consists of two distinct components: strongly dispersive spin wave excitations of the Fe$^{3+}$ sublattice spanning $\approx$~9 - 65 meV, and crystal electric field (CEF) excitations of Er$^{3+}$ ions below 36 meV. The observed spin wave dispersions and spectral weight are well captured within linear spin wave theory, enabling extraction of the key Fe-Fe exchange parameters. Low-energy incident neutrons with their enhanced energy resolution, further revealed the dispersive character and splitting of Kramers-degenerate CEF levels. We show that the dispersion is caused by the exchange coupling between Er$^{3+}$ ions, while the degeneracy is lifted by interactions between the Er$^{3+}$ and Fe$^{3+}$ sublattices. We further explore the influence of dipolar and antisymmetric exchange interactions with the focus on the magnetic ground state of ErFeO$_3$, with particular attention to the low-temperature spin arrangement. Taken together, our results provide a detailed account of the spin dynamics in ErFeO$_3$ and reveal a hierarchy of interactions scales characteristic for orthoferrites.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27087"></a>
### [Oscillators from non-semisimple walled Brauer algebras](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27087v1)

**Authors:** Sanjaye Ramgoolam, Michał Studziński  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27087v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27087_figures/2604.27087_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27087_figures/2604.27087_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27087_figures/2604.27087_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 shows the Bratteli diagram for 𝐵𝑁(2, 2) in the stable regime 𝑁≥4. The sequence of layers corresponds to</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27087_figures/2604.27087_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Coloured Bratteli diagram for 𝐵𝑁(2, 2) with 𝑁= 2. Red nodes violate the finite-𝑁constraint, while green nodes correspond to admissible Brauer triples.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper provides a new combinatorial method to calculate representation dimensions for walled Brauer algebras in the non-semisimple regime. It reveals that the complexity of these dimension corrections is governed by the same mathematics as an infinite tower of harmonic oscillators, linking algebraic combinatorics to fundamental quantum field theory structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Calculating dimension modifications and multiplicities for irreducible representations of the walled Brauer algebra in the non-semisimple regime where N < m + n.

**Main result.** The authors introduce Restricted Bratteli Diagrams and show that the counting of dimension corrections is governed by a universal generating function corresponding to the partition function of an infinite tower of simple harmonic oscillators.

**Method.** The study uses combinatorial path-counting on modified Bratteli diagrams, generating function analysis, and the identification of a stability property in the large-m, n regime.

**Model / system.** The mathematical model is the walled Brauer algebra B_N(m, n) acting on mixed tensor spaces, with physical applications in AdS/CFT gauge theory and quantum information protocols like port-based teleportation.

**Key observables.** Dimension modifications (delta), number of red and green nodes in the restricted Bratteli diagrams, and the universal generating function Z_univ(x).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The regime N < m + n, the parameter l = m + n - N, and the stability condition m, n >= 2l - 3.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the regime where l is small compared to m and n.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a mixed Young diagram; Figure 5 illustrates a specific Restricted Bratteli Diagram; Figure 6 demonstrates the stability of the diagram as m and n increase.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the non-semisimple problem, introduces the Restricted Bratteli Diagram construction, proves a stability property for large m and n, derives the universal oscillator generating function, and discusses implications for gauge theory and quantum information.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The walled Brauer algebras $B_N(m,n)$ govern Schur--Weyl duality for unitary groups $U(N)$ acting on mixed tensor spaces $V_N^{\otimes m}\otimes \overline{V}_N^{\otimes n}$ and play an important role in applications ranging from AdS/CFT to quantum information theory. In the stable regime $N\ge m+n$ the algebra is semisimple and its representation theory is well understood. For $N<m+n$, however, $B_N(m,n)$ becomes non-semisimple. The representation of the algebra on tensor space has a non-trivial kernel and the corresponding quotient algebra is semisimple, with representation dimensions differing from those in the stable regime.   We introduce \emph{restricted Bratteli diagrams}, obtained by modifying the standard Bratteli diagrams for $B_N(m,n)$. This construction provides a systematic way to use representation-theoretic data from the stable regime to compute the dimension modifications arising in the non-semisimple regime. In the regime $N=m+n-l$, with $l$ small compared to $m,n$, we show that the restricted diagrams exhibit a stability property and enable an efficient counting of the paths responsible for these dimension corrections.   Remarkably, the resulting generating functions are governed by the partition function of an infinite tower of simple harmonic oscillators. We briefly discuss implications for the construction of orthogonal bases of matrix invariants in gauge theory and related applications in quantum information theory.

</details>

### [Parametrized Variational Quantum Tomography](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27135v1)

**Authors:** V. A. Penas, M. Losada, D. Tielas, F. Holik  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27135v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** The authors propose a new method for quantum state tomography that unifies different variational approaches using a parametrized cost function. By tuning hyperparameters, the method can achieve results closer to Maximum Entropy solutions while remaining computationally efficient. This allows for better reconstruction of quantum states when measurement data is incomplete.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new tool for more accurate quantum state reconstruction in scenarios with limited measurement capabilities, which is critical for characterizing noisy quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the underdetermined nature of quantum state tomography when measurement data is not informationally complete.

**Main result.** The introduction of a unified framework that interpolates between the 1-norm and infinity norm, allowing for a controlled exploration of compatible density matrices with higher fidelity to MaxEnt solutions.

**Method.** A parametrized cost function that generalizes Variational Quantum Tomography (VQT) and VQT-infinity by interpolating between different norms.

**Model / system.** The paper focuses on the general problem of quantum state reconstruction from incomplete measurement statistics.

**Key observables.** Quantum state fidelity and measurement statistics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hyperparameters associated with the interpolated cost function that control the transition between 1-norm and infinity norm.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measurement data is not informationally complete, leading to multiple compatible density matrices.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of underdetermined tomography, discusses existing MaxEnt and VQT approaches, proposes a new interpolated cost function, and demonstrates its performance relative to VQT-infinity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum state tomography provides a fundamental framework for reconstructing quantum states. When the measurement data are not informationally complete, the observed statistics admit multiple compatible density matrices, making the reconstruction problem inherently underdetermined and calling for the selection of a meaningful estimator. Two well-established approaches to address this ambiguity are Maximum Entropy (MaxEnt) and Variational Quantum Tomography (VQT). A variant of VQT, named VQT$_\infty$, has been introduced to reproduce MaxEnt-like solutions. In this work, we generalize this approach by introducing a parametrized cost function that interpolates between the 1-norm and the infinity norm, thereby unifying VQT and VQT$_\infty$ within a single framework. By tuning the associated hyperparameters, the proposed method enables controlled exploration of the set of compatible density matrices. We show that this interplay yields reconstructed states with higher fidelity to the MaxEnt solution than those obtained via VQT$_\infty$ while preserving computational tractability.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27284"></a>
### [Quantum Anonymous Secret Sharing with Permutation Invariant Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27284v1)

**Authors:** Varin Sikand, Andrew Nemec  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27284v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Overview of ANONQ(|ϕ⟩) (Protocol II.4) with Alice encircled in green and Bob in blue. Each participant holds one qubit of the n-qubit GHZ state, denoted as σi, and all participants besides Alice and Bob apply an H gate to their qubit, leaving only Alice and Bob with an entangled pair. The green arrow represents the teleportation step after the AE protocol.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Overview of the QASS protocol. We initially assume the secret has been encoded into n shares. In Step 1, each green arrow denotes a share sent from each k participating shareholders via the ANONQ protocol. Once received by Bob, the identities of each share is entirely lost due to ANONQ. Finally, Bob decodes the anonymous set of retrieved shares using the PI code’s decoding circuit to obtain the secret.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Hmin for QASS schemes using the ((4, 2, 2)) Aydin et al. PI code [46] and the ((4, 2, 2)) Hagiwara and Nakayama PI code [13], the ramp QSS scheme using the [[4, 1, 2]] Leung et al. stabilizer code [44], and the HQASS scheme using Aydin et al. PI code [46] for both quantum and classical secrets. All three 4-qubit QSS schemes have identical Hmin values, whereas the HQASS scheme has a threshold of k = 3 with no intermediate share sets.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Comparison of Hmin across various PI codes of distance 3. (a) The ((7, 2, 3)) codes of Pollatsek and Ruskai [47], [49] and Aydin et al. [46], which have the same Hmin values. (b) The ((9, 2, 3)) code of Ruskai [48] (the gnu (3, 3, 1)-PI code [50]). (c) Three ((11, 2, 3)) codes including the one given by Kubischta and Teixeira [49], the shifted gnu (3, 3, 1)-PI code of Ouyang [45], and the Q4,1,2,−1 code of Aydin et al. [46]. (d) Two ((13, 2, 3)) codes including one given by Kubischta and Teixeira [49] and the shifted gnu (3, 3, 1)-PI code of Ouyang [45].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a new protocol for quantum secret sharing that protects the identity of the sender using permutation-invariant codes. It also introduces a mathematically rigorous way to measure how much information an adversary can steal from partial shares using quantum min-entropy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing Quantum Secret Sharing (QSS) schemes lack sender-anonymity and lack a robust single-shot metric to quantify information leakage in 'ramp' schemes where intermediate shares provide partial information.

**Main result.** The authors propose a Quantum Anonymous Secret Sharing (QASS) protocol using permutation-invariant (PI) codes that achieves sender-anonymity and can tolerate missing shareholders. They also establish quantum conditional min-entropy as a valid single-shot metric for quantifying information leakage.

**Method.** The protocol combines PI quantum error-correcting codes with anonymous quantum transmission algorithms (based on GHZ states) and utilizes the cleaning lemma and maximum likelihood decoders to evaluate security metrics.

**Model / system.** The study uses quantum error-correcting codes (specifically stabilizer and permutation-invariant codes) operating under erasure and depolarizing channels, utilizing n-qubit GHZ states for the transmission protocol.

**Key observables.** Quantum conditional min-entropy (Hmin), maximum fidelity (F), and quantum correlation (q_corr).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code parameters (n, K, d), threshold (k), number of erasures, and the number of participating shareholders (np).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes the use of permutation-invariant codes and an unbounded adversary who can corrupt up to n-2 players; it also assumes players are either honest, malicious, or corrupted.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the ANONQ protocol steps; Figure 2 illustrates the QASS protocol flow; Figure 3 shows fidelity and min-entropy for 4-qubit codes; Figure 4 compares min-entropy across various larger PI codes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of anonymity and leakage, describes the construction of the QASS protocol using PI codes, provides a theoretical justification for the min-entropy metric via Knill-Laflamme conditions, and concludes with numerical evaluations of various code sizes and erasure models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum secret sharing schemes are a family of quantum cryptographic protocols which provide secure quantum encodings, mapping one secret to multiple shares of information such that the original secret cannot be accessed without an authorized set of shares present for decoding. In this work, we describe a protocol that enables sender-anonymity during the secret decoding process. By using permutation-invariant QEC codes along with a set of anonymous quantum transmission algorithms, we construct a quantum anonymous secret sharing scheme that achieves sender-anonymity. We quantify information leakage in ramp quantum secret sharing schemes via the quantum conditional min-entropy, justifying it as a valid measure of leaked information by relating it to the Knill-Laflamme quantum error correction conditions. Finally, we evaluate several permutation-invariant codes using this measure to make observations on the information leakage of intermediate shares for each quantum anonymous secret sharing scheme.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27885"></a>
### [Quantum integrable matrix models of spinor Bose gases in one spatial dimension](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27885v1)

**Authors:** Hannes Köper, Thomas Gasenzer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27885v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a generalized integrable matrix model to describe 1D spinor Bose gases with arbitrary spin. By applying the Bethe-ansatz, the authors map out the phase diagram of a spin-1 gas and demonstrate how bound states alter the statistical behavior of interacting bosons.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful integrable framework for studying the many-body dynamics and phase transitions of complex spin multiplets in 1D, which is highly relevant for understanding the physics of ultracold atomic gases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to generalize the description of one-dimensional spinor Bose gases to arbitrary spin multiplets using a matrix-based integrable model.

**Main result.** The authors derive a quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model and compute the ground state phase diagram for a spin-1 Bose gas, revealing that paired bound states modify the effective Pauli exclusion principle for bosons.

**Method.** The study employs algebraic Bethe-ansatz techniques to construct eigenstates and derive Bethe equations, alongside the derivation of integral equations for thermodynamic properties.

**Model / system.** A quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model where fundamental fields are represented by an m x n matrix of bosonic field operators, specifically applied to spin-1 Bose gases with repulsive density-density and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin interactions.

**Key observables.** Ground state phase diagram, spectra of conserved quantities, and equilibrium thermodynamic properties.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potential, external magnetic field, and the Lieb parameter (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is restricted to one spatial dimension and assumes repulsive density-density interaction and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model, applies the algebraic Bethe-ansatz for arbitrary spin multiplets, focuses on the spin-1 case to derive thermodynamic integral equations, and concludes with a phase diagram analysis and discussion of bound state effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Degenerate spinor Bose gases with repulsive density-density interaction and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin coupling in one spatial dimension are shown to be described by a quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model, whose fundamental fields are described by an $m\,\times\,n$ matrix of bosonic field operators. The eigenstates of this model are constructed for arbitrarily sized matrix field operators by means of algebraic Bethe-ansatz techniques, and the corresponding Bethe equations governing the spectra of conserved quantities are derived. The approach thus generalizes previously chosen techniques to account for arbitrary spin multiplets and their spin-spin interaction. Focusing on the specific case of the $2\times2$ model, which is shown to correspond to a spin-$1$ Bose gas, a set of integral equations is derived, which describe its equilibrium thermodynamic properties. From these, the ground state phase diagram is computed both, numerically and analytically in the parameter plane spanned by the chemical potential and an external magnetic field. Furthermore, the existence of paired bound states is shown to modify the Pauli exclusion principle for interacting bosons in one dimension. In particular, it is found that no two quasiparticle rapidities can coincide, provided that the Lieb parameter satisfies $γ>4/3$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28160"></a>
### [Reorganizing Quantum Measurement Records Improves Time-Series Prediction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28160v1)

**Authors:** Markus Baumann, Maximilian Zorn, Thomas Gabor, Claudia Linnhoff-Popien, Jonas Stein  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28160v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28160_figures/2604.28160_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Three ways of organizing shot-based training data under a fixed measurement budget: EV averaging, split-ensemble training, and raw stacking.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.28160_figures/2604.28160_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Shared operating point (Q = 4, Nshots = 18, λ = 10, ρEV = 2.65125). Top: illustrative test trajectories, where the displayed seed is the one whose split-ensemble test NRMSE is closest to the 20-seed median. Bottom: test outcomes over all 20 seeds.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.28160_figures/2604.28160_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Baseline and estimator controls at the shared operating point. The compared methods are EV, Raw, EV-dup, EV-NA, split-ensemble, and Split- NA.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.28160_figures/2604.28160_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. EV-minus-split-ensemble test gap ∆as a function of ρEV across the common Nshots sweep. Points show individual seeds; curves and bands summarize the 20-seed mean and spread. Positive values favor split-ensemble.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.28160_figures/2604.28160_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6. Duplication control over the broader Nshots sweep. Duplication matches the split-ensemble example count by repeating the fully averaged feature vector, without creating distinct grouped views.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a simple classical algorithmic lever called 'split-ensemble training' to improve the performance of near-term quantum machine learning. By reorganizing existing measurement shots into intermediate groups, it achieves better time-series prediction accuracy without requiring any additional quantum hardware executions. This method effectively balances the trade-off between reducing shot noise and maintaining a sufficient number of training examples.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to optimize the organization of finite-shot measurement records in Quantum Reservoir Computing to improve time-series prediction without increasing the quantum execution budget.

**Main result.** The proposed 'split-ensemble training' method, which partitions shots into intermediate groups, outperforms both standard expectation-value averaging and raw shot stacking, especially when the readout dimension is large relative to the number of training examples.

**Method.** A split-ensemble training approach that partitions N shots into disjoint groups of size k, creating partially denoised feature vectors to balance the trade-off between sampling noise and training data scarcity.

**Model / system.** A Quantum Reservoir Computing (QRC) framework using a fixed Q-qubit circuit (e.g., ring-CNOT entanglers) as a dynamical system, with classical post-processing via leaky integration and a linear ridge regression readout.

**Key observables.** Single-qubit observables in the Z and X bases, processed into integrated feature vectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of shots (N_shots), group size (k), feature-to-dimension ratio (rho_EV), leakage parameter (eta), and regularization strength (lambda).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The total quantum execution budget (total number of shots) remains constant; the study assumes a fixed quantum circuit architecture and focuses on the classical reorganization of data.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the three data organization strategies; Figure 2 shows NRMSE results for various time-series benchmarks; Figure 3 shows the test gap between methods; Figure 5 visualizes NRMSE curves across different reservoir sizes; Figure 6 compares split-ensemble to a duplication control; Figure 9 shows sensitivity to ridge regularization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of shot-noise vs. data scarcity, proposes the split-ensemble method, provides theoretical propositions for the benefit of grouping, presents numerical benchmarks on various time-series tasks, validates the method via controls (EV-dup, EV-NA), demonstrates scalability with reservoir size, and concludes with hardware validation on IBM hardware.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Near-term quantum computers are accessed through repeated circuit executions, which produce finite measurement records rather than exact deterministic outputs. In quantum reservoir computing, these records are converted to feature vectors for a classical readout. The standard expectation-value approach averages all shots from one labeled time step into a single feature vector. This reduces finite-shot noise, but it also gives the readout only one training example from many circuit executions. We introduce split-ensemble training: the same shots are split into groups, and each group average is used as a separate, partially denoised feature vector for the same target. The quantum circuit, task, and measurement budget remain unchanged. Across simulated forecasting benchmarks and real hardware experiments, this simple reorganization improves prediction when full averaging leaves the readout with too few training examples, with the strongest gains observed on hardware. Our results establish shot-record organization as a simple, broadly applicable algorithmic lever for improving near-term quantum learning without additional quantum hardware cost.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27836"></a>
### [Towards High Performance Quantum Computing (HPQ): Parallelisation of the Hamiltonian Auto Decomposition Optimisation Framework (HADOF)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27836v1)

**Authors:** Namasi G Sankar, Georgios Miliotis, Simon Caton  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27836v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Standard QAOA circuit with alternating cost and mixer Hamiltonians[34]. The output produces a probability distribution over the solution space which can be samples with the shots parameter of the quantum simulation or backend. Higher sampled solutions are more likely to be solutions with better objective value.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Trotterised QAOA parameters based on Ref [34], [35]. We move βm from 1 to 0 and γm from 0 to 1 allowing the system to stay close to the ground state of the mixer Hamiltonian to the Cost Hamiltonian.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: General overview of the HADOF framework. Here, we use QAOA as the optimiser, which is called iteratively. (1) We choose subsets of sizes 5 from the binary variables of the global problem. (2) These are used to form the sub-Hamiltonians using P(xi), approximated as the expected value of each qubit. (3) The QAOA circuit set up with t = 1 layers and in every iteration we add a layer. In this study, we use 5 layers. (4) Once the n/k sub-Hamiltonians are optimised, we sample them and use an aggregation policy to form the global solution probability distribution. This can be done sequentially or in parallel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Simulating quantum circuit dynamics is hard classically, however with HADOF, problems up to size 500 variables are simulated in a comparable time to classical Simulated Annealing. Time advantage can be observed in HADOF simulation when running in parallel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 and Fig. 5 show the wall clock time to solution for the different algorithms. In the ideal simulation setting (Fig. 4), both sequential and parallel HADOF exhibit approximately linear scaling with respect to the number of binary variables. However, the parallel implementation consistently achieves lower wall clock times, with the performance gap widening as the problem size increases. For instance, at 500 variables, parallel HADOF achieves roughly a 3× speedup over the sequential variant. This indicates that parallelisation not only reduces constant overheads but also improves the effective scaling coefficient, suggesting strong suitability for large-scale QUBO instances. In...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a parallelized version of the HADOF framework to solve large-scale combinatorial optimization problems by decomposing them into smaller sub-problems. By utilizing multiple quantum processors simultaneously, the authors demonstrate significant reductions in computation time without sacrificing solution quality. This work provides a practical pathway toward High Performance Quantum (HPQ) computing for real-world applications like genomics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scalability of quantum optimization algorithms like QAOA is limited by the low qubit counts, noise, and connectivity of NISQ-era hardware, making large-scale combinatorial problems like genome assembly difficult to solve.

**Main result.** The parallelized HADOF framework achieves up to a 3-4x reduction in wall-clock time on real IBM QPUs and up to a 5x speedup in simulations, while maintaining competitive solution accuracy for genome assembly.

**Method.** The researchers extended the HADOF framework to enable parallel execution across single and multiple QPUs using asynchronous job orchestration and sub-Hamiltonian decomposition.

**Model / system.** The study uses Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) models, specifically applied to genome assembly via overlap graphs, and benchmarks on IBM Quantum processors (e.g., IBM_Kingston) and classical PennyLane simulations.

**Key observables.** Wall-clock time (makespan), solution accuracy (relative to Simulated Annealing), and probability distribution of the sampled solutions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sub-Hamiltonian size (k=5), QAOA circuit depth (L=5), number of qubits (up to 500 in simulation, 10-50 on hardware), and number of QPUs used for parallelization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that large problems can be effectively decomposed into smaller, independent sub-problems that can be recombined via expected values from previous iterations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a standard QAOA circuit; Figure 2 illustrates the Trotterized QAOA parameter schedule; Figure 3 provides an overview of the HADOF framework; Figures 4-5 compare wall-clock time for sequential vs. parallel execution in ideal and noisy settings; Figure 6 shows solution accuracy; Figure 8-9 illustrate the genome assembly overlap graph and reconstructed sequence.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability bottleneck, describes the HADOF decomposition framework, presents the new parallelization strategy, details the experimental setup on IBM hardware and simulations, validates the approach with a genome assembly case study, and discusses future directions for High Performance Quantum computing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Practical applicability of quantum optimisation on near term devices is constrained by limited qubit counts and hardware noise, which restricts the scalability of quantum optimisation algorithms for combinatorial problems. The simulation of large quantum circuits is also difficult and constrained by memory requirement. The Hamiltonian Auto Decomposition Optimisation Framework (HADOF) addresses this by decomposing large QUBOs into smaller subproblems that can be solved iteratively on quantum or classical backends. This allows the scalability of quantum QUBO algorithms beyond device limits, as well as their simulation on classical devices. In this research, we extend the evaluation of HADOF by benchmarking on real IBM QPUs across sequential, single-QPU parallel, and multi-QPU parallel execution modes, advancing toward High Performance Quantum (HPQ) computing for combinatorial optimisation problems.   Experimental results on IBM quantum hardware demonstrate up to 3-4x reduction in wall clock time when utilising four QPUs compared to sequential execution baseline, while maintaining comparable solution quality. Notably, even single QPU execution benefits from parallelised job orchestration and execution, yielding up to 3x speedup. Simulated results predict over 5x speed-up in parallel execution mode. We further validate the practical applicability of the approach on real world genome assembly instances, showing that both sequential and parallel HADOF variants achieve competitive accuracy while significantly improving time to solution. These results highlight the importance of parallelism at both the algorithmic and system levels, positioning HADOF as a viable pathway toward scalable quantum optimisation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27886"></a>
### [Unentangled stoquastic Merlin-Arthur proof systems: the power of unentanglement without destructive interference](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27886v1)

**Authors:** Yupan Liu, Pei Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27886v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27886_figures/2604.27886_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Relationships among StoqMA(2), its variants, and other complexity classes.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27886_figures/2604.27886_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Conjunction of stoquastic verifiers V (1) x1 , V (2) x2 , and V (3) x3 .</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores the complexity of quantum proof systems where the witnesses are unentangled but the system is stoquastic (sign-problem-free). It reveals that unentanglement can provide significant computational power even without the destructive interference typically found in general quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the computational complexity and structural properties of StoqMA(2), a complexity class defined by unentangled (separable) quantum proofs in sign-problem-free (stoquastic) systems.

**Main result.** The authors establish that StoqMA(2) is surprisingly powerful, containing NP and being contained in EXP, and show that for negligible completeness error, StoqMA(k) collapses to StoqMA(2).

**Method.** The study employs distribution testing techniques to derive lower bounds, a new rectangular closure testing framework for upper bounds, and the Sum-of-Squares (SoS) algorithm for complexity limits.

**Model / system.** The model focuses on stoquastic Hamiltonians, which are characterized by real, non-positive off-diagonal matrix elements, and unentangled witness states across multiple registers.

**Key observables.** Acceptance probabilities of the verifier, completeness and soundness errors, and the presence of destructive interference (or lack thereof).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Completeness and soundness error regimes, number of provers (k), witness length (qubit scale), and the promise gap.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the absence of destructive interference (stoquasticity) and relies on the Exponential Time Hypothesis (ETH) for establishing the optimality of certain algorithms.

**Figures summary.** A summary diagram illustrating the relationships and new inclusions between various complexity classes like NP, MA, QMA(2), and StoqMA(2).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the StoqMA(2) class, establishes lower bounds via distribution testing, provides upper bounds using SoS and rectangular closure testing, and concludes with structural results regarding error reduction and prover collapse.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stoquasticity, originating in sign-problem-free physical systems, gives rise to $\sf StoqMA$, introduced by Bravyi, Bessen, and Terhal (2006), a quantum-inspired intermediate class between $\sf MA$ and $\sf AM$. Unentanglement similarly gives rise to ${\sf QMA}(2)$, introduced by Kobayashi, Matsumoto, and Yamakami (CJTCS 2009), which generalizes $\sf QMA$ to two unentangled proofs and still has only the trivial $\sf NEXP$ upper bound. In this work, we initiate a systematic study of the power of unentanglement without destructive interference via ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$, the class of unentangled stoquastic Merlin-Arthur proof systems. Although $\sf StoqMA$ is semi-quantum and may collapse to $\sf MA$, ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$ turns out to be surprisingly powerful. We establish the following results:   - ${\sf NP} \subseteq {\sf StoqMA}(2)$ with $\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{n})$-qubit proofs and completeness error $2^{-{\rm polylog}(n)}$. Conversely, ${\sf StoqMA}(2) \subseteq {\sf EXP}$ via the Sum-of-Squares algorithm of Barak, Kelner, and Steurer (STOC 2014); with our lower bound, our refined analysis yields the optimality of this algorithm under ETH.   - ${\sf StoqMA}(2)_1 \subseteq {\sf PSPACE}$, and the containment holds with completeness error $2^{-2^{{\rm poly}(n)}}$.   - ${\sf PreciseStoqMA}(2)$, a variant of ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$ with exponentially small promise gap, cannot achieve perfect completeness unless ${\sf EXP}={\sf NEXP}$. In contrast, ${\sf PreciseStoqMA}$ achieves perfect completeness, since ${\sf PSPACE} \subseteq {\sf PreciseStoqMA}_1$.   - When the completeness error is negligible, ${\sf StoqMA}(k) = {\sf StoqMA}(2)$ for $k\geq 2$.   Our lower bounds are obtained by stoquastizing the short-proof ${\sf QMA}(2)$ protocols via distribution testing techniques. Our upper bounds for the nearly perfect completeness case are proved via our new rectangular closure testing framework.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27782"></a>
### [Explicit Quantum Search Algorithm for the Densest k-Subgraph Problem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27782v1)

**Authors:** Yu. A. Biriukov, R. D. Morozov, I. V. Dyakonov, S. S. Straupe  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27782v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Dicke superradiance` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1: General scheme of a single Grover-based search call at threshold mi.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27782_figures/2604.27782_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Gate scheme for oracle.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3: Diffusion operator</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27782_figures/2604.27782_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Benchmark graph with n = 10 vertices used in the convergence experiments.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27782_figures/2604.27782_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Convergence of the best-so-far edge count for the four algorithms on the benchmark graph from Fig. 4. From left to right: k = 4, 5, 6. Solid lines show the mean over 1000 independent runs, and shaded regions indicate empirical 90% confidence intervals.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new quantum algorithm for the NP-hard densest k-subgraph problem using Grover's search. By utilizing Dicke states and a QFT-based oracle, the method avoids the complexities of QUBO penalty terms and provides a provable quadratic speedup over classical brute-force methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Finding the densest k-vertex subgraph in an undirected graph, which is an NP-hard combinatorial optimization problem.

**Main result.** The authors propose an explicit gate-based Grover-based quantum search algorithm that achieves a provable quadratic speedup over classical brute-force search.

**Method.** The approach uses Dicke state preparation to represent the search space, a QFT-based oracle for edge counting, and an iterative thresholding strategy using the Durr-Hoyer modification.

**Model / system.** The computational model is a circuit-model quantum computer utilizing Dicke states |D^n_k> and Quantum Fourier Transform (QFT) based arithmetic for edge counting.

**Key observables.** Oracle calls required to find the densest subgraph and the scaling exponent of the oracle cost relative to the search space size.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of vertices (n), subgraph size (k), edge density (0.5 for Erdős-Rényi graphs), and the initial threshold (m0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The algorithm assumes the availability of efficient Dicke state preparation and a gate-based architecture capable of performing QFT-based arithmetic.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Grover search iteration scheme; Figure 2 details the QFT-based oracle gate scheme; Figure 5 shows convergence of edge counts; Figure 6 and 7 demonstrate the power-law scaling and quadratic speedup compared to classical baselines.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the DkS problem, reviews existing QUBO/annealing methods, introduces the new Grover-based architecture (Dicke states, QFT oracle, and diffusion operator), details the iterative thresholding process, and concludes with numerical scaling analysis and comparisons to classical heuristics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper addresses the problem of finding the densest $k$-vertex subgraph in an arbitrary graph. This problem is NP-hard and has important applications in social network analysis, fraud detection, recommendation systems, and bioinformatics. We propose two quantum approaches to solve this problem: reduction to Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) and using Grover's quantum search algorithm. For the latter approach, we present an explicit gate-based oracle circuit utilizing Dicke states and Quantum Fourier Transform for edge counting. Numerical simulations demonstrate a quadratic speedup over classical Brute-force search.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Magnetic Quantum Criticality inside the Superconducting State Revealed by Penetration Depth Scaling with Local $T_{\mathrm c}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27507v1)

**Authors:** Yusuke Iguchi, Kaede Inoh, Ryosuke Koizumi, Makoto Yokoyama

**Summary.** This study uses scanning SQUID microscopy to reveal a magnetic quantum critical point hidden within the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn5. By using local transition temperature as a tuning parameter, the authors demonstrate that the quantum critical behavior is modified by disorder, evidenced by a peak in the magnetic penetration depth and an increased critical exponent.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the microscopic origin of the antiferromagnetic quantum critical point (AF-QCP) embedded within the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn5 and distinguishing intrinsic critical behavior from disorder-induced broadening.

**Main result.** The researchers identified a pronounced peak in the magnetic penetration depth at zero temperature near the AF-QCP and found that the extracted critical exponent exceeds the clean spin-density-wave value, suggesting a disorder-modified quantum critical regime.

**Method.** Scanning SQUID microscopy was used to perform local magnetometry and susceptometry, allowing for the extraction of local Tc and penetration depth by parameterizing data with local Tc rather than nominal doping.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the heavy-fermion superconductor CeCo(In1-xZnx)5 in the low-doping regime, where Zn substitution induces antiferromagnetic order and competes with superconductivity.

**Key observables.** Magnetic penetration depth lambda(0), local superconducting transition temperature Tc, Néel temperature TN, and magnetic susceptibility chi.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical exponent nu, impurity scattering strength Gamma, and the temperature dependence of paramagnetic susceptibility alpha.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that using local Tc as a tuning parameter can circumvent the ambiguity of doping inhomogeneity, and certain data points were excluded due to extreme microscopic inhomogeneity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows phase diagrams of Tc versus effective impurity scattering, the relationship between Tc and TN, and a comparison of penetration depth data against quantum critical scaling models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of magnetism in doped CeCoIn5, describes the scanning SQUID experimental setup, presents the local Tc and penetration depth measurements, performs scaling analysis to extract critical exponents, and concludes with the implications for disorder-modified quantum criticality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate a magnetic quantum critical point embedded within the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn$_5$, revealed by a pronounced peak in the magnetic penetration depth at zero temperature $λ(0)$. Using scanning SQUID microscopy, we determine the local superconducting transition temperature $T_{\mathrm c}$ and $λ(0)$. By parameterizing $λ(0)$ in terms of the local $T_{\mathrm c}$ rather than nominal Zn substitution, we circumvent the ambiguity caused by doping inhomogeneity and enable a more precise extraction of the critical exponent. The extracted exponent exceeds the clean spin-density-wave value, indicating a disorder-modified quantum critical regime. The enhancement of $λ(0)$ reflects the suppression of the superfluid stiffness and is consistent with critical scaling. Our approach provides a route to uncover intrinsic quantum critical behavior hidden by inhomogeneity in unconventional superconductors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27110"></a>
### [Beyond Project-Based Learning: Conference-Style Writing as Authentic Assessment in Interdisciplinary Quantum Engineering Education](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27110v1)

**Authors:** Nischal Binod Gautam, Enrique P. Blair  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27110v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27110_figures/2604.27110_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: The semester-long project timeline shows how topic selection, literature review, midpoint feedback, implementation or simulation, and presentation progressively culminate in a conference-style paper as an integrated final synthesis rather than an isolated end-of-course assignment.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27110_figures/2604.27110_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: The venn diagram illustrates the complementary roles of project work, conference-style writing, and oral presentation in the course, where their overlap represents the integrated development of technical understanding, scholarly communi- cation, and authentic research practice resulting in a rehearsed scholar.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper investigates replacing traditional project reports with conference-style manuscripts in quantum engineering courses. It finds that while this increases student workload, it significantly enhances their ability to communicate complex quantum concepts and prepares them for professional research environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Evaluating whether a conference-style writing requirement serves as an effective 'authentic assessment' tool to develop professional research and communication skills in interdisciplinary quantum engineering education.

**Main result.** Students found the conference-style paper demanding but highly beneficial for developing scientific communication, research readiness, and technical confidence, suggesting the model should be retained with improved scaffolding.

**Method.** An exploratory, classroom-based study using post-course surveys (Likert-scale and open-ended) from a pilot run of a graduate-level quantum mechanics for engineers course.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on a pedagogical framework within an introductory quantum mechanics course for engineers, integrating physics, mathematics, computing, and electronics.

**Key observables.** Student perceptions of conceptual understanding, scientific communication skills, research readiness, engagement, and workload.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the primary goal of the course is to prepare students for authentic disciplinary research practice and relies on student self-reporting.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a semester-long timeline of project progression; Figure 2 is a Venn diagram illustrating the overlap between project work, writing, and oral presentation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of authentic assessment in PBL, reviews existing literature on PBL artifacts, describes the instructional design and survey methodology, presents results regarding student skill gains and workload, and concludes with recommendations for pedagogical scaffolding.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Project-based learning is recognized as an effective approach for improving engagement and applied understanding in STEM education. In quantum engineering courses, however, the question is no longer only whether students benefit from projects but how those projects should culminate if the goal is authentic disciplinary preparation. This paper examines the educational role of a conference-style paper requirement embedded within a project-based learning implementation for an introductory quantum mechanics course for engineers. We use post-course survey responses from students in a pilot run of the course. We evaluate perceived effects on conceptual understanding, scientific communication, research readiness, and attitudes toward the writing requirement itself. The results suggest that students viewed the project as beneficial for engagement, confidence, and technical skill development, while the conference-style paper emerged as a demanding but meaningful component of the experience. We argue that once PBL has been established in quantum mechanics education, conference-style writing can serve as an extension of that model, especially for graduate students. The findings support retaining the conference-paper requirement with improved scaffolding.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27788"></a>
### [Chern number reversal and emergent superconductivity in rhombohedral graphene induced by in-plane magnetic fields](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27788v1)

**Authors:** Xiaozhou Zan, Hangzhe Li, Jiawei Guo, Gengdong Zhou, Kangyao Chen, Cihan Gao, Zijun Xu, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Anqi Wang, Jie Shen, Jinsong Zhang, Zhida Song, Yayu Wang  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27788v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper explores the emergence of topological and superconducting phases in eight-layer rhombohedral graphene. It demonstrates that in-plane magnetic fields can reverse the Chern number and induce new superconducting phases, providing evidence for spin-triplet pairing. The findings highlight the system's potential as a tunable platform for novel quantum electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the interplay between topological states and unconventional superconductivity in eight-layer rhombohedral graphene under in-plane magnetic fields.

**Main result.** The study observes Chern number reversal in the QAH state and identifies three distinct superconducting phases with varying responses to in-plane magnetic fields, including a field-induced phase suggesting spin-triplet pairing.

**Method.** Transport measurements on hBN-aligned eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices using displacement fields and in-plane magnetic fields as control parameters.

**Model / system.** Eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices aligned with hexagonal boron nitride (hBN), characterized by topological flat bands.

**Key observables.** Chern number, superconducting phase transitions, and response to in-plane magnetic fields and displacement fields.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electron-doped and hole-doped carrier densities, displacement fields, and in-plane magnetic field strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the rhombohedral graphene platform, details the observation of Chern number reversal in the QAH regime, analyzes the three distinct superconducting phases under in-plane magnetic fields, and concludes with the implications for spin-triplet pairing and device engineering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rhombohedral graphene with topological flat bands offers an ideal platform for realizing correlated and topological quantum phases. Here we investigate hBN aligned eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices, which host a robust quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) state alongside three unconventional superconducting phases. For electron-doped carriers away from the moire potential, we observe QAH Chern number reversal driven by the displacement fields and in plane magnetic fields. For hole-doped carriers near the moire superlattice, the three superconducting phases exhibit distinctively different in plane magnetic field responses: one is weakly enhanced, the second is strongly suppressed, and the third exclusively induced by in plane magnetic field. The isotropic in plane magnetic field response in the QAH regime points to interplay between orbital magnetism and spin-orbit coupling, and the field-emergent superconductivity provides compelling evidence for spin-triplet pairing. Our work demonstrates a highly versatile platform for coexisting topological and superconducting states, and highlights in plane magnetic field as a powerful in-situ control knob for engineering novel quantum devices.

</details>

### [Clifft: Fast Exact Simulation of Near-Clifford Quantum Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27058v1)

**Authors:** Bradley A. Chase, Farrokh Labib  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27058v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27058_figures/2604.27058_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Lifecycle of an active operation in the frame-factored representation. Physical Clifford gates are absorbed into the offline Clifford frame UC, while a non-Clifford operation is Heisenberg mapped into a virtual Pauli rotation. Pauli localization uses virtual Clifford transformations to reduce the mapped multi-qubit Pauli string to a single virtual axis. The resulting localized operation either updates the active state vector or promotes a dormant virtual qubit into the active set, increasing k. Later projective measurements can collapse an active axis and return it to the dormant set, decreasing k. Stochastic Pauli noise and feed-forward corrections update only the virtual Pauli...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27058_figures/2604.27058_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: The high-level Clifft execution pipeline. The compiler resolves Clifford-coordinate evolution, HIR optimization, Pauli localization, and active-space planning once. The SVM then samples the compiled bytecode many times over the frame-factored runtime state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27058_figures/2604.27058_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Single-shot execution time vs. qubit count N for random Quantum Volume circuits at depth D = N, run on a 16-core CPU. This dense-limit benchmark corresponds to the worst case kmax = N, where Clifft no longer benefits from near-Clifford structure. Even in this regime, Clifft tracks the leading dense state vector simulators within a small constant factor up to N = 28; its near-Clifford speedups are shown separately in Table 1.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2604.27058_figures/2604.27058_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6: Inject + cultivate stage: T-gate vs S-proxy logical error rate per kept shot, estimated using stratified importance sampling across uniform circuit-level depolarizing noise. Shaded regions in the top panels show absolute error-rate values within a factor of 1000 in likelihood relative to the maximum-likelihood estimate, following the convention used by Sinter for rare-event visualization. Shaded regions in the bottom panels show 95% Bayesian credible intervals for the T/S ratio, computed as described in Appendix D. For d = 5, we also show the SOFT results from Table III in Li et al. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.27058_figures/2604.27058_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 7: End-to-end magic state cultivation including the escape stage to a d = 15 surface code. Top panels: conservative infidelity estimate per kept shot after decoder correction for the true T-gate circuit (Clifft) and the S-proxy baseline (Gidney et al. [9]). Note that the credible intervals for d = 5 cultivation extend beyond the plotted range due to low rare-event statistics.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The authors present Clifft, an open-source simulator that accelerates the exact simulation of near-Clifford circuits by focusing computational effort on a small, dynamic active subspace. This allows for the first large-scale, end-to-end simulation of complex error correction protocols like magic state cultivation. The work provides new insights into the error discrepancies between true T-gates and their S-gate approximations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Exact classical simulation of fault-tolerant quantum circuits is bottlenecked by exponential scaling with total qubit count, T-count, or per-shot tracking overhead.

**Main result.** The Clifft simulator achieves orders-of-magnitude throughput gains in the near-Clifford regime by scaling complexity with a dynamic active dimension rather than total qubit count, enabling the first end-to-end simulation of large-scale magic state cultivation.

**Method.** A frame-factored representation that decouples the quantum state into an offline Clifford frame, an online Pauli frame, and a dynamically sized active state vector using Pauli localization.

**Model / system.** Near-Clifford quantum circuits, specifically focusing on fault-tolerant protocols like Magic State Cultivation (MSC) and surface/color codes subject to circuit-level depolarizing or coherent noise.

**Key observables.** Logical error rates, T/S ratio (discrepancy between T-gate and S-proxy), and logical Pauli expectation values (e.g., <YL>).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total qubit count (N), peak active virtual dimension (k_max), error probability (p), and decoder-gap threshold.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The efficiency is highly dependent on the active subspace size remaining small; the method is optimized for the low-magic/near-Clifford regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the lifecycle of an active operation; Figure 2 details the Clifft pipeline; Figure 4 illustrates bytecode optimization; Figure 6 and 7 show T/S ratio discrepancies and end-to-end error rates as a function of decoder thresholds.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the simulation bottleneck, presents the Clifft architecture (compiler, HIR, and SVM), provides mathematical proofs for Pauli localization and frame-factored stability, benchmarks performance against existing tools (Stim, Tsim, SOFT), and applies the simulator to analyze magic state cultivation protocols.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exact classical simulation of fault-tolerant quantum circuits remains limited by a tradeoff between exponential state vector scaling, exponential $T$-count scaling in stabilizer-rank approaches, and per-shot tracking overhead in sparse generalized stabilizer simulators. In this work, we introduce Clifft, an open-source simulator that shifts the dominant exponential cost from the total qubit count to a dynamic active subspace by factoring the quantum state into an offline Clifford frame, an online Pauli frame, and a dynamically sized active state vector. This architecture resolves deterministic Clifford coordinate transformations ahead of time, generalizing Stim's compile-once, sample-many execution model to circuits with non-Clifford operations. Consequently, exponential simulation costs are determined by the peak active virtual dimension, which expands during non-Clifford operations and contracts during measurements. Clifft remains within a constant factor of standard tools in the pure-Clifford and non-Clifford limits, while delivering up to orders-of-magnitude throughput gains over GPU-accelerated near-Clifford simulators on low-magic fault-tolerant benchmarks. Executing on commodity CPUs and exposing a Stim-like API, Clifft enables, to our knowledge, the first exact end-to-end simulation of magic state cultivation including the escape stage, over hundreds of billions of shots. These simulations show that escape-stage failures suppress the discrepancy between the true $T$-gate circuit and its $S$-proxy at low decoder-gap thresholds, while at high thresholds the full-protocol behavior approaches the larger discrepancy observed in the cultivation stages alone.

</details>

### [Defending Quantum Classifiers against Adversarial Perturbations through Quantum Autoencoders](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28176v1)

**Authors:** Emma Andrews, Sahan Sanjaya, Prabhat Mishra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28176v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Fig. 3: Classical autoencoder structure.</sub>

<sub>Fig. 4: Quantum autoencoder structure.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2604.28176_figures/2604.28176_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6: An overview of our defense framework. A sample, either clean or adversarial, is given as input x to the QAE. The QAE produces a reconstruction ˆx which is given to the VQC for classification. The QAE also produces the encoding fidelity ⟨σZ⟩x, which is used in calculation of the confidence metric. Once the VQC has predicted the reconstruction ˆx, it produces the logit difference lˆx which is used in addition to the encoding fidelity ⟨σZ⟩x to calculate the confidence metric C. This is compared against the confidence threshold T. If the calculated confidence metric C meets the confidence threshold T, the predicted class label from the VQC is used. Otherwise, the sample is rejected as a...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new way to protect quantum machine learning models from adversarial noise without needing to retrain them on attack samples. By using a quantum autoencoder to clean the data and a diagnostic metric to reject suspicious inputs, the authors demonstrate much higher robustness in quantum classifiers.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Variational Quantum Classifiers (VQCs) are highly vulnerable to adversarial attacks like FGSM and PGD, where small perturbations cause incorrect predictions, and existing defenses like adversarial training are often impractical or prone to overfitting.

**Main result.** The proposed QAE++ framework, which uses a quantum autoencoder for purification and a confidence metric for sample rejection, significantly outperforms classical autoencoder defenses and achieves up to a 68% improvement in prediction accuracy under attack.

**Method.** An adversarial training-free defense framework that utilizes a Quantum Autoencoder (QAE) to reconstruct and purify input samples, combined with a confidence metric based on encoding fidelity and logit difference to identify and reject adversarial inputs.

**Model / system.** The study uses Variational Quantum Classifiers (VQCs) with amplitude embedding and strongly entangling layers, alongside Quantum Autoencoders (QAEs) designed to compress input states into a latent space.

**Key observables.** Encoding fidelity (measured via SWAP test), logit difference (difference between the two highest logits), and expectation values of the Pauli-Z operator.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Epsilon (perturbation magnitude), alpha (PGD step size), threshold T (for sample acceptance/rejection), and the number of qubits/parameters in the VQC.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoder is assumed to be the Hermitian conjugate of the encoder, and the trash qubits are assumed to converge to a specific reference state.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate adversarial attacks on MNIST, the architectures of VQCs and Autoencoders (both classical and quantum), the QAE++ algorithm flow, and comparative performance plots showing accuracy gains and reconstruction stability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of adversarial vulnerability, describes the QAE++ architecture and confidence metric, details the experimental setup using MNIST/FMNIST, presents comparative results against classical and undefended models, and discusses limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Machine learning models can learn from data samples to carry out various tasks efficiently. When data samples are adversarially manipulated, such as by insertion of carefully crafted noise, it can cause the model to make mistakes. Quantum machine learning models are also vulnerable to such adversarial attacks, especially in image classification using variational quantum classifiers. While there are promising defenses against these adversarial perturbations, such as training with adversarial samples, they face practical limitations. For example, they are not applicable in scenarios where training with adversarial samples is either not possible or can overfit the models on one type of attack. In this paper, we propose an adversarial training-free defense framework that utilizes a quantum autoencoder to purify the adversarial samples through reconstruction. Moreover, our defense framework provides a confidence metric to identify potentially adversarial samples that cannot be purified the quantum autoencoder. Extensive evaluation demonstrates that our defense framework can significantly outperform state-of-the-art in prediction accuracy (up to 68%) under adversarial attacks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28054"></a>
### [Dimensionality-Driven Electronic and Orbital Transitions Mediating Interfacial Magnetism in LaNiO3/CaMnO3 Observed In Situ](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28054v1)

**Authors:** B-A. Courchene, A. Hampel, S. Beck, J. R. Paudel, J. D. Grassi, L. A. Lapinski, A. M. Derrico, M. Terilli, M. Kareev, C. Klewe, A. Gloskovskii, C. Schlueter, S. K. Chaluvadi, F. Mazzola, I. Vobornik, P. Orgiani, J. Chakhalian, A. J. Millis, A. X. Gray  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28054v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the magnetic properties of LaNiO3/CaMnO3 interfaces are controlled by the thickness of the LaNiO3 layer. As the layer becomes thinner, a metal-insulator transition and orbital reconstruction occur, which suppresses the interfacial magnetism. This reveals a tunable coupling between electronic, orbital, and magnetic degrees of freedom in oxide heterostructures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how dimensional confinement and thickness reduction in LaNiO3/CaMnO3 superlattices drive electronic, orbital, and magnetic transitions at the interface.

**Main result.** Reducing LaNiO3 thickness triggers a metal-insulator transition and orbital-polarization crossover, which in turn weakens charge transfer and suppresses the interfacial Mn magnetic moment.

**Method.** The study combines in situ synthesis, polarization-dependent ARPES, X-ray magnetic circular dichroism (XMCD), and first-principles calculations using DFT combined with DMFT.

**Model / system.** LaNiO3/CaMnO3 oxide superlattices, specifically focusing on the thickness-dependent behavior of the LaNiO3 layers.

**Key observables.** Electronic coherence, orbital polarization, charge transfer across the interface, and the interfacial Mn magnetic moment.

**Important parameters / regimes.** LaNiO3 layer thickness (dimensional confinement), electronic-structure parameters, and orbital-polarization crossover.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the observed insulating state and orbital reconstruction can be accurately captured by DFT+DMFT calculations.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an investigation of oxide heterostructures through in situ synthesis, followed by spectroscopic analysis (ARPES, XMCD) and theoretical validation via DFT+DMFT to establish the coupling between electronic, orbital, and magnetic degrees of freedom.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Emergent magnetic states at oxide interfaces arise from the interplay of charge transfer, orbital reconstruction, and dimensional confinement, offering a route to engineered correlated-electron behavior in nanoscale spintronic materials. Here, we combine in situ synthesis, polarization-dependent angle-resolved photoelectron spectroscopy, X-ray magnetic circular dichroism, and first-principles electronic-structure calculations to investigate LaNiO3/CaMnO3 superlattices. We show that reducing the LaNiO3 thickness drives a metal-insulator transition accompanied by loss of electronic coherence and an orbital-polarization crossover in the ultrathin limit. These changes weaken charge transfer across the interface and suppress the interfacial Mn magnetic moment in CaMnO3, revealing that the emergent ferromagnetic state is directly governed by electronic confinement in LaNiO3. The insulating state and orbital reconstruction are reproduced by density functional theory combined with dynamical mean-field theory. Together, these results establish a direct and tunable coupling among electronic, orbital, and magnetic degrees of freedom in oxide heterostructures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27992"></a>
### [Discontinuous BBP transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27992v1)

**Authors:** Dario Bocchi, Giulio Biroli, Chiara Cammarota, Federico Ricci-Tersenghi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27992v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27992_figures/2604.27992_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Continuous vs. Discontinuous BBP transitions. Each row shows (left to right) the inverse resolvent map z(g), the spectral density near the right edge, and the eigenvector overlap as a function of the spike strength. In the continuous case, z(g) develops a turning point (dz/dg = 0) at the edge, beyond which lies the incorrect branch, the density vanishes sub-linearly, and the overlap turns on smoothly. In the discontinuous case, z(g) reaches the edge without a turning point (dz/dg ̸= 0), the density vanishes super-linearly at the edge, and the overlap exhibits a finite jump at onset.</sub>

</details>

Right column (a &gt; 2): The derivative z′(x) is finite at the domain boundary, and depending on the values of hmax and σ can be positive (mid panel), leading again to a square-root edge, or negative (bottom panel), leading...</sub>

Left column (a &lt; 2): The branch with incorrect positive derivative z′(g), diverging at the domain boundary gmax = (qfmax)−1, ensures the existence of a turning point with z′(g) = 0 and consequently a standard square-root edge. Right column (a &gt; 2): The derivative z′(g) is finite at the domain boundary, and depending on the value of q can be positive (top panel), allowing for a turning point and a square-root edge, or negative (bottom panel), leading to a super-linear edge.</sub>

</details>
