<a id="paper-2604.27304"></a>
### [Topological phase transitions in twisted bilayer graphene/hBN from interlayer coupling and substrate potentials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27304v1)

**Authors:** Huiwen Wang, Wei Jiang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27304v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper investigates how varying interlayer coupling and substrate potentials affects the topological phases in TBG/hBN. It identifies new high-Chern number states and explains the underlying band-inversion mechanisms. These findings help interpret experimental observations in moire materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to provide a systematic understanding of how the combined influence of interlayer coupling and substrate potentials drives topological phase transitions in twisted bilayer graphene/hBN.

**Main result.** The authors map out Chern number phase diagrams, revealing a rich topological landscape that includes high-Chern number states (C = 3, 4, and 5) and identifying specific band-inversion mechanisms.

**Method.** A comprehensive study using a continuum model to vary material parameters and analyze the evolution of Berry curvature.

**Model / system.** Twisted bilayer graphene aligned with hexagonal boron nitride (TBG/hBN) described by a continuum model including interlayer hopping strengths and hBN-induced potentials.

**Key observables.** Chern number, Berry curvature, and band-inversion mechanisms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interlayer hopping strengths (w0, w1), hBN-induced staggered potential, and the presence or absence of the hBN moire potential.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study relies on the validity of the continuum model for describing the moire flat bands.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a systematic study of phase diagrams, identifies specific topological transitions, and links these transitions to band-inversion mechanisms at various k-points.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Twisted bilayer graphene aligned with hexagonal boron nitride (TBG/hBN) hosts rich topological and correlated quantum phases, such as (fractional) Chern insulators, whose character is dictated by the topology of the moiré flat band. This topology is highly sensitive to several material parameters in the continuum model, yet a systematic understanding of their combined influence has been lacking. Here, we present a comprehensive study of topological phase transitions in TBG/hBN by varying the interlayer hopping strengths ($w_0, w_1$) and hBN-induced staggered potential, both with and without the hBN moiré potential. We map out Chern number phase diagrams across a broad, experimentally relevant parameter space, revealing a progressive enrichment of the topological landscape including multiple high-Chern number ($C$ = 3, 4, and 5) states. Each transition is linked to distinct band-inversion mechanisms at generic $C_3$-symmetric k points, high-symmetry momenta, or parabolic touchings, clearly reflecting in the evolution of the Berry curvature. Our results offer theoretical insights that help interpret existing experimental observations, elucidate the mechanisms driving these topological phase transitions and facilitate the exploration of topological states in TBG/hBN and related moiré systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27256"></a>
### [Towards Accelerated SCF Workflows with Equivariant Density-Matrix Learning and Analytic Refinement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27256v1)

**Authors:** Zuriel Y. Yescas-Ramos, Andrés Álvarez-García, Huziel E. Sauceda  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27256v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27256_figures/2604.27256_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. dm-PhiSNet model. (A) The PhiSNet-based backbone (gray box) builds blockwise AO 1-RDMs from molecular geometries R and atomic numbers Z, which are then refined by the analytic block (green box) to give the 1-RDM predictor. (B) Representation of the PhiSNet architecture. [25] (C) The AnalyticBlock is constructed by three sub-blocks to enforce physical consistency using the overlap matrix S: (i) trace rescaling, (ii) McWeeny purification cycle, and (iii) a final trace rescaling.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27256_figures/2604.27256_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Mean numbers of SCF iterations over 500 test configurations for all benchmarked systems. Results compare the performance of the 1-RDMs predicted by our model against the conventional SAD (atom), MINAO, and H¨uckel initial guesses. Magnitudes of the standard deviations (STD) are represented by vertical black lines on the frequency bars. Reductions with respect to the three averaged standard initial guesses are also shown.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27256_figures/2604.27256_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (A) Distributions of single-point errors in total en- ergy ∆E (left axis) and force norms ∥∆F∥(right axis) com- puted directly from the predicted 1-RDM ˆP0, for ethanol (top panel) and NO− 3 (bottom panel). (B) Conceptual integration into MD: conventional SCF versus using ˆP0 either (i) directly for an SCF-free MD step (as a CP-style electronic initializer) or (ii) as a warm start for accelerated SCF refinement for BO- MD.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces dm-PhiSNet, a machine learning model designed to accelerate quantum chemistry calculations by predicting physically consistent density matrices. By enforcing physical constraints like electron conservation, the model provides high-quality initial guesses that significantly reduce the computational time required for SCF convergence. It also demonstrates the ability to predict accurate molecular forces without direct training on force data.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Accelerating Self-Consistent Field (SCF) workflows in electronic-structure theory by generating physically admissible initial density matrix guesses.

**Main result.** The dm-PhiSNet model reduces SCF iteration counts by 49-81% compared to standard initializations and provides accurate one-shot total energies and forces without explicit force supervision.

**Method.** A hybrid architecture combining an SE(3)-equivariant PhiSNet backbone with a lightweight analytic refinement block that enforces electron-number conservation and generalized idempotency.

**Model / system.** The model predicts one-electron reduced density matrices (1-RDMs) in an atomic-orbital basis for closed-shell molecular systems including H2O, CH4, NH3, HF, ethanol, and NO3-.

**Key observables.** One-electron reduced density matrices (1-RDMs), total energies, and Hellmann-Feynman atomic forces.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Tight convergence thresholds (Energy: 10^-9 Ha, Density: 10^-8, Gradient: 10^-6 Ha/Bohr) using the DIIS solver.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on closed-shell systems within the Restricted Kohn-Sham (RKS) theory framework using a non-orthogonal AO basis.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 details the dm-PhiSNet and AnalyticBlock architectures; Figure 2 shows a bar chart of SCF iteration reductions; Figure 3 displays error distributions for energy and force norms and illustrates integration routes for molecular dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the dm-PhiSNet architecture, details the analytic refinement and two-stage training protocol, presents benchmarks on molecular systems showing SCF acceleration and force accuracy, and concludes with the scalability of the approach.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present \textsc{dm-PhiSNet}, a physically constrained \textsc{PhiSNet}-based equivariant model that predicts one-electron reduced density matrices (1-RDMs) directly from molecular geometries in an atomic-orbital (AO) basis for accelerated self-consistent field (SCF) workflows. Training follows a two-stage schedule with progressively introduced physically motivated objectives, and the resulting predictions are refined by a lightweight analytic block. This block enforces electron-number conservation, drives the 1-RDM toward generalized idempotency in the AO metric, and regularizes the occupation spectrum of the Löwdin-orthogonalized density. Across six closed-shell systems -- H$_2$O, CH$_4$, NH$_3$, HF, ethanol, and NO$_3^-$ -- the refined 1-RDMs provide SCF initial guesses that substantially reduce iteration steps by 49--81\% relative to standard initializations. Beyond SCF acceleration, the learned 1-RDMs yield accurate one-shot total energies and Hellmann--Feynman atomic forces without force supervision, indicating that the model captures chemically meaningful electronic structure. These results demonstrate that combining equivariant learning with analytic constraint enforcement provides a simple, general route to solver-ready density-matrix initializations and accelerated SCF workflows.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28145"></a>
### [Strong coupling between quantized magnon modes in a YIG microstucture and microwaves in a superconducting resonator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28145v1)

**Authors:** Seth W. Kurfman, Philipp Geyer, Anoop Kamalasanan, Karl Heimrich, Kwangyul Hu, Paul Tharnier, Frank Heyroth, Michael Flatté, Georg Schmidt  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28145v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **5/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5**

**Summary.** The study achieves strong coupling between multiple magnon modes and superconducting resonator photons in micron-scale YIG structures. By using FIB-milled microplatelets, the researchers overcame previous fabrication limits on mode volume. This provides a foundational method for efficient, low-power, on-chip magnonic quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates a platform for scalable, on-chip strong coupling between many long-lived excitations (magnons) and microwave photons, essential for hybrid quantum architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving strong coupling between magnons and microwave photons in sub-10 micron Yttrium Iron Garnet (YIG) structures has been difficult due to limited mode volume caused by YIG thickness and fabrication constraints.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated strong coupling between numerous magnon modes in a FIB-manufactured YIG microplatelet and a superconducting LC resonator, even at extremely low input powers (<= 10 fW).

**Method.** The team used Focused Ion Beam (FIB) techniques to create a YIG microplatelet from a single crystal and placed it on a constricted inductive line of an optimized superconducting lumped element LC resonator.

**Model / system.** The system consists of quantized magnon modes within a micron-scale YIG microplatelet coupled to photons in a superconducting lumped element LC resonator.

**Key observables.** Microwave transmission signal and anti-crossings in the transmission spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sub-10 micron YIG structures, device input powers <= 10 fW.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of small-scale magnonics, describes the FIB fabrication and resonator design, presents experimental evidence of anti-crossings, validates results with micromagnetic simulations and analytical calculations, and demonstrates low-power operation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strong-coupling experiments based on magnons enable the exploration into on-chip demonstrations involving numerous long-lived excitations. Yttrium iron garnet (YIG) has been considered for decades as a gold standard material for magnonics due to its low-loss magnonic properties. While YIG has successfully demonstrated strong-coupling in macroscopic device geometries, the strong coupling of magnons in truly sub-10 micron YIG structures to date has not yet been realized. This obstacle is due to the difficulty producing large enough effective magnonic mode volume necessary primarily due to thickness limitations of YIG deposition and device fabrication techniques. Here, we demonstrate the use of a microplatelet of YIG, manufactured from a single crystal of YIG via focused ion beam (FIB) techniques, placed on a constricted inductive line of an optimized superconducting lumped element LC resonator to achieve strong coupling between numerous magnon modes and the LC resonator photons. These experimental findings are qualitatively backed by micromagnetic simulations and quantitatively supported by analytical calculations to identify the magnon modes corresponding to the experimentally observed anti-crossings in the microwave transmission signal. Further, we show that these anti-crossings remain even at incredibly low device input powers ($\leq 10$ fW). The fabrication techniques and device geometry enable the deterministic use of numerous confined magnon modes in micron-scale YIG structures for various magnetic field strengths and orientations at substantially reduced device powers. The results here establish a foundational path forward to achieving efficient magnon-based strong-coupling experiments in micron-scale YIG magnetic elements for effective on-chip studies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27843"></a>
### [Spin-coherence characterization of boron vacancy defects in hexagonal boron nitride with broadband microwave pulses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27843v1)

**Authors:** Yuki Nakamura, Takuya Iwasaki, Shu Nakaharai, Shinichi Ogawa, Yukinori Morita, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Kento Sasaki, Kensuke Kobayashi  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27843v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5**

**Summary.** This study characterizes the spin coherence of boron vacancy defects in hBN using broadband microwave pulses. By measuring T2* and T2 times, the researchers established an effective method for evaluating spin defects in van der Waals thin films. These findings are crucial for developing hBN-based nanoscale quantum sensors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it provides experimental characterization of decoherence mechanisms and spin-coherence dynamics in a solid-state platform suitable for quantum sensing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Characterizing the spin coherence times (T2* and T2) of negatively charged boron vacancy (VB-) defects in hBN for use in nanoscale quantum sensing.

**Main result.** The study achieved sub-GHz Rabi oscillations and measured spin coherence times of T2* = 13.8 ns and T2 = 108.7 ns using broadband microwave pulses.

**Method.** The researchers used broadband microwave pulses on an isotopically enriched hBN thin film stamped onto a gold wire to perform Ramsey interference and Hahn echo measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of negatively charged boron vacancy (VB-) defects in an isotopically enriched hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) thin film integrated with a narrow gold wire.

**Key observables.** Rabi oscillations, Ramsey interference signal (Gaussian-like decay), Hahn echo signal, and the stretch factor (alpha).

**Important parameters / regimes.** T2* = 13.8 ns, T2 = 108.7 ns, stretch factor alpha = 1.25, and sub-GHz Rabi frequency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the potential of VB- in hBN for sensing, describes the experimental setup using gold wires and isotopically enriched films, presents the results of Rabi, Ramsey, and Hahn echo measurements, and concludes with the implications for evaluating spin defects in van der Waals materials.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Negatively charged boron vacancy (VB-) defects in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) are promising for nanoscale-proximity quantum sensing. To evaluate their performance, it is important to characterize the spin coherence times T2* and T2. In this study, we realized sub-GHz Rabi oscillations of VB- using an isotopically enriched hBN thin film directly stamped onto a narrow gold wire. Using these strong microwave pulses, we performed Ramsey interference and Hahn echo measurements. The Ramsey interference signal showed Gaussian-like decay, yielding T2* = 13.8 ns. The Hahn echo measurement gave T2 = 108.7 ns and a stretch factor of α= 1.25. These results experimentally clarify the spin coherence properties of VB- and provide an effective method for evaluating the coherence of spin defects in van der Waals thin films with broad resonance linewidths.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27417"></a>
### [Mobile Exceptional Points Generate Momentum-Space Switching Domains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27417v1)

**Authors:** Jung-Wan Ryu, Chang-Hwan Yi  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27417v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper shows that moving exceptional points under periodic modulation create distinct regions in momentum space that exhibit different band-switching behaviors. This mechanism allows for the engineering of non-Hermitian topology by controlling the trajectories of EPs. The findings are validated through both theoretical modeling and a photonic crystal implementation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates how controlled motion of non-Hermitian degeneracies can be used to engineer specific topological band structures and switching phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates what new band topology emerges when exceptional points (EPs) become mobile under cyclic modulation in non-Hermitian systems.

**Main result.** Mobile EPs generate momentum-space switching domains that partition the Brillouin zone into regions with distinct band-switching behavior, which can eventually lead to global band switching.

**Method.** The authors use a minimal two-band lattice model to introduce a band-permutation invariant and demonstrate the effect in a photonic crystal with lossy materials.

**Model / system.** A minimal two-band lattice model under cyclic modulation and a photonic crystal platform incorporating lossy materials.

**Key observables.** Band-switching behavior, band-permutation invariant, and EP trajectories in an extended parameter space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Crystal momentum, modulation parameter, and modulation strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of mobile EPs, presents a theoretical framework using a two-band lattice model and a band-permutation invariant, describes the formation of switching domains, and concludes with a demonstration in a photonic crystal system.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exceptional points (EPs), non-Hermitian degeneracies where both eigenvalues and eigenvectors coalesce, play a central role in the topology of non-Hermitian spectra. Recent advances have enabled the controlled creation and manipulation of EPs in a wide range of physical systems, raising the question of what new band topology emerges when EPs become mobile under cyclic modulation. Here we show that mobile EPs generate momentum-space switching domains that partition the Brillouin zone into regions with distinct band-switching behavior. Using a minimal two-band lattice model, we introduce a band-permutation invariant that determines whether eigenmodes exchange after one modulation cycle. The boundaries between switching regions arise from the projection of EP trajectories in an extended parameter space combining crystal momentum and the modulation parameter. As the modulation strength increases, the switching domains expand and eventually cover the entire Brillouin zone, resulting in global band switching. The predicted switching-domain structure is further demonstrated in a photonic crystal with lossy materials. These results open a new avenue within non-Hermitian topology by enabling the engineering of EP-driven phenomena through their controlled motion.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Directional Cluster Migration Driven by Escape-Rate Asymmetry in Multi-Compartment Granular Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27611v1)

**Authors:** Kai Kono, Hiroyuki Ebata, Shio Inagaki  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27611v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<img src="2604.27611_figures/2604.27611_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explains how clusters of different-sized particles move directionally through a multi-compartment system under vibration. By measuring how one particle species affects the escape rate of another, the authors created a predictive model that reproduces complex, stepwise migration patterns. This provides a framework for understanding collective transport in driven, out-of-equilibrium systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The asymmetric, population-dependent escape rates and the resulting directional transport are analogous to activating-inhibitory interactions in reaction-diffusion systems and non-equilibrium steady states in open systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the quantitative mechanism behind directional, stepwise cluster migration in multi-compartment, vertically vibrated granular systems.

**Main result.** The researchers identified that directional migration is driven by an asymmetric interaction where large particles enhance the escape rate of small particles, while small particles suppress the escape rate of large particles.

**Method.** The authors used high-speed video to directly measure particle escape rates as a function of population and developed a minimal flux model based on these experimental measurements.

**Model / system.** A vertically vibrated, bidisperse granular system (small silicon nitride and large zirconia particles) in an acrylic cell with 2 to 7 compartments and slits for particle exchange.

**Key observables.** Particle flux, particle populations (Ns, NL), and spatiotemporal migration patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of compartments (K), particle counts (Ns, NL), vibration acceleration (Gamma), and cell depth.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes particle exchange occurs only between adjacent compartments and that material properties are implicitly encoded in the measured escape rates.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup and snapshots of migration; Figure 2 presents spatiotemporal diagrams; Figure 3 shows different stationary states; Figure 4 and 12 show phase diagrams in the Ns-NL plane; Figure 7-10 show escape rate heat maps and fitting parameters; Figure 11 shows numerical simulations of cluster motion.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the phenomenon of cluster migration, describes the experimental setup and measurement of escape rates, presents the development of a minimal flux model using empirical fits, validates the model against experimental observations, and discusses the resulting phase diagrams and physical mechanisms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Granular materials are inherently out-of-equilibrium systems due to energy dissipation through inelastic collisions and friction. When driven by mechanical agitation such as vibration, they exhibit rich collective behaviors including segregation, clustering, and spontaneous oscillations. Here, we report directional stepwise migration of particle clusters from one compartment to the next in a vertically vibrated granular system composed of small and large particles. To clarify the underlying mechanism, we directly measured how the flux of both particle species depends on the instantaneous particle populations. The measurements reveal an asymmetric interaction between particle species: the flux of small particles is enhanced by the presence of large particles, whereas that of large particles is suppressed by small particles. A minimal flux model incorporating these measured fluxes reproduces the observed directional dynamics and provides an experimentally grounded framework for collective transport in vibrated granular systems.

</details>

### [Mapping the Phase Diagram of the Vicsek Model with Machine Learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28167v1)

**Authors:** Grace T. Bai, Brandon B. Le  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28167v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28167_figures/2604.28167_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. K-Means classification of Vicsek-model simulation data. (a) Clustering in the observable space spanned by the order parameter ⟨ϕ⟩, Binder cumulant G, and susceptibility χ. (b) The same cluster labels projected onto the parameter space (η, ρ, v0), showing the corresponding disorder, order, and coexistence regions.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.28167_figures/2604.28167_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Three-dimensional learned phase diagram of the Vic- sek model in the parameter space (η, ρ, v0). Each point rep- resents a classified parameter-grid point, with purple, orange, and blue denoting disorder, order, and coexistence, respec- tively. The classifier predicts a broad ordered region at low noise, a disordered region at higher noise, and a narrower co- existence region separating the two phases.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.28167_figures/2604.28167_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Two-dimensional slices of the learned Vicsek-model phase diagram in the (η, ρ) plane at fixed particle speeds v0 = 0.017, 0.043, and 0.109. The shaded regions show the neural-network classification on an evenly spaced parameter grid, while the overlaid points show the original labeled simulation data near each slice. Purple, orange, and blue denote disorder, order, and coexistence, respectively.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses machine learning to automate the discovery of phase transitions in the Vicsek model of active matter. By combining K-Means clustering with neural networks, the authors can efficiently map out complex phase boundaries and identify coexistence regions in a 3D parameter space.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Mapping and classifying the global phase structure of the Vicsek model across a three-dimensional parameter space to resolve phase boundaries and identify coexistence regions.

**Main result.** The study successfully used a machine learning pipeline to achieve 92% classification accuracy, resolving a narrow coexistence phase and extending phase boundaries beyond the original simulation points.

**Method.** An unsupervised K-Means clustering approach was used to label phases based on dynamical observables, followed by training a supervised neural network to interpolate the phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The Vicsek model, a minimalist agent-based model for active matter consisting of N self-propelled particles in 2D that update orientation based on neighbors subject to additive noise.

**Key observables.** Order parameter (phi), Binder cumulant (G), and susceptibility (chi).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise (eta), density (rho), and self-propulsion velocity (v0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The phase map is considered 'coarse' and focuses on steady-state dynamics by discarding the initial 20% of simulation time.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows K-Means clustering in observable space and the resulting phase labels in parameter space; Figure 2 presents a 3D learned phase diagram; Figure 3 shows 2D slices of the phase diagram in the (eta, rho) plane.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Vicsek model and the problem of phase mapping, describes the simulation and ML pipeline (clustering then neural network), presents the resulting 3D and 2D phase diagrams, and concludes with validation and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this study, we use machine learning to classify and interpolate the phase structure of the Vicsek flocking model across the three-dimensional parameter space $(η,ρ,v_0)$. We construct a dataset of simulated parameter points and characterize each point using long-time dynamical observables. These observables are then used as inputs to a K-Means clustering procedure, which assigns each point to a disorder, order, or coexistence phase. Using these clustered labels, we train a neural-network classifier to learn the mapping from model parameters to phase behavior, achieving a classification accuracy of 0.92. The resulting phase map resolves a narrow coexistence region separating the ordered and disordered phases and extends the inferred phase boundaries beyond the originally sampled simulation points. More broadly, this approach provides a systematic way to convert sparse simulation data into a global phase diagram for collective-motion models.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27946"></a>
### [Theory of quantum decoherence in macroscopic topological insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27946v1)

**Authors:** Xian-Peng Zhang, Yan-Qing Feng, Wanxiang Feng, Yugui Yao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27946v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a theory to explain how quantum decoherence affects the spin Hall effect in large-scale topological insulators. It discovers a new, stronger type of spin Hall effect caused by decoherence and provides a specific scaling law that can be tested experimentally. This work highlights the importance of decoherence in the development of future spintronic technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep look into how environmental coupling (decoherence) fundamentally alters transport properties and introduces new scattering mechanisms in topological matter.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the microscopic mechanisms of quantum decoherence and its impact on the quantum spin Hall effect in macroscopic, infinite-size topological insulators.

**Main result.** The authors identify a new second-order skew-scattering mechanism for the extrinsic spin Hall effect tied to decoherence and predict a new scaling law where spin Hall conductivity scales quadratically with longitudinal conductivity.

**Method.** Development of a comprehensive theoretical framework to quantitatively investigate decoherence-induced corrections in topological transport.

**Model / system.** Macroscopic topological insulators subject to interactions with an environment and impurity-induced scattering.

**Key observables.** Quantum spin Hall conductivity, longitudinal conductivity, and impurity density scaling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Impurity density and the relationship between spin Hall and longitudinal conductivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory focuses on the regime of macroscopic, infinite-size topological insulators.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of decoherence in topological insulators, develops a theory for decoherence-induced corrections, uncovers a new skew-scattering mechanism, and concludes with a predicted experimental scaling law.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum decoherence-the loss of quantum coherence due to interactions with an environment-plays a central role in quantum transport, and controlling this ubiquitous yet inevitable phenomenon is essential for practical quantum technologies. Despite its importance, the microscopic mechanisms of decoherence in infinite-size topological insulators remain poorly understood. Here, we develop a comprehensive theory that quantitatively investigates how quantum decoherence shapes the quantum spin Hall effect in macroscopic topological insulators, and reveal that decoherence-induced corrections scale quadratically with impurity density. Besides, we uncover a previously unidentified mechanism of the extrinsic spin Hall effect: a second-order skew-scattering process intrinsically tied to quantum decoherence-fundamentally distinct from, yet substantially stronger than, the conventional third-order skew-scattering mechanism. Furthermore, we predict a new scaling law in which the decoherence-induced spin Hall conductivity scales quadratically with the longitudinal conductivity, providing a clear experimental signature of decoherence effects. Our results establish the essential role of decoherence in quantum transport of topological insulators and reveal that macroscopic topological insulators offer a promising platform for next-generation spintronic applications.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27076"></a>
### [Superconductivity-Enabled Conversion of Ferromagnetic Resonance into Standing Spin Waves](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27076v1)

**Authors:** Ya. V. Turkin, N. G. Pugach, F. M. Maksimov, A. S. Pakhomov, A. I. Chernov, V. I. Belotelov, S. N. Polulyakh, V. S. Stolyarov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27076v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27076_figures/2604.27076_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Sample and measurement geometry. A Bi- substituted iron-garnet film is capped by a Nb layer and placed on a coplanar waveguide. The microwave magnetic field hAC drives magnetization precession about the out-of- plane static field Hext.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27076_figures/2604.27076_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Normalized microwave transmission |S21| versus out-of-plane field at f = 4 GHz for representative temperatures T = 11 K (normal state), T = 8 K (near Tc), and T = 4 K (deep in the superconducting state). Left column: uncapped Bi-GdIG film (experiment). Right column: Bi-GdIG/Nb bilayer (experiment, blue) together with the calculated lineshape (theory, orange). Below Tc, the bilayer develops an additional resonance feature (the “right” peak) near the uniform FMR position, while the uncapped film shows only the conventional response.For more details see Supplemental Material [30]</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27076_figures/2604.27076_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Calculated transverse susceptibility χxy of the Bi-GdIG/Nb structure versus frequency and field. The dashed line indicates the uniform-mode (Kittel) dispersion. Proximity-enabled hybridization with the lowest PSSW pro- duces two nearby resonant branches, consistent with the two- peak structure observed in Fig. 2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that a superconductor can act as a control knob to excite high-frequency spin waves in an adjacent insulator. By utilizing both triplet-mediated spin torque and vortex-induced field gradients, the researchers show how to convert uniform magnetic resonance into complex standing wave modes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study explores how a conventional superconductor can be used to actively control and convert uniform ferromagnetic resonance (FMR) into short-wavelength perpendicular standing spin waves (PSSWs) in an adjacent magnetic insulator.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that superconductivity enables mode conversion through a dual mechanism: interfacial spin-transfer torque from triplet Cooper pairs and symmetry breaking via vortex-induced electromagnetic proximity.

**Method.** The work combines broadband microwave spectroscopy on Bi-GdIG/Nb bilayers with a microscopic theory coupling the Keldysh-Usadel formalism for the superconductor to the Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert dynamics for the magnet.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a bilayer of Bi-substituted iron-garnet (Bi-GdIG) and Niobium (Nb) in the Abrikosov vortex state. The theoretical model uses a linearized 1D LLG equation with a Robin-type boundary condition at the interface and a depth-dependent effective field from vortices.

**Key observables.** Complex microwave transmission (S21 amplitude) and transverse magnetic susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (4K to 11K), external magnetic field, frequency (4 GHz), exchange stiffness, and spin-mixing conductance.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a diffusive Nb film, a linearized response regime, and attributes peak asymmetry to an unmodeled magnetostatic field from the Gadolinium substrate.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the sample geometry and setup; Figure 2 compares microwave transmission in uncapped vs. bilayer films, highlighting the new resonance peak below Tc; Figure 3 shows the calculated susceptibility and mode hybridization; Supplementary figures show temperature-dependent transmission and FMR peak shifts.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of magnon control, describes the experimental bilayer setup, presents the observed resonance emergence below Tc, develops a microscopic theory (Keldysh-Usadel + LLG) to explain the dual mechanism of torque and vortex-induced asymmetry, and concludes with a discussion on tunability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductors can transport spin without Joule dissipation, yet their coherent coupling to short-wavelength magnons in insulating magnets remains largely unexplored. Here we demonstrate experimentally and theoretically that a conventional diffusive superconductor can enable the conversion of the uniform ferromagnetic-resonance (FMR) mode into perpendicular standing spin waves (PSSWs) in an adjacent ferrimagnetic insulator. In Bi-substituted iron-garnet/Nb bilayers, the microwave transmission develops an additional resonance feature that appears only below the Nb transition temperature and lies close to the uniform FMR peak. A microscopic theory that self-consistently couples the quasiclassical Keldysh--Usadel description of the superconducting condensate to the Landau--Lifshitz--Gilbert dynamics shows that the conversion requires two ingredients: (i) an interfacial spin-transfer torque mediated by spin-polarized triplet Cooper pairs and (ii) a depth-dependent effective field produced by Abrikosov vortices (electromagnetic proximity). The resulting susceptibility reproduces the measured lineshapes and establishes superconductivity as an active control knob for exchange standing-wave modes in magnetic insulators.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27719"></a>
### [Emergent electric fields driven by phonon-coupled skyrmion resonances](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27719v1)

**Authors:** Seno Aji  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27719v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides a unified theoretical framework for understanding how phonon-driven deformations in a skyrmion lattice generate macroscopic electric fields. It shows that breathing and rotational dynamics can produce measurable ac and dc electric signals even without moving the lattice. This is significant for developing new ways to control and detect topological magnetic textures via spin-charge-lattice coupling.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to describe the generation of macroscopic emergent electric fields resulting from the coupling between phonons and the breathing/rotational dynamics of a skyrmion lattice under microwave excitation.

**Main result.** The study identifies the specific symmetry and dynamical conditions required to produce both rectified (dc) and oscillating (ac) electric fields, even without net lattice translation.

**Method.** The authors develop a coarse-grained theoretical framework to analyze the electrodynamic response in the long-wavelength phonon limit.

**Model / system.** A skyrmion lattice subject to phonon-coupled lattice deformations and microwave excitation, specifically focusing on breathing and rotational modes.

**Key observables.** Macroscopic emergent electric fields (dc and ac components), skyrmion radius, domain-wall width, and dynamical resonance frequency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Long-wavelength phonon limit (q -> 0), finite phonon frequency, and the dilute-lattice limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes a coarse-grained description and focuses on the long-wavelength limit of phonons.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a theoretical development of a coarse-grained model, followed by an analysis of symmetry and dynamical conditions for electric field generation, and concludes with an identification of harmonic components in the electrodynamic response.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a coarse-grained theoretical description of the macroscopic emergent electric field generated by phonon-coupled lattice deformations in the breathing and rotational dynamics of a skyrmion lattice under microwave excitation. The analysis identifies the symmetry and dynamical conditions that yield rectified (dc) and oscillating (ac) electric fields, even in the absence of net translational motion of the skyrmion lattice, particularly in the dilute-lattice limit. Using experimentally measurable skyrmion profile parameters such as the equilibrium radius, domain-wall width, and dynamical resonance frequency of skyrmion lattice, the model further enables identification of harmonic components contributing to the observed macroscopic electrodynamic response in the long-wavelength phonon limit ($q \to 0$) and at finite phonon frequency, providing a unified framework for phonon-driven spin-charge-lattice coupling in topological magnets.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27331"></a>
### [Low-Energy Purification of Crystal Defects by Rydberg Excitons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27331v1)

**Authors:** Shiva Kant Tiwari, Tijs Karman, Valentin Walther  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27331v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper presents a multichannel theory to explain how Rydberg excitons can be used to purify crystals by neutralizing charged impurities. It demonstrates that while high-energy collisions can hinder purification through inelastic scattering, an ultralow-energy quantum regime exists that maximizes purification efficiency. This regime can be experimentally accessed using tunable two-photon excitation in cuprous oxide.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a theoretical framework for controlling impurity-driven decoherence and purification in solid-state systems using Rydberg-like physics, bridging the gap between atomic physics techniques and condensed matter applications.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the competing mechanisms of capture, elastic scattering, and inelastic transitions during Rydberg exciton-impurity collisions and how they affect crystal purification efficiency.

**Main result.** The authors identify a quantum regime at ultralow collision energies where s-wave scattering enhances capture while suppressing loss channels, making purification more efficient than at high collision energies.

**Method.** Development of a multichannel theory of Rydberg exciton-impurity scattering.

**Model / system.** Rydberg excitons in cuprous oxide (Cu2O) interacting with charged impurities.

**Key observables.** Purification efficiency, capture rate, elastic scattering cross-section, and inelastic transition rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Collision energy, s-wave scattering regime, and even-parity Rydberg exciton states via two-photon excitation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of impurity neutralization, presents a multichannel scattering theory, compares high-energy vs. low-energy collision regimes, and proposes a method for accessing the optimal purification regime via two-photon excitation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent experiments show that optically generated Rydberg excitons in cuprous oxide can neutralize charged impurities, strongly reducing stray electric fields and effectively purifying the crystal. Here, we develop a multichannel theory of Rydberg exciton-impurity scattering that resolves the competing roles of capture, elastic scattering, and inelastic transitions between excitonic states. We find that at high collision energies, as effective under conventional single-photon excitation, purification is reduced relative to Langevin capture. These collisions are accompanied by inelastic redistribution and dominant elastic scattering, including pronounced glory scattering, which suppress purification efficiency. We identify a quantum regime at ultralow collision energies favorable for purification, where only the s-wave contributes: capture is enhanced while elastic and inelastic channels are strongly suppressed. This regime can be accessed via degenerate two-photon excitation of even-parity Rydberg excitons with tunable recoil, additionally enabling the systematic exploration of exciton-impurity scattering over a wide range of collision energies beyond what is readily achievable in atomic counterparts in atomic gas experiments.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper uses real-time TDDFT to show that core electrons in beryllium both enhance and suppress electronic stopping power. It identifies an electron capture mechanism as the cause for valence excitation suppression, providing a new perspective on energy dissipation in ion-matter interactions that standard semi-empirical models fail to capture.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the complex, multi-stage Bragg peak structure in electronic stopping power and the dual role of core electrons in energy dissipation.

**Main result.** Core electrons play a dual role by providing an additional dissipation channel through excitation while simultaneously suppressing valence electron excitations via an electron capture process.

**Method.** Real-time time-dependent density functional theory (rt-TDDFT) using the INQ/QB@LL program with explicit treatment of all electrons.

**Model / system.** A beryllium (Be) projectile moving through a bulk beryllium (Be) host, modeled using a 5 x 6 x 3 hexagonal close-packed (hcp) supercell.

**Key observables.** Electronic stopping power (Se), friction coefficient, Bragg peak structure, and excitation numbers for 1s and 2s electrons.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Velocity regimes (low-velocity, Bragg peak, and high-velocity), atomic number (Z1), and trajectory types (hyper-channeling vs. off-channeling).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Host atoms are kept frozen to isolate the pure electronic stopping process; use of the PBE exchange-correlation functional; adiabatic approximation in TDDFT.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares stopping power and Bragg peak structure across different trajectories and models; Figure 2 shows the decomposition of stopping power into 1s and 2s components; Figure 3 illustrates excitation distributions and time evolution of excitation events; Supplemental figures demonstrate numerical robustness and energy expectation values.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of energy dissipation, describes the rt-TDDFT methodology and Be system, presents results comparing trajectories and models, analyzes the dual role of core electrons through energy decomposition, and concludes with a discussion on model limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-equilibrium energy dissipation in multi-shell swift-ion/matter systems remains a fundamental yet incompletely understood problem, with electronic stopping power $\mathcal{S}_\text{e}$ as a relevant observable for electronic friction. Using real-time time-dependent density functional theory, we perform first-principles calculations of $\mathcal{S}_\text{e}$ for beryllium self-irradiation with explicit treatment of all electrons. Our results reveal a Bragg peak exhibiting a distinct structure which lies beyond the reach of standard semi-empirical models. We attribute its appearance to a dual effect of the presence of core electrons, by which their excitation provides an additional dissipation channel while simultaneously suppressing valence electron excitations. An electron capture process by the projectile's core from the host cores is behind such suppression, rather than Pauli blocking. This dual mechanism contrasts with the shake-up effect reported for water, whereby the inclusion of core electrons enhances valence excitation. Our work provides a new perspective on the effect and importance of core electrons in projectile energy dissipation in matter.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28008"></a>
### [Compressibility of micromagnetic solutions in tensor train format](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28008v1)

**Authors:** Thierry Valet, Nicolas Vukadinovic  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28008v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper shows that using Tensor Train formats can drastically reduce the computational cost of 3D micromagnetic simulations by exploiting the sparsity of magnetic structures. By reducing the scaling of parameters relative to system size and grid refinement, this method paves the way for much larger and more detailed magnetic simulations than currently possible.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard micromagnetic simulations suffer from poor computational scaling (~L^3 and (1/a)^3) because they use uniform 3D grids that fail to exploit the inherent spatial sparsity of magnetic structures.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that Tensor Train (TT) representations can exploit spatial sparsity to reduce parameter scaling to approximately L^1.8 and (1/a)^1.2, providing a significant advantage for large-scale simulations.

**Method.** The study utilizes Tensor Train (TT) compression techniques to represent micromagnetic magnetization states, specifically analyzing the compressibility of flux-closure configurations.

**Model / system.** The system consists of 3D soft-magnetic rectangular prisms in the near-micrometer regime, focusing on configurations containing both high-gradient regions (domain walls) and uniform volumetric domains.

**Key observables.** Parameter count scaling (compression ratio) and accuracy preservation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Linear size L, cell size a, and exchange length.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on representative flux-closure configurations in soft-magnetic materials.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of scaling in micromagnetics, proposes the Tensor Train format as a solution, demonstrates the scaling advantages through numerical analysis of magnetic prisms, and discusses future implications for solvers.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For three-dimensional (3D) magnetic objects with linear size $L$ exceeding a few exchange lengths, the micromagnetic state exhibits pronounced informational sparsity: low-dimensional, high-gradient regions (e.g., domain walls) coexist with near-uniformly magnetized volumetric domains. Because standard micromagnetic simulation methods discretize the magnetization on near-uniform 3D grids with linear cell size $a$, they cannot take advantage of this sparsity. The computational problem scales as $\sim L^3$ and $\sim (1/a)^3$. In this Letter, we establish that direct tensor-train (TT) representations overcome these poor scalings by exploiting the spatial sparsity optimally, while preserving accuracy in a controlled way. Focusing on representative flux-closure configurations in soft-magnetic rectangular prisms, in the near-micrometer regime, we demonstrate that the parameter count of TT-compressed micromagnetic data scales approximately as $L^{1.8}$ and $(1/a)^{1.2}$. Hence the relative advantage over dense discretizations rapidly grows with the problem size and refinement level. These first results provide a strong motivation for future developments of micromagnetic solvers in TT format which could transcend the limitations of traditional simulators, with far reaching potential impacts on fundamental research and technology development.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how to electrically tune the polarization (chirality) of terahertz light by using a gate voltage to change the size of Fermi pockets in a Dirac semimetal. This process modulates the Berry curvature-driven component of the THz emission, allowing for programmable control of light's polarization state on the Poincaré sphere.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving all-electrical, deterministic control over the chirality (polarization state) of Terahertz (THz) emission by manipulating the quantum geometry (Berry curvature) of electronic wavefunctions.

**Main result.** By using electrostatic gating to reshape Fermi pockets in a Cd3As2 thin film, the researchers achieved near-circular THz polarization and demonstrated that the Berry curvature integral can be tuned electrically.

**Method.** The researchers used electrostatic gating of a 3D Dirac semimetal thin film under oblique incidence of circularly polarized femtosecond light, employing THz emission spectroscopy and Stokes parameter analysis to track polarization evolution.

**Model / system.** A 40 nm thin film of the 3D Dirac semimetal Cd3As2 grown on sapphire, subject to Floquet engineering via circularly polarized 800 nm laser pulses to create Floquet Weyl nodes.

**Key observables.** THz electric field components (Ey and EXZ), Stokes parameters (S1, S2, S3), ellipticity angle (chi), and Berry curvature integral.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate voltage (ranging from -90 V to +90 V), pump wavelength (800 nm), pump fluence (1.02 mJ/cm2), and Fermi energy (E_F approx 210 meV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a linear isotropic dispersion for the Dirac semimetal and uses an asymmetric screening model to relate gate voltage to Fermi level shifts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Floquet band engineering and device architecture; Figure 3 illustrates the evolution of polarization ellipses from vertical to circular to horizontal; Figure 4 provides quantitative analysis of the gate-dependent Berry curvature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of controlling optical observables via quantum geometry, describes the Floquet-engineered Cd3As2 device, details the experimental THz spectroscopy and gating methods, presents the results of polarization tuning and Berry curvature quantification, and concludes with the implications for programmable quantum geometric control.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum geometry encoded in the momentum space structure of electronic wavefunctions, governs charge dynamics through Berry curvature, enabling unconventional transport and optical responses. In topological semimetals, this geometry is sampled over Fermi pockets, suggesting electrical control by Fermi surface tuning, yet such control has remained largely limited to DC transport. Here we show that electrostatic gating of the 3D Dirac semimetal Cd3As2 reshapes Fermi pockets surrounding photoinduced Floquet Weyl nodes, enabling electrical control of terahertz (THz) emission chirality. Gate tuning selectively modulates the Berry curvature driven linearly polarized THz component by up to 60% and 49% at positive and negative bias, respectively, while the orthogonal linearly polarized photon-drag component remains unchanged. With the two orthogonal fields intrinsically phase-locked at \sfracπ{2} by the excitation geometry, the selective gate-tuned amplitude control enables the polarization tuning across the Poincaré sphere, achieving near-circular polarization (χ\approx-42°) at +10 V. These results establish Fermi surface tuning as a general route to programmable quantum geometric control of chiral terahertz emission.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27729"></a>
### [On the proposed concept of mechanical phasons in Ni-Mn-Ga modulated martensite](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27729v1)

**Authors:** Petr Sedlák, Tomáš Grabec, Hanuš Seiner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27729v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that 'mechanical phasons' in Ni-Mn-Ga alloys act as a mechanism to relax macroscopic shear strains, explaining why the material appears much softer to ultrasonic probes than to neutron scattering. The model reconciles the observed extreme elastic compliance with the known stiff lattice structure by focusing on the collective dynamics of the modulation phase.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the discrepancy between experimental observations of extreme elastic softening and theoretical/neutron scattering predictions of a stiff lattice in 10M modulated Ni-Mn-Ga martensite.

**Main result.** The authors propose that 'mechanical phasons' (phase shifts in the modulation wave) can relax external shear loads in commensurate and weakly incommensurate phases, explaining the macroscopic softness without requiring intrinsic lattice-scale softness.

**Method.** A mechanical model that merges adaptive martensite theory with electronic-structure considerations, using energy minimization of a modulated lattice.

**Model / system.** The system is the 10M modulated martensite in Ni-Mn-Ga ferromagnetic shape memory alloys, modeled as a periodic modulation wave interacting with a Landau-type energy landscape of non-modulated martensite variants.

**Key observables.** Shear modulus (c55), monoclinic distortion angle (gamma), modulation wave vector (q), and twinning stress.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weighting factor (epsilon) controlling the transition from sine-wave to zig-zag modulation, modulation vector (q), and phason amplitude (delta phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The lattice consists of rigid planes that can shift along the modulation amplitude; the modulation is treated as a perturbation of a sine wave.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows INS phonon branches and the notation for the modulation function; Figure 2 illustrates the evolution of the energy-minimizing lattice and energy landscape for different epsilon values; Figure 3 shows the cumulative sum of monoclinic distortion and the effect of phase shifts.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental discrepancy, presents a mechanical model of phason-driven relaxation, explores the transition from commensurate to incommensurate regimes, and concludes by explaining the origin of monoclinic distortion and twinning mobility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We discuss modulation phasons as a possible source of unusual elastic behavior of five-layer modulated (10\,M) martensite of the Ni-Mn-Ga shape memory alloy. This material exhibits anomalous macroscopic shear compliance along specific planes perpendicular to the modulation vector, and this compliance disappears when the modulations become incommensurate. Using a simple mechanical model, we show that modulation phasons in Ni-Mn-Ga can have macroscopic mechanical manifestations, and that the resulting 'mechanical phasons' can relax external shear loadings for commensurate and weakly incommensurate modulations, but not for strongly incommensurate modulations. The model merges ideas from the adaptive martensite theory and electronic-structure considerations, and enables straightforward explanations of several properties of the 10\,M lattice, such as spontaneous monoclinic distortion or easy formation and propagation of $a/b$ twins.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27751"></a>
### [Quantum Metric and Nonlinear Hall Effect of Photons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27751v1)

**Authors:** Keidai Akiba, Naoki Yamamoto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27751v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

**Summary.** The paper shows that the quantum metric of photons induces a nonlinear Hall effect in both varying refractive index media and curved spacetime. This effect represents a higher-order correction to light trajectories, such as gravitational lensing, that does not depend on photon helicity. It highlights a deep interplay between the geometry of position space and momentum space.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is interesting for quantum optics researchers as it connects the fundamental quantum geometry of the photon (quantum metric) to observable nonlinear transport phenomena and higher-order corrections to light propagation in complex environments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether photons possess a nontrivial quantum metric in momentum space and whether this geometry induces nonlinear transport phenomena, such as a nonlinear Hall effect, in inhomogeneous media or curved spacetime.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the quantum metric of photons leads to a nonlinear Hall effect of light in media with varying refractive indices and a nonlinear gravitational Hall effect in curved spacetime, providing $O(\hbar^2)$ corrections to gravitational lensing.

**Method.** The study employs the path-integral formalism to derive a semiclassical action and equations of motion, utilizing perturbative diagonalization methods (Luttinger-Kohn and Schrieffer-Wolff) to account for the quantum metric.

**Model / system.** The system consists of photons described by a spin-1 Hamiltonian $H = \pm c \mathbf{S} \cdot \mathbf{p}$ traveling through media with a spatially varying refractive index $n(\mathbf{x})$ or through a static, weak background gravitational field.

**Key observables.** Quantum metric ($G_{ij}$), Berry curvature ($\Omega$), and trajectory shifts in light propagation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Geometric-optics limit (wavelength much smaller than curvature radius), weak gravitational potential ($|\phi|/c^2 \ll 1$), and the scaling of corrections ($O(\hbar)$ spin Hall effect vs. $O(\hbar^2)$ nonlinear Hall effect).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a static and weak gravitational field, a medium with unit permeability, the geometric-optics approximation, and the regime where the momentum derivative is small ($|\dot{p}| \ll |p|^2$).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the path-integral formalism for photons, derives the effective action and equations of motion including the quantum metric, applies these results to inhomogeneous refractive media to identify the nonlinear Hall effect, and finally extends the analysis to curved spacetime to describe corrections to gravitational lensing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using the path-integral formalism, we show that photons possess a nontrivial quantum metric in momentum space. We derive the semiclassical action and equations of motion by taking into account the quantum metric. In media with a spatially varying refractive index $n(\mathbf{x})$, the quantum metric induces a shift in the trajectory of light at second order in derivatives of $n$, which may be regarded as a nonlinear Hall effect of light. The quantum metric also gives rise to corrections to gravitational lensing in curved spacetime at the nonlinear order in wavelength. This gravitational nonlinear Hall effect results from the interplay between the geometry of position space and that of momentum space.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27554"></a>
### [Topological antiqued mechanical toy](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27554v1)

**Authors:** Hirofumi Wada, Hayato Mizobata, Shuto Ueno, Taiju Yoneda  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27554v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper explores the physics of the 'Jacob's ladder' toy, identifying its flipping waves as a class of topological solitons. It demonstrates that while the waves resemble those in topological chains, the system's unique 'floppiness' allows for forbidden modes like kink-antikink coexistence. The study uses a combination of experiments and theory to show how topological singularities drive the toy's motion.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The physical origin and design principles behind the unidirectional flipping waves in the 'Jacob's ladder' mechanical toy are investigated.

**Main result.** The study reveals that the toy's waves are topological solitons arising from bistability under gravity, and that its symmetric connection creates a topologically singular state allowing for kink-antikink coexistence.

**Method.** The researchers combine water-tank experiments, numerical simulations, and analytical theory including the use of the index theorem.

**Model / system.** A mechanical frame known as 'Jacob's ladder' consisting of rigid blocks connected by strings, analyzed under the influence of gravity and pretension.

**Key observables.** Unidirectional flipping waves, kink and antikink waves, and pair annihilation of waves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gravity, pretension, and the symmetry/asymmetry of the connections.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the toy can be modeled as a bistable system where gravity provides the necessary pretension to stiffen zero modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with an experimental exclusion of the domino mechanism, followed by an analytical demonstration of bistability and topological soliton behavior, a comparison to the Kane-Lubensky chain, an application of the index theorem to explain kink-antikink coexistence, and finally an analysis of asymmetric toy configurations and wave annihilation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

{\it Jacob's ladder} -- a classic children's toy -- is a simple mechanical frame comprising rigid blocks connected by strings that shows curious unidirectional flipping waves. Nonetheless, its physical origin remains elusive. By combining experiment, numeral simulation, and theory, we show that understanding the underlying design principle of this toy requires diverse physical ideas. First, we conduct a water-tank experiment that excludes the domino-like mechanism, thus defying widespread expectations. Subsequently, we analytically demonstrate that the toy is bistable under gravity, thus implying its kink wave as a class of topological solitons. The waves are surprisingly reminiscent -- both experimentally and theoretically -- to those in the Kane--Lubensky topological chain, owing to the stiffening of zero modes by the pretension under gravity. However, a close examination based on the index theorem reveals that the similarity remains superficial and that the floppiness of the toy underlies the kink and antikink coexistence -- a forbidden mode in the topological chain. By analyzing a generalized asymmetric toy, we reveal that its symmetric connection renders it topologically singular, thus resulting in amusing motions. We demonstrate these ideas by experimentally observing a dramatic pair annihilation of kink and antikink waves.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Ultrafast Sliding Ferroelectric Switching in Bilayer Hexagonal Boron Nitride Revealed by Deep Learning Molecular Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27342v1)

**Authors:** Yinan Wang, Poyen Chen, Teruyasu Mizoguchi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27342v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.27342_figures/2604.27342_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig.2 Performance of the EGCNN Born effective charge model and validation of the path-integral</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2604.27342_figures/2604.27342_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig.3 Electric-field-driven structural switching dynamics, atomic trajectory snapshots, and root-</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2604.27342_figures/2604.27342_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig.4 Physical origin of the dielectric background and extraction of the intrinsic ferroelectric</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a deep learning-based molecular dynamics approach to simulate the ultrafast switching of ferroelectricity in bilayer h-BN. It successfully isolates intrinsic polarization from field-induced dielectric noise, revealing a picosecond-scale sliding mechanism. This work provides a powerful tool for studying the non-equilibrium dynamics of 2D sliding ferroelectrics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the atomistic, real-time dynamics of electric-field-driven polarization switching in sliding ferroelectrics and decoupling intrinsic polarization from dielectric background.

**Main result.** The study reveals an ultrafast (5 ps) coherent single-domain rigid sliding mechanism in bilayer h-BN and develops a method to extract clean ferroelectric hysteresis loops from massive dielectric backgrounds.

**Method.** A coupled deep learning molecular dynamics framework integrating a fine-tuned MACE machine learning potential with an Equivariant Graph Convolutional Neural Network (EGCNN) for real-time Born effective charge prediction.

**Model / system.** Bilayer hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN) in AB and BA stacking configurations, modeled using large-scale non-equilibrium molecular dynamics under applied out-of-plane electric fields.

**Key observables.** Out-of-plane polarization (Pz), Born effective charge (BEC) tensors, potential energy surfaces, and ferroelectric hysteresis loops.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electric field strength (up to +/- 2.0 V/A), temperature (50 K to 300 K), and switching timescale (~5 ps).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The bilayer is treated as a free-standing system (no substrate interference) and the lattice is assumed to be defect-free.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows phonon dispersions, potential energy surfaces, and the minimum energy pathway; Figure 3 illustrates atomic trajectories and RMSD during switching; Figure 4 demonstrates the extraction of intrinsic polarization from the dielectric background.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of switching dynamics, presents the MACE-EGCNN machine learning framework, validates the model accuracy against DFT, details the polarization extraction protocol, describes the observed switching mechanism and its electromechanical nature, and concludes with stability analysis of the AB state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Sliding ferroelectricity in bilayer hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN) offers compelling prospects for next-generation non-volatile memory, yet the atomistic dynamics of electric-field-driven polarization switching remain poorly understood. Here, we present a fully data-driven, coupled atomistic framework that integrates a fine-tuned MACE machine learning potential (MLP) with an equivariant graph convolutional neural network (EGCNN) for real-time Born effective charge (BEC) prediction, enabling large-scale non-equilibrium molecular dynamics simulations of AB-stacked bilayer h-BN under applied electric fields. By implementing a rigorous real-space path-integral polarization formalism combined with a state-constrained Gaussian convolution background extraction procedure, we successfully isolate the intrinsic spontaneous polarization from the dominant dielectric background. Our simulations reveal that coherent single-domain rigid sliding, completing within 5 ps, constitutes a physically viable ultrafast switching mechanism, and reproduces clean ferroelectric hysteresis loops whose shape is qualitatively consistent with experimental observations.

</details>
