<a id="paper-2604.28029"></a>
### [A nanoionic diode: Equilibrium rectifying junction enabling large and stable resistance variations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28029v1)

**Authors:** Chuanlian Xiao, Joachim Maier  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28029v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The paper presents a new type of nanoionic diode that operates in full contact equilibrium, preventing the drift common in traditional diodes. By using Li-ions in TiO2 films, the device achieves an extremely high current on-off ratio of over 6 orders of magnitude. This approach offers a stable and easily tunable alternative for nanoscale electronic components.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a nanoscale rectifier that maintains stability and avoids drift under high temperatures or long waiting times, unlike traditional non-equilibrium diodes.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a nanoionic diode based on TiO2/Ru that achieves a current on-off ratio exceeding 6-7 orders of magnitude through an equilibrium rectifying junction.

**Method.** The researchers used nanosized TiO2 films on a Ru substrate to create a mixed conductor system where Li-ions and electrons are partitioned at the interface.

**Model / system.** A nanoionic device consisting of nanosized TiO2 films on a Ruthenium (Ru) substrate, utilizing a job-sharing mechanism for Li-ions and electrons.

**Key observables.** Current on-off ratio, resistance variations, and ionic/electronic mobility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Li-ion mobility, electron conductivity, and temperature/waiting time stability.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The device operates via a 'job-sharing' mechanism where dopant mobility is high enough to respond to electric fields but low enough to not interfere with electronic conductivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of an equilibrium nanoionic rectifier, describes the experimental setup using TiO2 and Ru, demonstrates the high on-off ratio, and discusses the advantages of electrochemical tuning.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report on a new type of rectifier which is in full contact equilibrium and thus, if down-sized to the nanoscale, shows no drift even if exposed to elevated temperatures and/or extreme waiting times. This is in contrast to existing diodes which rely on frozen doping profiles and are hence non-equilibrium devices. Our rectifiers are related to Schottky diodes but employ "dopants" whose mobilities are high enough to follow the electrical field quickly but low enough to not compete with the electrons in terms of conductivities. In order to realize such a device based on mixed conductors, we use nanosized TiO2 films on Ru as a substrate which can store Li at the interface according to a job-sharing mechanism (Li-ions on the TiO2 side, electrons on the Ru side). The excellent functionality of this nanoionic device is demonstrated (e.g., current on-off ratio can exceed 6-7 orders of magnitude) and the additional advantages stressed (such as ease of preparation and tuning the characteristics electrochemically).

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27876"></a>
### [Acoustic modulation of shear thickening transition in dense adhesive suspensions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27876v1)

**Authors:** Aoxuan Wang, Fabrice Toussaint, Thomas Gibaud  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27876v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This study demonstrates that ultrasound can control the flow of dense suspensions by shifting the onset of shear thickening to higher shear rates. It reveals that acoustic energy destabilizes the internal force networks through hydrodynamic fluctuations rather than simply lowering viscosity. This provides a mechanism for using sound to prevent flow instabilities in industrial processing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the physical origin of how high-power ultrasound reduces the apparent viscosity and modifies the discontinuous shear thickening (DST) transition in dense adhesive suspensions.

**Main result.** Ultrasound does not directly reduce viscosity but shifts the DST transition toward higher shear rates by destabilizing load-bearing force networks through fluctuating hydrodynamic forces.

**Method.** The researchers used a rheo-ultrasound setup to study dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions under acoustic modulation and analyzed stress probability distributions.

**Model / system.** Dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions characterized by fragile, load-bearing force networks embedded in heterogeneous density-wave structures.

**Key observables.** Apparent viscosity, shear rate, stress probability distributions, and the shear-thickening transition threshold.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shear rate, ultrasound power/frequency, and the separation of time scales between acoustic frequency and suspension dynamics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The suspension can be modeled as an effectively immobile porous medium subjected to high-frequency interstitial flows during the acoustic period.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of DST-induced instabilities, describes the experimental rheo-ultrasound setup, presents the observation of the shifted transition via stress distribution collapse, proposes a mechanism involving pore-scale hydrodynamic fluctuations, and concludes with the implications for controlling flows.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Discontinuous shear thickening (DST) in dense suspensions leads to flow instabilities that limit processing in many systems. While high-power ultrasound has been reported to reduce the apparent viscosity of such materials, the origin of this effect remains unclear. Here, we investigate dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions, where shear thickening arises from fragile, load-bearing force networks embedded in heterogeneous density-wave structures. Using a rheo-ultrasound setup, we show that ultrasound does not directly reduce viscosity but instead shifts the shear-thickening transition toward higher shear rates. This is evidenced by the collapse of stress probability distributions onto master curves, revealing a continuous evolution toward more fluid-like states without a sharp threshold. We interpret these results through a separation of time scales, in which the suspension behaves as an effectively immobile porous medium subjected to high-frequency interstitial flows. Fluidization then arises from a combination of boundary slip, bulk destabilization of force networks by drag-force fluctuations, and localized acoustic streaming. Beyond these mechanisms, we propose that ultrasound modifies the stability of force networks by introducing fluctuating hydrodynamic forces at the pore scale. As a result, larger stresses or shear rates are required to sustain jammed states, leading to a continuous renormalization of the DST transition. These findings provide a consistent physical picture of acoustic fluidization in adhesive suspensions and establish ultrasound as a powerful tool to control discontinuous shear thickening in confined flows.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28066"></a>
### [Anharmonic phonon coupling enabled by local inversion symmetry breaking at domain walls in ferroelastic](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28066v1)

**Authors:** Seyyed Jabbar Mousavi, Vivek Unikandanunni, Niccolo Sellati, Paolo Barone, Martina Basini, Steven L. Johnson, Andrey Shalit, Peter Hamm, Mattia Udina, Thomas Feurer  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28066v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The researchers used 2D Raman-terahertz spectroscopy to demonstrate that ferroelastic domain walls in LaAlO3 break inversion symmetry, enabling new types of anharmonic phonon coupling. This discovery shows how local structural defects can fundamentally alter phonon selection rules and lattice dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether local inversion symmetry breaking at ferroelastic domain walls enables anharmonic phonon coupling that is otherwise absent in the centrosymmetric bulk.

**Main result.** Direct evidence of both mechanical and electrical anharmonicity between A1g and Eg phonons was found in LaAlO3, driven by local symmetry breaking at domain walls.

**Method.** Two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy was used to detect cross-peaks in the spectra.

**Model / system.** The physical system is ferroelastic LaAlO3, specifically focusing on the lattice dynamics at twin domain walls where inversion symmetry is locally broken.

**Key observables.** Cross-peaks in two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectra, Raman-active A1g phonon, and infrared-active Eg phonon.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local inversion symmetry breaking at domain walls; mechanical and electrical anharmonicity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The bulk crystal is assumed to be centrosymmetric, with symmetry breaking localized at the domain walls.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of symmetry breaking at domain walls, presents experimental evidence using 2D Raman-THz spectroscopy, attributes observed cross-peaks to specific anharmonicities, and concludes with the implications for understanding lattice dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In ferroelastic materials, spontaneous symmetry breaking leads to the formation of twin domains. Although the bulk crystal typically remains centrosymmetric, inversion symmetry can be locally broken at the domain walls, potentially changing phonon selection rules and enabling local anharmonic phonon coupling. Here we report direct evidence of such anharmonic coupling in ferroelastic LaAlO$_3$ using two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy. We attribute the cross-peaks observed in the two-dimensional spectra to both mechanical and electrical anharmonicity between the $A_{1g}$ Raman-active phonon and the $E_g$ phonon, which acquires finite infrared activity through local inversion symmetry breaking at ferroelastic domain walls. These findings provide new insight into the complex lattice dynamics of ferroelastic materials and highlight the potential of two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy to uncover subtle symmetry breaking through the detection of intrinsically weak anharmonic signals.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27266"></a>
### [AutoREC: A software platform for developing reinforcement learning agents for equivalent circuit model generation from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy data](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27266v1)

**Authors:** Ali Jaberi, Yonatan Kurniawan, Robert Black, Shayan Mousavi M., Kabir Verma, Zoya Sadighi, Santiago Miret, Jason Hattrick-Simpers  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27266v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper introduces AutoREC, an open-source Python package that uses reinforcement learning to automatically create equivalent circuit models from electrochemical data. By treating circuit generation as a decision-making task, it eliminates the need for manual expert intervention. This automation is a key step toward enabling self-driving laboratories for electrochemical research.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Automating the generation of equivalent circuit models (ECMs) from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data to replace manual, trial-and-error identification processes.

**Main result.** The development of AutoREC, an RL-based platform that achieves over 99.6% success on synthetic data and generalizes well to experimental data from batteries, corrosion, and electrochemical reduction systems.

**Method.** Formulating ECM construction as a sequential decision-making problem using a Markov Decision Process (MDP) and implementing a Double Deep Q-Network with prioritized experience replay.

**Model / system.** The system involves electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data from various electrochemical interfaces, including batteries, corrosion, oxygen evolution reaction, and CO2 reduction systems, modeled via equivalent circuit models.

**Key observables.** Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data and the accuracy of the generated equivalent circuit models.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paper discusses limitations in agent design and the need for future strategies to mitigate them, but does not explicitly list fundamental physical assumptions.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the AutoREC package, description of the MDP framework and RL architecture (Double DQN), demonstration of performance on synthetic and experimental datasets, and discussion of limitations and future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper introduces AutoREC, an open-source Python package for developing reinforcement learning (RL) agents to automatically generate equivalent circuit models (ECMs) from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data. While ECMs are a standard framework for interpreting EIS data, traditional identification is typically based on manual trial-and-error, which requires domain experts and limits scalability, particularly in autonomous experimental pipelines such as self-driving laboratories. AutoREC addresses this challenge by formulating ECM construction as a sequential decision-making problem within a Markov Decision Process framework. It implements a Double Deep Q-Network with prioritized experience replay, along with a dedicated dead-loop mitigation strategy, to efficiently explore a complex action space for circuit generation. To demonstrate the capabilities of the platform, we trained an RL agent using AutoREC and evaluated its strengths and limitations across diverse datasets, while also discussing possible strategies to mitigate these limitations in future agent designs. The trained agent achieved a success rate exceeding $99.6\%$ on synthetic datasets and demonstrated strong generalization to unseen experimental EIS data from batteries, corrosion, oxygen evolution reaction, and CO$_2$ reduction systems. These results position AutoREC as a promising platform for adaptive and data-driven ECM generation, with potential for integration into automated electrochemical workflows.

</details>

### [Bound states of solitons in fiber lasers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28090v1)

**Authors:** Yudong Cui, Tianchang Lu, Yusheng Zhang, Dong Mao, Boris A. Malomed  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28090v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This review provides a comprehensive overview of how dissipative solitons form bound states in fiber lasers. It covers the mathematical foundation using Ginzburg-Landau equations and summarizes recent experimental progress in multi-soliton and vector soliton dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reviews the formation, stabilization, and various configurations of bound states of dissipative solitons (soliton molecules) in fiber lasers.

**Main result.** A systematic review of theoretical frameworks and experimental evidence for multi-soliton bound states across temporal, frequency, scalar, and vector domains.

**Method.** The review synthesizes theoretical analysis using the complex Ginzburg-Landau equations with recent experimental findings in fiber laser systems.

**Model / system.** The physical systems studied include dissipative solitons in fiber lasers, specifically focusing on single-component (scalar) and two-component (vector) modes, as well as spatiotemporal solitons in multimode fibers.

**Key observables.** Temporal and frequency domain characteristics of soliton bound states, and multi-soliton mode configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the theoretical basis of soliton molecules via complex Ginzburg-Landau equations, followed by a detailed presentation of experimental results including temporal/frequency domains, scalar/vector components, and multimode fiber dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This article presents a systematic review of theoretical and experimental findings for bound states of two and several dissipative solitons in fiber lasers. The theoretical basis underlying the formation and stabilization of soliton molecules in the fibers, which is provided by the complex Ginzburg-Landau equations and bound states of such equations, is presented in necessary detail, which is followed by a detailed presentation of experimental findings, including very recent ones. In particular, included are the results for the multi-soliton bound states in the fibers, as well as for the bound states in the temporal and frequency domains, single-component (scalar) and two-component (vector), two- and multi-soliton modes, as well as for bound states of spatiotemporal dissipative solitons in the lasers based on multimode fibers.

</details>

### [Complex Effects of Salt on Small-Angle X-ray Scattering of BSA Originate From the Interplay of Ions and Hydration Water](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27913v1)

**Authors:** Anshika Dhiman, Sanbo Qin, Huan-Xiang Zhou  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27913v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper investigates how salt affects the structural measurements of the protein BSA via SAXS. By combining advanced computational modeling with molecular dynamics, the authors show that these effects are driven by the complex interaction between ions and the surrounding hydration water. This provides a clearer understanding of how salt environments influence biomolecular structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of accurately extracting information regarding the effects of salt concentrations on protein structures from Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) data.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the complex effects of salt on the SAXS of BSA arise from the interplay between ions and hydration water, providing a unified picture of protein-ion-water interactions.

**Method.** The researchers combined the newly developed FMAPIq approach with explicit-solvent all-atom molecular dynamics (MD) simulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of bovine serum albumin (BSA) in protein solutions at various salt concentrations, including explicit solvent and ions.

**Key observables.** Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) profiles and protein-ion-water interaction dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Salt concentrations and the role of hydration water.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of salt effects on SAXS, describes the combination of the FMAPIq approach with MD simulations, and presents findings on the interplay of ions and hydration water.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Salts are an integral part of the environment for living systems and, therefore, understanding their effects on proteins and other biomolecules is of fundamental interest. Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) of protein solutions can provide valuable information on salt effects, but extracting this information has been a significant challenge. For example, SAXS data of bovine serum albumin (BSA) at various salt concentrations were fit to three different spherical models. Here we combined the newly developed FMAPIq approach with explicit-solvent all-atom molecular dynamics simulations to show that the complex effects of salt on the SAXS of BSA originate from the interplay of ions and hydration water, leading to a general picture of protein-ion-water interactions.

</details>

### [Conditional Generative Models Enable Targeted Exploration of MAX Phase Design Space](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27709v1)

<img src="2604.27709_figures/2604.27709_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Element frequency heatmap for the set of novel stable structures showing element usage weighted by stoichiometry (upper left corner) and the mean energy above hull (lower right corner).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a conditional generative large language model designed to accelerate the discovery of MAX phases and their MXene derivatives. By conditioning the model on physical and statistical properties, the researchers can target specific regions of the material design space to find novel, thermodynamically stable structures. This approach significantly reduces the need for expensive first-principles calculations during the initial screening process.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently exploring the vast compositional and structural design space of MAX phases to identify stable, synthesizable candidates and precursors for MXenes without the computational bottleneck of traditional DFT screening.

**Main result.** The CrystaLLM-pi model can target specific regions of phase space using conditioning vectors, doubling the generation rate of novel stable structures, with 50% of novel candidates passing stringent DFT stability validation.

**Method.** A multi-stage pipeline involving fine-tuning a transformer-based LLM on MAX phase CIFs, using property-based conditioning vectors, and validating results via MACE/ALIGNN machine-learned potentials and DFT.

**Model / system.** The system consists of MAX phases (M_{n+1}AX_n) and MAB phases. The model is CrystaLLM-pi, an autoregressive generative large language model fine-tuned on 6,179 double transition-metal MAX phases.

**Key observables.** MXene derivative count, A-site well curvature (surrogate for vacancy formation energy), energy above hull (E_hull), and mechanical properties (bulk and shear moduli).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stability threshold of E_hull < 0.050 eV/atom; conditioning vectors for MXene count (0.75-0.95) and A-site curvature (0.0-0.4).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes that structural features encoded in CIFs can be effectively learned and that A-site well curvature serves as a valid proxy for exfoliation potential.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: A-site sublattice perturbation schematic; Fig 2: Distribution of A-site well curvature; Fig 3: Correlation between condition vectors and calculated curvature; Fig 4: Comparison of generation rates; Fig 5: Element frequency and stability heatmap; Fig 6: Generative workflow.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MAX phase design problem, details the CrystaLLM-pi architecture and conditioning mechanism, describes the multi-stage validation workflow, presents results for targeted generation and stability, and concludes with a low-shot analysis of MAB phases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

MAX phases (M$_{n+1}$AX$_n$), precursors to MXenes, span a vast compositional space, motivating efficient computational screening for synthesisable candidates. We employ CrystaLLM$-π$, a large language model fine-tuned on 6,179 double transition-metal MAX phases, and demonstrate its ability to generate out-of-sample structures consistent with known experimental trends. Using a conditioning vector with two dimensions (a statistically derived MXene derivative count and a surrogate for A-site binding energy), the model was able to target MXene-favourable regions of phase space for generation. Specific condition vectors double novel stable structure generation rates versus unconditioned baselines. Of ten compositionally novel candidates, five exhibit DFT-validated stability ($E_{hull} < 0.050$ eV/atom). This work showcases the potential for autoregressive generative models to explore targeted materials' spaces, offering a scalable framework for accelerated discovery in compositionally complex systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27240"></a>
### [Confinement-Connectivity Coupling Enables High-Efficiency Piezoionic Transduction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27240v1)

**Authors:** Tofayel Ahammad Ovee, Daniel Kroeger, Jean-François Louf  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27240v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The paper presents a new way to design hydrogels for bioelectronics by controlling how ions move through small pores. By using a specific mesoporous structure, they achieved high electrical outputs that can even trigger nerve responses without external power. This approach shifts the focus from bulk conductivity to the interplay between pore connectivity and confinement.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Piezoionic hydrogels suffer from low output efficiency due to rapid, symmetric ion redistribution that dissipates charge gradients during mechanical deformation.

**Main result.** A confinement-connectivity design strategy using a mesoporous PVA-glycerol-CB[5] network enables high-efficiency piezoionic transduction, producing up to 180 mV and 9 mA and driving electromyographic responses in nerves.

**Method.** The researchers implemented a layered Negative-Neutral-Positive architecture in a supramolecular mesoporous network to simultaneously increase pore fraction and reduce pore size.

**Model / system.** A supramolecular poly(vinyl alcohol)-glycerol-cucurbit[5]uril (PVA-glycerol-CB[5]) mesoporous hydrogel network with a layered architecture designed to regulate ionic transport.

**Key observables.** Peak voltage (~180 mV), peak current (~9 mA), and electromyographic responses in the mouse sciatic nerve.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pore fraction, characteristic pore size, and the coupling of pore connectivity and confinement.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a design strategy for hydrogels, describes the implementation of the mesoporous architecture, demonstrates the resulting electrical output under compression, and validates the device via biological interface testing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Piezoionic hydrogels offer a route to mechanically driven bioelectronic interfaces, but their output is limited by rapid, symmetric ion redistribution that dissipates charge gradients. In biological electrocytes, efficient signal generation arises from the coupling of ion selectivity with spatial confinement that regulates transport. Here, we introduce a confinement-connectivity design strategy for piezoionic hydrogels, implemented through a supramolecular poly(vinyl alcohol)-glycerol-cucurbit[5]uril (PVA-glycerol-CB[5]) mesoporous network with a layered Negative-Neutral-Positive architecture that simultaneously increases pore fraction while reducing characteristic pore size. This architecture constrains ionic redistribution while maintaining a large mobile-ion reservoir, enabling deformation-driven charge separation. Compression generates peak outputs of ~180 mV and ~9 mA and elicits synchronized electromyographic responses in the mouse sciatic nerve without external power. These results establish confinement-connectivity coupling, rather than bulk conductivity, as a materials design framework in which coupling pore connectivity and confinement governs piezoionic transduction.

</details>

**Authors:** Aqsa Fayyaz, Ulises Martin Diaz, Jianyu Dai, Homero Castaneda, Chenglin Wu

**Summary.** This study demonstrates that the impact of surface chemistry on corrosion protection depends heavily on whether the MXene is a simple binary system or a complex medium-entropy alloy. While oxygenation helps stabilize binary MXene coatings, it hinders the self-healing properties of medium-entropy MXenes by passivating the active metal sites needed for protective oxide formation.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how surface functionalization (oxygenation) and compositional complexity (binary vs. medium-entropy) affect the corrosion protection performance of MXene-reinforced epoxy coatings.

**Main result.** Oxygenation improves corrosion resistance in binary Ti3C2Tx MXenes by enhancing interfacial bonding, but impairs it in medium-entropy (TiVCrMo)C3 MXenes by disrupting the formation of protective, redox-active oxide layers.

**Method.** Experimental techniques include EIS, SEM, AFM, XPS, and XRD, complemented by DFT calculations and Electron Localization Function (ELF) analysis to study adsorption energies and electronic structure.

**Model / system.** A nacre-inspired multilayer epoxy coating architecture reinforced with either binary Ti3C2Tx or medium-entropy (TiVCrMo)C3 MXene flakes, applied to C1010 carbon steel substrates.

**Key observables.** Impedance modulus (|Z|), coating resistance (R_coat), charge transfer resistance (R_ct), interlayer spacing, surface topography, and adsorption energies of O2 and H2O.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Surface terminations (-F, -O, -OH), compositional entropy, and the presence of multi-metal electronic structures.

**Figures summary.** The figures likely show SEM/EDS for morphology and elemental mapping, XRD for interlayer spacing, XPS for chemical states, EIS Bode/Nyquist plots for resistance, and DFT-based energy landscapes or electron density plots.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of steel corrosion, describes the fabrication of multilayer MXene-epoxy coatings, presents characterization of structural and chemical properties, details electrochemical performance comparisons, uses DFT to explain the underlying electronic mechanisms, and concludes with design principles.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Developing scalable and environmentally benign anticorrosion coatings is critical for protecting steel infrastructure in chloride-rich environments. Here, a nacre-inspired multilayer epoxy coating reinforced with four MXene systems is investigated. This architecture forms a dense lamellar network that increases diffusion tortuosity and introduces electroactive surfaces for ion interactions. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) confirms that the multilayer design increases coating resistance from ~103 to ~108 Ohm/cm2. A clear performance hierarchy was observed: P-(TiVCrMo)C3 > O-Ti3C2Tx > O-(TiVCrMo)C3 > P-Ti3C2Tx. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations reveal that P-Ti3C2 strongly adsorbs O2, indicating higher surface reactivity, while oxygen termination stabilizes the surface by partially passivating Ti sites. In contrast, P-(TiVCrMo)C3 exhibits strong adsorption of oxygen-containing species due to its multi-metal electronic structure, promoting the formation of protective oxides. These results highlight the delicate balance of surface chemistry, electronic structure, and compositional entropy in designing next-generation MXene-based anticorrosion coatings for marine and industrial environments.

</details>

**Authors:** V. I. Kuznetsov, O. V. Trofimov  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27822v1>

</details>

<img src="2604.27822_figures/2604.27822_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: (Color online) Squares and triangles are the switching and retrap- ping currents as functions of the temperature, respectively, recorded in a nar- row structure according to the measurement circuit: I-7-8, V-6-5. **Summary.** The researchers found that narrowing superconducting aluminum wires unexpectedly decreases their critical temperature and current density. They propose that this is caused by increased depairing from defects on the wire boundaries and observed that the structures behave as SNS junctions near the transition temperature.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating why narrower quasi-one-dimensional aluminum structures exhibit lower critical temperatures and critical current densities compared to wider structures of the same thickness.

**Main result.** The study discovered that narrower structures have lower Tc and jc, likely due to stronger depairing effects from dirty longitudinal boundaries, and identified a transition to a Josephson SNS junction regime at higher temperatures.

**Method.** Electrical measurements of voltage-current (V-I) characteristics and resistance-temperature (R-T) transitions were performed in zero magnetic field using various measurement circuits on fabricated silicon chips.

**Model / system.** Quasi-one-dimensional superconducting aluminum thin-film structures (thickness 19 nm) with widths of approximately 0.27 um and 0.48 um, operating in the dirty limit.

**Key observables.** Critical temperature (Tc), switching critical current (Isw), retrapping current (Iret), and critical current density (jc).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Aluminum film thickness (19 nm), wire widths (0.27 um and 0.48 um), and the dirty limit regime (electron mean free path < coherence length).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reduction in Tc is attributed to the influence of depairing centers (magnetic atoms or vacancies) located on the dirty longitudinal boundaries of the structures.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows resistance vs. temperature for wide and narrow structures; Figure 2 displays V-I curves and switching current temperature dependence for wide structures; Figures 3 and 4 show switching and retrapping current temperature dependence for narrow structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Tc discrepancy, describes the experimental setup and fabrication, presents measurement results for resistance and critical currents, compares data to Ginzburg-Landau and Kupriyanov-Lukichev theories, and concludes with a discussion on the physical mechanism of depairing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We measured the critical temperatures and critical switching and retrapping currents of wide and narrow thin-film quasi-one-dimensional superconducting aluminum structures of the same thickness in zero magnetic field. For the first time, we found that the narrower the structure, the lower the critical temperature and critical current density in the structure. Probably, the influence of depairing centers that are on dirty longitudinal boundaries of the structure, is the stronger than the narrower the structure. It is found for the first time that, in most cases, the temperature-dependent switching critical current in both structures is approximated by two functions. At temperatures below the temperature corresponding to the bottom of the resistive N-S transition of structures, the switching critical current is described by the Kupriyanov-Lukichev theory. At temperatures close to the top of the N-S transition, the switching current is linear with temperature and coincides with the critical Josephson current. At these temperatures, Josephson SNS junctions are formed in structures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27544"></a>
### [Curvature-induced nonlinear anomalous Hall effect in thin magnetic shells](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27544v1)

**Authors:** Maria Teresa Mercaldo, Mario Cuoco, Carmine Ortix  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27544v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper shows that the physical curvature of magnetic shells can be used to engineer nonlinear transport properties. By manipulating the geometry, one can selectively access the real and imaginary components of the quantum geometric tensor. This provides a new platform for studying the interplay between geometry and topology in condensed matter systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how geometric curvature and strain gradients in thin magnetic shells can be used to engineer and probe the quantum geometric tensor (quantum metric and Berry curvature) in centrosymmetric ferromagnets.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that curvature-induced orbital Rashba coupling and broken symmetries in magnetic shells generate an intrinsic nonlinear anomalous Hall effect (NAHE) that allows for the selective probing of both the quantum metric and the Berry curvature dipole.

**Method.** The study uses a theoretical framework to analyze how geometric bending and strain gradients across shell thickness break inversion and rotational symmetries, leading to specific spin textures and transport responses.

**Model / system.** The system consists of thin, curved ferromagnetic shells characterized by in-plane magnetization, spin-orbit coupling, and curvature-induced strain gradients.

**Key observables.** Nonlinear anomalous Hall effect (NAHE), quantum metric, Berry curvature dipole, and spin textures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** In-plane magnetization direction, applied electric field direction, and geometric deformations (curvature and strain gradients).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the presence of spin-orbit coupling and that geometric bending induces sufficient strain gradients to break inversion symmetry.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of probing the quantum geometric tensor, describes the mechanism of curvature-induced symmetry breaking and orbital Rashba coupling, analyzes the resulting NAHE components, and concludes with the implications for engineering anisotropic nonlinear transport.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optoelectronic and nonlinear transport experiments probe the quantum geometric tensor of Bloch states, whose real and imaginary components -- the quantum metric and the Berry curvature -- are typically constrained by symmetry. Here, we show that geometric bending provides a route to engineer such responses in centrosymmetric ferromagnets. Curvature-induced strain gradients across the shell thickness break inversion symmetry and activate an orbital Rashba coupling. In the presence of in-plane magnetization and spin-orbit coupling, this generates spin textures with a nontrivial quantum geometry, leading to an intrinsic nonlinear anomalous Hall effect (NAHE) governed by the quantum metric and maximized when the magnetization aligns with the applied electric field. When geometric deformations further break twofold rotational symmetry around the out-of-plane axis, an additional NAHE emerges, maximal for magnetization perpendicular to the driving electric field and governed by the Berry curvature dipole, thus giving access to the imaginary component of the quantum geometric tensor. These results establish curved ferromagnetic shells as a platform for engineering anisotropic nonlinear transport and for selectively probing both components of the quantum geometric tensor.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27775"></a>
### [Data-Efficient Indentation Size Effect Correction in Steels Using Machine Learning and Physics-Guided Augmentation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27775v1)

**Authors:** Radmir Karamov, Tagir Karamov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27775v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

**Summary.** This paper presents a machine learning approach to correct hardness measurements in steels that are otherwise distorted by small-scale effects. By using physics-informed features and data augmentation, the authors developed a neural network that can accurately predict bulk material properties from very shallow indentation tests.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Correcting the Indentation Size Effect (ISE) in nanoindentation, where measured hardness is artificially inflated at shallow depths due to strain-gradient plasticity and geometric artifacts like pile-up.

**Main result.** A constrained bottleneck neural network (64-8-64) successfully predicts bulk hardness from shallow-depth data with high accuracy (MAPE = 5.4%), outperforming classical Nix-Gao models and tree-based regressors.

**Method.** A physics-guided machine learning framework using data augmentation (Gaussian noise, drift, and MixUp) and feature engineering with area-invariant mechanical descriptors.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of multiphase steels with varying microstructures (ferritic/pearlitic to martensitic). The ML models evaluated include Ridge Regression, Random Forest, XGBoost, and constrained Neural Networks.

**Key observables.** Hardness (H), reduced elastic modulus (Er), contact depth (hc), maximum load (Pmax), contact stiffness (S), and plastic work fraction (Wp/Wtot).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shallow contact regime (hc < 215 nm), hardness range 2-6.5 GPa, and the use of area-invariant proxies like Pmax/S^2.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The training steels share a constrained chemical composition; the model is not universal and fails on amorphous materials like fused silica.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows depth-dependent hardness and SEM images of pile-up; Figure 2 compares Nix-Gao errors; Figure 3 shows model parity plots; Figure 5 compares RF and NN performance on unseen specimens; Figure 6 shows model failure on fused silica.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the ISE problem, describes the experimental nanoindentation setup and steel specimens, details the physics-guided data augmentation and feature engineering, evaluates various ML architectures, performs interpretability analysis via SHAP and PCA, and concludes with limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Shallow nanoindentation enables mechanical characterization of thin films, individual phases and other volume-constrained materials, but measured hardness is often inflated by the indentation size effect (ISE), contact-area errors and tip-geometry artifacts. Classical ISE corrections such as the Nix-Gao require a deep linear regime and are unreliable when only shallow measurements are used. This study investigates how a small experimental dataset can be used to predict a reference hardness with physics-guided feature engineering and augmentation. Approximately 700 experimental indentations were collected from three steel reference specimens covering a hardness range of 2-6.5 GPa and augmented using physically motivated variations representing instrumental noise, session-level drift, and local multiphase boundary blending. The input space combined Oliver-Pharr values with mechanics descriptors, including indentation work partitioning, ($H\text{/}E_{r}$), and the area-invariant compliance proxy ($P_{\max}\text{/}S^{2}$). Ridge Regression (RR), Random Forest, XGBoost, and Neural Networks (NN) were evaluated using a quarantined fourth steel specimen tested at staggered loads. The hardness mapping was nonlinear: RR failed, whereas nonlinear models achieved ($R^2 > 0.98$) internally. A constrained (64-8-64) NN gave the best results, reaching RMSE = 0.470 GPa, MAPE = 5.4% on the quarantined steel. Unlike Nix-Gao analysis, the NN produced stable estimates in the shallow regime. SHAP and latent-space analysis showed reliance on area-invariant and energy-based descriptors. The results demonstrate the feasibility of a this workflow for ISE correction in steels using small datasets and suggest a pathway toward data-efficient characterization of any volume constrained materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27120"></a>
### [First-Principles Thermodynamic Analysis of Ternary Chalcogenide Phase Change Materials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27120v1)

**Authors:** Felix Adams, Ichiro Takeuchi, Carlos Ríos Ocampo, Yifei Mo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27120v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new thermodynamic screening method to discover new phase-change materials for memory and photonics. By analyzing the energetics of ternary chalcogenide mixtures, the authors identify new candidate materials that follow the crystallization pathways of the well-known GST system. This approach provides a practical, computationally efficient alternative to simulating full transition dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for a computationally efficient way to identify new chalcogenide phase-change materials (PCMs) with properties similar to the GST family without simulating full phase-transition dynamics.

**Main result.** The development of a thermodynamic framework based on Ostwald's rule that uses polymorph screening to identify promising ternary chalcogenide mixtures.

**Method.** First-principles calculations are used to evaluate the energetics of ternary chalcogenide mixtures along binary-binary tie lines and their various polymorphs.

**Model / system.** Ternary chalcogenide mixtures, specifically focusing on the GeTe-Sb2Te3 (GST) system and related compositions.

**Key observables.** Phase stability, miscibility, and the likelihood of polymorph-mediated crystallization pathways.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ground-state and metastable structure energies, composition space along binary-binary tie lines.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that thermodynamic polymorph screening can serve as a proxy for complex phase-transition dynamics, motivated by Ostwald's rule.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of PCM discovery, proposes a thermodynamic framework based on Ostwald's rule, describes the first-principles methodology, presents results evaluating ternary mixtures and polymorph stability, and concludes with implications for future material discovery.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Chalcogenide phase-change materials (PCMs) are important for nonvolatile memory and reconfigurable photonic technologies. The GeTe-Sb2Te3 mixture system, commonly referred to as GST, is the most well-known PCM family, but new PCMs are needed to broaden the accessible property space while retaining fast switching. Here, we propose a thermodynamic framework, motivated by Ostwald's rule, for understanding and identifying PCM materials. Since direct modeling of phase-transition dynamics is computationally expensive, Using first-principles calculations, we systematically evaluate the energetics of ternary chalcogenide mixtures along binary-binary tie lines and their polymorphs. By comparing ground-state and metastable structures, we assess phase stability, miscibility, and the likelihood of GST-like polymorph-mediated crystallization pathways across a broad composition space. The calculations reproduce known behavior in GST and related systems and identify several promising candidate mixtures with similar features. These results provide insight into why some PCM systems are more favorable than others and establish thermodynamic polymorph screening as a practical route for future PCM discovery.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Fragment-Constrained Charge Equilibration for Charge-Aware Machine Learning Potentials at Electrochemical Interfaces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27910v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new machine learning solver layer that allows for different chemical potentials in different parts of a system. This enables reactive machine learning potentials to accurately simulate the electric double layer at electrochemical interfaces, which was previously impossible with standard global charge equilibration methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing charge-aware machine learning interatomic potentials (MLIPs) using global charge equilibration (QEq) cannot model the electrochemical potential gradient at interfaces because they force a single system-wide chemical potential.

**Main result.** The authors developed a 'Soft-FQEq' solver layer that uses differentiable fragment identification to allow distinct chemical potentials for different fragments, successfully recovering the interfacial potential gradient.

**Method.** An augmented-Lagrangian Uzawa iterative solver integrated into the hippynn framework, using a graph Laplacian resolvent for differentiable fragment assignment and Gaussian-screened Ewald electrostatics.

**Model / system.** An electrochemical interface consisting of an IrO2 electrode, aqueous electrolyte (H2O, Na+, ClO4-), and a bulk water region, modeled via a machine learning potential (HIP-NN) with a fragment-constrained charge solver.

**Key observables.** Local electrochemical potential (mu_phys), fragment-resolved chemical potentials (mu_frag), per-atom electronegativity, and DDEC6 atomic charges.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Penalty strength beta = 50, Ewald screening parameter alpha_E = 0.5 A^-1, and soft bond connectivity weights.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes fragment identification can be treated as a differentiable function of geometry via soft connectivity; assumes the solver's coupling is restricted to scalar MLP readouts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a snapshot of the IrO2/aqueous NaClO4 interface with partitioned regions; Figure 3 illustrates the electrochemical potential profile showing the electrode plateau, interfacial gradient, and bulk water plateau.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the failure of global QEq, introduces the Soft-FQEq mathematical framework and differentiable fragment identification, details the implementation within the HIP-NN/hippynn architecture, validates the model against DFT/DDEC6 data, and performs an ablation study to prove the necessity of the fragment-constrained approach.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Predictive simulation of electrochemical interfaces requires atomistic models that capture reactive bond rearrangements, long-range electrostatics, and charge distributions reflecting the electronic distinctness of electrode and electrolyte. Existing charge-aware machine-learned interatomic potentials (MLIPs) built on global charge equilibration (QEq) settle electrode and electrolyte at a common electrochemical potential, leaving no room for the interfacial gradient that the double layer requires and admitting spurious charge transfer between electronically disconnected regions. Per-fragment charge equilibration is the established remedy in classical molecular dynamics, but reliance on predefined molecular topology has confined it to non-reactive systems. We lift this restriction by making fragment identification itself a differentiable function of atomic geometry, yielding soft fragment-constrained charge equilibration (Soft-FQEq) -- a solver layer that restores fragment-resolved charge conservation in reactive MLIPs. The layer consumes four scalar MLP readouts from a shared atomic-feature network -- per-atom electronegativity, source charge, short-range energy, and a soft bond connectivity -- and returns equilibrated charges together with per-fragment chemical potentials. We implement Soft-FQEq as an extension of the hippynn framework on a HIP-NN feature network and train it on DFT energies, forces, and DDEC6 charges for IrO2/H2O/Na+/ClO4- interfaces. The trained model recovers a clear electrode-to-electrolyte gradient in the per-atom electrochemical potential. With the same trained weights but the fragment-constrained solver replaced by global QEq at inference, this gradient collapses to an essentially uniform profile, directly showing that the gradient cannot be sustained within global QEq while the fragment formulation recovers it.

</details>

### [From Narrow-gap Semiconductor to Metallic Altermagnet: Optical Fingerprints of Co-Doped FeSb$_2$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28105v1)

**Authors:** R. Mathew Roy, M. Povolotskiy, J. Kirschke, C. Prange, Y. Xia, V. Sundaramurthy, P. Puphal, M. Pinteric, M. van de Loo, A. Kreyssig, T. Zhang, A. E. Böhmer, M. Dressel, M. Wenzel  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28105v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that doping the semiconductor FeSb2 with cobalt induces a metallic altermagnetic state stable at room temperature. By using optical spectroscopy, the authors identify unique electronic transitions and phonon behaviors that serve as fingerprints for this new magnetic phase. This work establishes Co-doped FeSb2 as a key platform for studying the interplay between altermagnetism and lattice degrees of freedom.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The search for bulk metallic altermagnetism and the characterization of the electronic and lattice properties in Co-doped FeSb2.

**Main result.** Cobalt substitution (~15%) transforms the narrow-gap semiconductor FeSb2 into a metallic altermagnetic state that persists up to room temperature, characterized by specific low-energy interband transitions and enhanced electron-phonon coupling.

**Method.** The study combines infrared optical conductivity measurements with density functional theory (DFT) calculations to analyze electronic transitions and phonon modes.

**Model / system.** The physical system is Co-doped FeSb2, a correlated narrow-gap semiconductor that transitions into a metallic altermagnetic state with d-wave spin symmetry.

**Key observables.** Infrared optical conductivity, low-energy interband transitions (near 0.1 eV), spin-split bands, Fano lineshapes in infrared-active phonons, and mode mixing.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cobalt substitution level (~15%), temperature (up to room temperature), spin-split band energy (~0.2 meV), and spin-orbit coupling induced splitting (~5 meV).

**Paper structure.** The paper presents experimental evidence of a phase transition via doping, followed by spectroscopic analysis of electronic transitions and phonon dynamics, supported by DFT calculations of the spin-split band structure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The realization of bulk metallic altermagnetism has remained elusive despite the growing number of candidate materials. Here, we present evidence that moderate cobalt substitution ($\sim$15%) drives the correlated narrow-gap semiconductor FeSb$_2$ into a metallic altermagnetic state persisting up to room temperature. The infrared optical conductivity reveals low-energy interband transitions near 0.1 eV that emerge upon doping and grow with Co concentration. Density functional theory calculations show that these transitions originate exclusively from altermagnetic spin ordering, with spin split bands ($\sim$0.2 meV) of non-relativistic origin, together with spin-orbit coupling induced band splitting of the order of $\sim$5 meV near the Fermi level. Co substitution further leads to Fano lineshapes and mode mixing in the infrared-active phonons, reflecting enhanced electron-phonon coupling and local inversion symmetry breaking, while leaving the altermagnetic spin symmetry intact. Our results establish carrier-tuned FeSb$_2$ as a platform for exploring metallic $d$-wave altermagnetism and its coupling to lattice degrees of freedom.

</details>

### [Generation of magnetic metal-organic frameworks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27879v1)

**Authors:** Alexander C. Tyner, Avinash Pathapati, Alexander V. Balatsky  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27879v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper addresses the difficulty of simulating large-scale organic materials like MOFs by fine-tuning a machine-learning interatomic potential (CHGNet) with a new dataset of 15,000+ computations. The researchers developed a workflow to discover new, highly magnetic metal-organic frameworks by substituting sites in known structural prototypes. This approach provides a scalable way to explore the vast chemical space of magnetic organic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Simulating metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) is computationally challenging due to large unit cells and the underrepresentation of organic materials in existing machine-learning training sets.

**Main result.** The authors successfully fine-tuned the CHGNet interatomic potential using a new dataset of 15,000+ first-principles computations from the OMDB and implemented a workflow to identify novel magnetic MOFs.

**Method.** Fine-tuning the CHGNet machine-learned interatomic potential using a dataset derived from the Organic Materials Database (OMDB) and applying a site substitution workflow on QMOF structural prototypes.

**Model / system.** Metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) characterized by large unit cells containing both organic and inorganic components, specifically targeting the generation of highly magnetic structures.

**Key observables.** Magnetic properties of the identified MOFs

**Assumptions / limitations.** The effectiveness of fine-tuning CHGNet relies on the representativeness of the 15,000 single-point first-principles computations from the OMDB.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational challenge of MOFs, describes the creation of a new training dataset from OMDB, details the fine-tuning of the CHGNet model, and presents a workflow for identifying new magnetic MOFs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The potential to utilize metal-organic frameworks as a replacement for rare earth materials as well as in technological applications has prompted increased interested in this material class. The simulation of organic materials, including metal-organic frameworks (MOFs), represents a computational challenge due to an increased average number of atoms in the unit cell. Compounding this challenge, modern materials databases are generally limited to inorganic structures due to their utility in modern technologies such as batteries and integrated circuits. Machine-learning tools appear ideally suited to study these systems. However, organic materials are generally underrepresented in the training sets of foundational models. In this work we leverage the the Organic Materials Database (OMDB) to create a training dataset comprised of more than 15,000 single-point first-principles computations for finetuning machine learned interatomic potentials. Specifically, we fine tune CHGNet and implement a site substitution workflow to identify novel, highly magnetic, MOFs from structural prototypes within the QMOF database.

</details>

### [Giant Spin Magnetization from Quantum Geometry in Altermagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28088v1)

**Authors:** Neelanjan Chakraborti, Sudeep Kumar Ghosh, Snehasish Nandy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28088v1>

A finite linear spin mag- netization (MB) is induced under an applied magnetic field (B), whereas the linear response to an electric field (E) is forbidden in a centrosymmetric altermagnet. The magnetic- field-driven response originates from the spin-rotation quan- tum metric.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies a new, universal mechanism for generating giant spin magnetization in centrosymmetric altermagnets driven by the spin-rotation quantum metric. The predicted magnetization is several orders of magnitude larger than in conventional magnets, making these materials ideal platforms for spintronic applications and probes of quantum geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Identifying the quantum-geometric mechanism responsible for linear spin magnetization in centrosymmetric altermagnets, where symmetry constraints suppress equilibrium and electric-field-driven responses.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that magnetic-field-induced spin magnetization in centrosymmetric altermagnets originates entirely from the spin-rotation quantum metric, predicting a giant response magnitude of 10^-2 mu_B/nm^3.

**Method.** The study employs a generalized quantum geometric tensor framework incorporating momentum translations and spin rotations, combined with a symmetry analysis and a perturbative expansion of the density matrix using the quantum Liouville equation.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on centrosymmetric altermagnets, specifically the d-wave compound FeSb2 and the g-wave compound CrSb, using a tight-binding Hamiltonian with spin-orbit coupling and a Neel exchange field.

**Key observables.** Linear spin magnetization induced by a magnetic field, spin-rotation quantum metric (SRQM), and the spin-rotation Berry curvature.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field strength of ~10 mT, spin-split band structures, and the weak-disorder limit (relaxation-time approximation).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a weak-disorder limit (relaxation-time approximation) and focuses on the intrinsic, $	au$-independent regime of the response.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the mechanism of magnetization in an altermagnet, showing the vanishing magnetization at equilibrium and the finite response induced by a magnetic field due to the spin-rotation quantum metric.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spin magnetization in altermagnets, develops a generalized quantum geometric tensor framework, performs a symmetry-based decomposition of magnetization components, applies this to specific altermagnetic models (FeSb2 and CrSb), and discusses experimental implications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnets host spin-split band structures while exhibiting vanishing equilibrium spin magnetization, making field-induced responses a direct probe of their quantum geometry. A central question, in this regard, is which quantum-geometric mechanism can generate a linear spin magnetization in centrosymmetric systems. Here we develop a unified framework based on a generalized quantum geometric tensor that incorporates both momentum translations and spin rotations of Bloch states, and decompose spin magnetization into equilibrium, electric-field-driven, and magnetic-field-driven contributions. We show that inversion symmetry forbids the linear electric-field response in centrosymmetric systems, while $C_n T$ symmetry further suppresses the equilibrium contribution in altermagnets. Consequently, centrosymmetric altermagnets provide a particularly clean realization in which the magnetic-field-induced spin magnetization emerges as the only symmetry-allowed linear quantum-geometric response. We demonstrate that this contribution originates entirely from the spin-rotation quantum metric, establishing it as the sole linear quantum-geometric mechanism in such systems. Using representative centrosymmetric altermagnets, including the $d$-wave compound $\mathrm{FeSb}_2$ and the $g$-wave compound $\mathrm{CrSb}$, we show that the spin-rotation quantum metric directly controls this response. Crucially, we predict a giant linear spin magnetization of order $10^{-2}μ_B\,\mathrm{nm}^{-3}$ at magnetic fields of $\sim 10\,\mathrm{mT}$, exceeding typical experimental values for conventional magnets by several orders of magnitude. Our results identify a universal quantum geometric mechanism of spin magnetization operative in centrosymmetric systems in general, and establish centrosymmetric altermagnets as an ideal platform for its experimental detection with potential applications in spintronics.

</details>

### [Guided elastic waves for soft elastomer characterization: an alternative to conventional rheometry](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27722v1)

**Authors:** Samuel Croquette, Pierre Chantelot, Daniel A. Kiefer, Claire Prada, Fabrice Lemoult  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27722v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new method to characterize the mechanical properties of soft elastomers using guided elastic waves. By analyzing wave dispersion under tension, the researchers can extract rheological and hyperelastic parameters across a wider frequency range than traditional rheometry. This approach offers a simpler, broadband alternative for studying the mechanics of soft materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an alternative to conventional rheometry for characterizing the viscoelastic and acoustoelastic properties of soft elastomers.

**Main result.** A wave-based characterization method using guided elastic waves was successfully implemented, providing mechanical parameters consistent with plate-plate rheometry but over a broader frequency range.

**Method.** The method uses stroboscopic measurements of monochromatic wave fields in a thin elastomer strip acting as a waveguide, combined with a numerical implementation to match experimental dispersion curves to a theoretical framework.

**Model / system.** The system consists of thin strips of commercial silicone elastomers acting as waveguides for in-plane guided elastic modes under controlled static elongation.

**Key observables.** Complex-valued dispersion relations for guided modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Frequency-dependent viscoelasticity and elongation-dependent acoustoelasticity (hyperelastic parameters).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the elastomer strip acts as a waveguide supporting multiple in-plane modes and relies on the coupling of viscoelasticity and acoustoelasticity.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the wave-based method, description of the experimental setup using elastomer strips, presentation of the theoretical framework coupling viscoelasticity and acoustoelasticity, numerical implementation for data matching, and validation using commercial silicone elastomers.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Elastic wave propagation is intrinsically sensitive to the mechanical properties of the medium through which it travels. In soft elastomers, this makes guided elastic waves natural probes of viscoelastic and acoustoelastic behavior over a broad frequency range. In this work, we introduce a wave-based mechanical characterization method in which a thin elastomer strip acts as a waveguide supporting multiple in-plane guided modes. By combining stroboscopic measurements of monochromatic wave fields with a theoretical framework that couples frequency-dependent viscoelasticity and elongation-dependent acoustoelasticity, we extract complex-valued dispersion relations for guided modes under controlled static elongation. A dedicated numerical implementation allows these experimental dispersion curves to be quantitatively matched to theory, enabling identification of the material's rheological and hyperelastic parameters. Applied to several commercial silicone elastomers, the method yields mechanical parameters that are consistent with conventional plate-plate rheometry, while extending the accessible frequency range beyond that of conventional techniques. By exploiting the richness of guided-wave dispersion and the sensitivity of waves to both frequency and pre-stress, this approach provides a unified, broadband, and experimentally simple route to the mechanical characterization of soft elastomers.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28184"></a>
### [Intrinsic anomalous thermal hall effect as a signature of quantum metric in d-wave altermagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28184v1)

**Authors:** Rishi G. Gopalakrishnan, Srimayi Korrapati, Sumanta Tewari  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28184v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2604.28184_figures/2604.28184_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Variation of the factors F xx + Ωz + (top panel), F xy + Ωz + (bottom panel) in Eq. (16) over the kx −ky plane. On the other hand, F xy T,+Ωz + is odd in both kx and ky and accord- ingly integrates to zero, resulting in vanishing ηabxy and ηabyx. **Summary.** This paper identifies that in d-wave altermagnets, the first non-vanishing intrinsic thermal Hall effect occurs at the third order of the temperature gradient. It introduces a new geometric quantity, the nonlinear thermal Berry connection polarizability, which links quantum metric effects to measurable nonlinear thermal transport.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the intrinsic nonlinear anomalous thermal Hall effect (NATHE) in d-wave altermagnets, specifically seeking the leading-order nonvanishing contribution to the transverse heat current in the absence of a magnetic field.

**Main result.** The authors find that due to crystalline symmetries, the linear and second-order thermal Hall effects vanish, leaving a third-order response proportional to (grad T)^3 driven by a new quantity called the nonlinear thermal Berry connection polarizability.

**Method.** The researchers use Schrieffer-Wolff perturbation theory to calculate corrections to energy and Berry curvature and introduce a new tensor, the Nonlinear Thermal Berry Connection Polarizability (NTBClamP), to describe the third-order response.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-dimensional d-wave altermagnet described by a two-band Hamiltonian featuring d-wave order parameter, Rashba spin-orbit coupling, and specific C4zT and C2z symmetries.

**Key observables.** Third-order intrinsic anomalous thermal Hall conductivity, transverse thermal current, and the Nonlinear Thermal Berry Connection Polarizability (NTBClamP) tensor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature gradient (grad T), temperature (T), chemical potential (mu), d-wave order parameter (delta_d), and crystal axes orientation (theta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the intrinsic (scattering-time-independent) contribution and assumes a two-band model approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the singular kernel of the NTBClamP tensor; Figures 2-4 demonstrate the cancellation of band contributions to ensure finite current and show the unimodal temperature dependence and non-monotonic chemical potential dependence of the conductivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing symmetry constraints that forbid lower-order effects, introduces a new theoretical framework for the third-order response via a new polarizability tensor, provides an analytical evaluation of the conductivity, and concludes with the identification of experimental signatures like angular and temperature dependence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the intrinsic anomalous thermal Hall effect in d-wave altermagnets, where a transverse heat current is generated by a longitudinal temperature gradient in the absence of a magnetic field, with the leading response proportional to $(\nabla T)^3$. In these systems, the intrinsic Berry curvature-driven linear and thermal quantum-metric-driven second-order anomalous thermal Hall currents vanish as a consequence of crystalline symmetry. We show that the first nonvanishing contribution arises at third order in the temperature gradient and is governed by a nonlinear thermal Berry-connection polarizability, a quantity introduced in this work. Our analysis reveals a distinctive angular dependence of the anomalous thermal Hall conductance as the applied thermal gradient is rotated with respect to the crystal axes. We also find characteristic temperature and chemical-potential dependences that can be tested experimentally. These results identify unique quantum geometry-induced thermal responses and establish altermagnets as a promising platform for exploring intrinsic (i.e., scattering-time-independent) geometric transport phenomena.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a way to use atomic force microscopy to locally probe superconductivity in patterned oxide interfaces. It reveals that superconductivity in LAO/STO is localized at the edges of patterned structures, with the interior being superconducting only due to the proximity effect. This provides a powerful tool for studying quantum confinement in correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the spatial distribution and nature of superconductivity (2D vs 1D) in patterned LaAlO3/SrTiO3 (LAO/STO) oxide interfaces.

**Main result.** The study identifies that superconductivity is preferentially localized in narrow (~200 nm) edge channels, with the interior of overdoped regions exhibiting superconductivity via a proximity effect.

**Method.** Ultra-low temperature (10 mK) scanning probe microscopy, including non-contact AFM, Kelvin probe force microscopy (KPFM), and dissipation spectroscopy.

**Model / system.** LaAlO3/SrTiO3 (LAO/STO) heterostructures featuring a tunable two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG), specifically patterned into nanowires, waveguides, and 2D sheets using ULV-EBL and c-AFM lithography.

**Key observables.** Contact Potential Difference (CPD), energy dissipation (damping coefficient components alpha and beta), frequency shift, and critical current/magnetic field.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature of 10 mK, magnetic fields up to 300 mT, carrier density (n), and channel widths ranging from 200 nm to 1 um.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a capacitor model to relate CPD variations to local carrier density changes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows device fabrication and transport properties; Figure 2 displays AFM topography and CPD maps; Figure 3 shows spatial carrier density profiles; Figure 4 presents dissipation measurements and magnetic field dependence; Figure 5 provides 2D maps of dissipation and CPD correlated with the superconducting phase diagram.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the fabrication of patterned LAO/STO devices, demonstrates the local mapping of electrostatic potential and carrier density via KPFM, presents dissipation spectroscopy as a local diagnostic for superconductivity, and concludes by analyzing the spatial hierarchy of the superconducting state and its relationship to the doping phase diagram.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductivity in strontium titanate has remained enigmatic for more than 50 years. The LaAlO$_3$/SrTiO$_3$ (LAO/STO) heterointerface enables systematic dimensional confinement, from a two-dimensional electron gas to quasi-one-dimensional nanostructures, providing access to this quantum state. Transport measurements in patterned devices reveal puzzling phenomena, including width-independent critical currents and anomalous pairing suggestive of one-dimensional behavior, but direct local probes of the patterned interface and its superconducting response have been lacking. Here we use ultralow-temperature non-contact atomic force microscopy, dissipation spectroscopy, and Kelvin probe force microscopy to locally probe signatures of superconductivity in patterned LAO/STO devices. Spatially resolved energy-dissipation measurements reveal superconducting signatures, with features confined in some devices to edge channels approximately 200 nm wide. Dissipation spectra exhibit a characteristic nonlinear bias dependence that provides a local diagnostic of superconductivity, consistent with the intermediate carrier-density regime near the superconducting dome, and persisting up to the critical field. These results establish atomic force microscopy as a local probe of superconductivity in patterned LAO/STO structures and provide a route to addressing longstanding questions about quantum confinement and transport anomalies in correlated oxide nanostructures.

</details>
